Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
6 min
Sale of Carrion and Blood: Void Due to Absence of Property Value
Explanation of prohibition and invalidity due to absence of financial value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of carrion and blood, distinguish between batil and fasid in this context, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding non-Muslims.

Core Idea
Carrion and blood have no property value; their sale is void, not just defective.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author explains why selling carrion and blood is invalid: they lack maliyyah (financial value), which is an essential condition for a valid sale. The ruling is batil (void), not merely fasid (defective). The text also explores the definition of carrion and addresses a significant intra-Hanafi dispute: can non-Muslims sell carrion among themselves? Let's unpack this ruling step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
Start by noting that the sale of carrion and blood is 'not permissible' (la yajuz). The author explains this is because they are not considered property (mal) by anyone, and thus the contract is batil (void). Recognize 'maliyyah' as a required condition (shart) for the subject matter of a sale.
The matn uses 'la yajuz' which, in this context, covers both batil and fasid, but the commentary clarifies these items specifically cause batil.
2
Distinguish between batil and fasid
The author notes that the term 'la yajuz' is broad. For carrion and blood, the contract is batil from the start because the subject matter lacks the fundamental attribute of being property. Contrast this with fasid contracts, which have all pillars but miss a condition that can potentially be corrected.
This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh: batil contracts are void ab initio and cannot be rectified.
3
Define 'carrion' (maitah) precisely
Refer to the dictionary definition from Al-Qamus: carrion is an animal that has not been properly slaughtered (i.e., died without ritual slaughter). This includes natural death, but the passage also discusses animals killed by strangulation or beating. For Muslims, all such animals are carrion. For non-Muslims, there is a nuance: if they slaughter by their own methods (e.g., strangling), they may consider it property.
Fish and locusts are excluded from maitah by Hanafi consensus.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on non-Muslims
The passage presents two positions within the Hanafi school: Abu Yusuf permits non-Muslims to sell carrion among themselves because they treat it as valuable property, analogous to their allowance for wine. Muhammad prohibits it, arguing that the rulings on non-Muslims mirror ours except for wine. Understand the evidence for each view.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) appears if a non-Muslim buyer purchases carrion and later becomes Muslim: under Abu Yusuf, the ownership is recognized; under Muhammad, it is not.
5
Apply to similar items (blood, liver, dung, etc.)
Flowing blood (al-dam al-masfuh) is also non-property, so its sale is batil. However, organs like liver and spleen (which contain blood) are permissible because they are considered valuable. Similarly, human waste is non-property, but animal dung can be sold as fuel. This shows that the presence of maliyyah is the decisive factor.
The passage also lists other invalid sales: slaughtered animals of Magians, apostates, polytheists, intentional omission of tasmiyah, and game hunted by a muhrim.

His saying: (The sale of carrion and blood is not permissible) due to the absence of financial value which is a pillar of sale, for they are not considered property by anyone, and it is of the category of the void. And the author, may God have mercy on him, since he used 'fasid' in the chapter in a general sense, he expressed it by 'lack of permissibility' which includes both void and defective. In Al-Qamus: carrion is that which has not been reached by ritual slaughter, and by the broken form it denotes the type. Endquote. If by lack of permissibility is meant its lack in the right of Muslims, then 'carrion' remains in its absolute sense. If the general for Muslim and non-Muslim is meant, then it refers to that which dies a natural death. As for the strangled and the beaten, they are not included because of what is in Al-Tajnis: the people of disbelief, if they sell carrion among themselves, it is not permissible because it is not property with them; but if they sell their slaughtered animals and their method of slaughter is to strangle a sheep and beat it until it dies, it is permissible because with them it is like a slaughtered animal with us. In Jami' al-Karkhi: the sale is permissible according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, because Abu Yusuf holds that they consider it as property like wine, and Muhammad holds that their rulings are like our rulings except in wine. In Al-Dhakhira: according to us the suspended on permission is its validity, but let one consider the difference between it and the mentioned items here. Contemplate. His saying: (And I have not seen in what I have of the books anyone who called it defective) if the pronoun 'called it' refers to the sale of another's property, as apparent from the expression, the exception is not suitable unless one says he meant by another's property that which is connected with a right of another. His saying: (And it is not concluded) he meant by carrion that which dies naturally; as for that which dies by a cause like strangulation and wounding in a non-slaughter place, then the sale is defective, not void. Similarly, the slaughtered animals of the Magians are considered valuable property with them like wine; thus in Al-Mi'raj. The summary is that regarding that which does not die naturally but by a cause other than slaughter, there are two narrations concerning the non-Muslim: one narration of permissibility and one of defectiveness, but not voidness. As for us, all are the same. He said in Al-Bada'i: The sale of carrion, blood, the slaughtered animal of a Magian, apostate, polytheist, and that on which the name of God was intentionally omitted according to us, and the slaughter of an insane person and a child who does not understand, and also the slaughter of the sanctuary's game whether the slaughterer is in ihram or not, and the slaughter of a muhrim of game in the halal or haram, because all are carrion, and the sale of a muhrim's game, whether it is game of the sanctuary or the halal, is not concluded. Endquote. In Al-Bazzaziyya: the sale of intentionally omitted mentioning God's name by a non-Muslim is not permissible. Endquote. He made it absolute, so it includes whether the carrion is the sold item or the price. As for blood, he said in Al-Qamus: its dual is damyan and daman, its plural is dima', a language; its piece is damma and dmay, and it is in ladam, and it has bled blood, and I bled it, and it is bleeding. Endquote. He meant by blood the flowing blood; the sale of liver and spleen is permissible. He meant by carrion other than fish and locusts. He indicated the prohibition of what is not property, like the sale of pure human waste. And the sale of dung and camel dung and benefiting from them and using them as fuel is permissible; thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on the sale of carrion (maitah) and blood. The author explains that these are not considered property (mal) in Islamic law, and therefore their sale is void (batil) from the outset. He clarifies that the matn's use of 'la yajuz' (not permissible) covers both void and defective sales. The definition of maitah is given: any animal that has not been properly slaughtered. A distinction is made between natural death and death by cause (strangling, beating); for Muslims, all such animals are non-property. For non-Muslims, there is disagreement among Hanafi scholars: Abu Yusuf allows their sale of such items among themselves because they treat them as property comparable to wine, while Muhammad disallows it because their rulings mirror ours except for wine. The passage then lists various items whose sale is invalid, including the slaughter of Magians, apostates, polytheists, and animals on which God's name is omitted intentionally, as well as the slaughter of a muhrim's game. The sale of flowing blood is invalid, but organs like liver and spleen are permissible. Finally, items that have no value, like human waste, cannot be sold, but dung and similar items can be sold and used as fuel.

Key Terms
ميتة (maitah): An animal that has not been properly slaughtered according to Islamic law; includes animals that died naturally or by improper means.
دم (dam): Blood, specifically the flowing blood (al-dam al-masfuh).
مالية (maliyyah): The quality of being considered property (mal) in Islamic law; an essential condition for a valid sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the sale of carrion and blood according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is makruh but valid if both parties agree.
B) It is batil because they are not considered property.
C) It is fasid and can be corrected by specifying the item.
D) It is permissible for non-Muslims to sell among themselves.

Key Takeaway
The sale of carrion and blood is batil due to the absence of property value (maliyyah), an essential condition for a valid sale.

Ruling on Sale of Pig and Wine: Fasid vs Batil
Distinction between fasid and batil in prohibited sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fasid and batil in the sale of pig and wine and apply the Hanafi ruling to barter and monetary transactions.

Core Idea
Selling pig or wine is haram; the contract is fasid in barter but batil for money.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the Hanafi distinction: when pig or wine are sold for a commodity (barter), the sale is fasid (defective) because these items are considered property in some sense; when sold for currency, it is batil (void). Understanding this difference is crucial for determining legal consequences such as ownership and compensation. The passage also touches on the skin of carrion and the sale of game by a muhrim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being sold and for what consideration. The act is selling pig or wine, which is prohibited. The ruling depends on whether the consideration is another commodity (barter) or currency (money).
The text specifies that the prohibition applies to Muslims.
2
Locate the dalil
The prohibition is based on the general evidence (Quran and Sunnah) against wine and pork. The text cites Al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir, using the reasoning that wine was once property but then disgraced. The sale's existence as an exchange of property makes it fasid in barter but batil in monetary exchange because wine lacks legitimate property value in the latter.
The dalil is both naqli (Quranic prohibition) and aqli (qiyas on the nature of mal).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In a barter, the items are exchanged as commodities; wine is considered mal ghayr mutaqawwim (non-compensable property) allowing the contract to be fasid. In a monetary sale, the price is fungible and wine is not recognized as valid property, making the contract batil. The text highlights that the buyer's freeing of a slave received in exchange is effective if the slave was obtained via barter, but not if obtained via monetary sale.
The key is the nature of the consideration: commodity vs. currency.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that pig and wine are never property, so their sale is always batil regardless of consideration. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the historical status of wine as property before prohibition.
This ikhtilaf leads to practical differences in legal outcomes.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The author adopts the Hanafi position, preferring the distinction between fasid and batil. The tarjih is based on the principle of caution and honoring the commodity in barter transactions.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that sale is fasid in barter and batil in monetary exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والخنزير والخمر أي في حق المسلم للنهي عن بيعهما وقربانهما. وصرح في الهداية بالفساد فيهما لوجود حقيقة البيع وهو مبادلة المال بالمال فإنه مال عند البعض، ومراده ما إذا كانا مبيعين قوبلا بعرض بيع مقايضة، أما إذا قوبلا بالدراهم أو الدنانير فالبيع باطل حتى لو بيع أحدهما بعبد فقبضه البائع وأعتقه نفذ عتقه، ولو استحقه مستحق فالمشتري خصم له بخلاف بيعه بالميتة إذا أعتقه لم ينفذ، وإذا استحق فليس بخصم كما في البناية. والفرق أن الخمر مال في الجملة في شرع ثم أمر بإهانتها في شرع آخر بطريق النسخ، وفي تمليكها بالعقد مقصوداً اعزاز له بخلاف جعله ثمناً. واعتبر في بيع المقايضة الخمر ثمناً العرض مبيعاً والعكس وإن كان ممكناً لكن ترجح هذا الاعتبار لما فيه من الاحتياط للقرب من تصحيح تصرف العقلاء المكلفين بطريق الإعزاز للعرض فاعتبرنا ذكرها لإعزاز الثوب لا الثوب للخمر فوجبت قيمة العرض لا الخمر ولا فرق بين دخول البائع على الثوب أو الخمر في جعل الثوب هو المبيع؛ كذا في فتح القدير والحاصل أن . بيع نفس الخمر باطل مطلقاً وإنما الكلام فيما قابله، فإن ديناً كان باطلاً أيضاً، وإن عرضاً كان فساداً. وجلد الميتة كالخمر في رواية ... وهو بيع صيد المحرم الخ) قال الرملي : تقدم في الحج في الكلام على جزاء الصيد أنه إن كان قد اصطاده حلال ثم أحرم فباعه فإن المشتري يضمن له قيمته وهو يقتضي فساد البيع وبه صرح في النهر فعلم أن بيع صيد الحلال للمحرم فاسد سواء باعه وهو محرم أو حلال، وإذا أتلفه المحرم ضمن قيمته لصاحبه ومثله الله تعالى جزاء الصيد والله تعالى أعلم. قوله : (وفي البزازية بيع متروك التسمية
His saying: (And the pig and wine) meaning in the right of a Muslim, due to the prohibition of selling them and taking them as a means of approach. And it is explicitly stated in Al-Hidayah that they are defective due to the existence of the reality of a sale, which is an exchange of property for property, for they are property according to some. And what is meant is when they are sold items, they are paired with a commodity in a barter sale. As for when they are paired with dirhams or dinars, the sale is void, such that if one of them is sold for a slave and the seller takes possession and frees him, his freeing is effective; and if a claimant claims the slave, the buyer is a valid opponent for him, unlike the case of selling it for carrion: if he frees it, it is not effective, and if it is claimed, he is not a valid opponent, as in Al-Binaya. The difference is that wine is property in general in one law, then it was commanded to be disgraced in another law by way of abrogation, and making it the object of ownership by contract intentionally is an honor for it, unlike making it a price. And in a barter sale, it was considered that wine as a price and the commodity as sold, and vice versa, even though it is possible, but this consideration is preponderant because of caution and nearness to validating the transactions of rational responsible persons by way of honoring the commodity. So we considered mentioning it for honoring the garment, not the garment for the wine. Thus the value of the commodity is due, not the wine. And there is no difference whether the seller includes the garment or the wine in making the garment the sold item; thus in Fath al-Qadir. The summary is that the sale of wine itself is absolutely void; the discussion is only about what it is exchanged for. If exchanged for a debt, it is also void; if for a commodity, it is defective. And the skin of carrion is like wine in one narration. And it is the sale of a muhrim's game, etc. Al-Ramli said: It preceded in Hajj in the discussion on the penalty for game that if a non-muhrim hunted then entered ihram and sold it, the buyer guarantees its value, which implies the sale is defective. It is stated explicitly in Al-Nahr. Thus it is known that the sale of a halal's game to a muhrim is defective, whether the seller sold it while in ihram or not. And if the muhrim destroys it, he guarantees its value to its owner, and like it is the penalty for game, God knows best. His saying: (And in Al-Bazzaziyya, the sale of intentionally omitted tasmiya)
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale of pig and wine, which is prohibited for Muslims. The author cites Al-Hidayah that these sales are fasid (defective) because the reality of a sale exists: an exchange of property for property, since pig and wine are considered property by some (non-Muslims). However, this applies only when they are the subject matter in a barter exchange. If exchanged for dirhams or dinars (currency), the sale is batil (void). The practical difference: if wine is sold for a slave and the seller frees the slave, the freeing is valid; but if wine is sold for carrion, freeing would not be valid. Then a detailed discussion follows on the status of wine: it was once property but then commanded to be disgraced. In a barter transaction, scholars consider the wine as the price and the commodity as the sold item to honor the commodity, so the value of the commodity is due, not the wine. The sale of wine itself is absolutely void. The skin of carrion is treated like wine in one narration. Then the passage discusses the sale of a muhrim's game: if a non-muhrim hunts, then enters ihram, and sells the game, the sale is fasid. Finally, a brief mention of sale of intentionally omitted tasmiya from Al-Bazzaziyya, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Intoxicating wine; in this context, it is considered mal (property) in a general sense but its sale is prohibited.
بيع مقايضة (bay' muqayadah): Barter sale: an exchange of a commodity for a commodity without using currency.
جلد الميتة (jild al-maitah): The skin of a dead animal (carrion) that has not been tanned; its ruling is similar to wine in some narrations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when wine is sold for a slave in a barter exchange?
A) The sale is valid and the slave becomes the property of the seller.
B) The sale is batil and the slave remains the property of the original owner.
C) The sale is fasid and the seller must give the value of the slave.
D) The sale is makruh but valid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the sale of pig and wine is prohibited; the contract is fasid in barter exchanges but batil in monetary exchanges.

The Prohibition of Selling Carrion and Blood
Explains ruling on sale of carrion and blood with types and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling carrion and blood, distinguish between fasid and batil sales based on cause of death, and identify the exceptions and additional prohibited items.

Core Idea
Selling carrion and poured-out blood is invalid, but liver, spleen, fish, and locusts are exceptions.

The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the comprehensive Hanafi ruling on selling carrion (maytah) and blood (dam). It not only declares the general prohibition but also delineates nuanced distinctions: carrion from natural death vs. death by cause, the ruling for non-Muslims, and a list of exceptions such as liver and spleen. Understanding these details is essential for correctly identifying valid and invalid sales in Islamic commercial law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Ruling and its Basis
The text states that the sale of carrion and blood does not take effect (la yan'qidu). Recognise that carrion and poured-out blood are not considered property (mal) for Muslims and are ritually impure (najis), making their sale invalid.
The ruling is based on Quran and Sunnah; the passage does not cite proofs but assumes them known.
2
Distinguish Natural Death from Death by Cause
Note the two categories: 'ma'at hatt anfihi' (died of itself) – its sale is batil (void). 'Al-maytah bi al-sabab' (died by cause like strangulation) – its sale is fasid (defective). Understand the difference: batil has no legal effect; fasid can be corrected if the defect is removed.
For non-Muslims, there is a narration difference, but for Muslims the ruling is uniform.
3
Define Blood and Its Exceptions
Blood (dam) here means poured-out blood (dam masfuh). Liver and spleen are not included in this category, so their sale is permissible. Identify that the lexical clarification ensures that organs with blood residue are not automatically prohibited.
The text cites al-Qamus for linguistic confirmation.
4
List Additional Prohibited Items and Exceptions
The passage enumerates items whose sale is invalid: carrion, blood, slaughter of a Magian, apostate, polytheist, intentionally unslaughtered meat (matruk al-tasmiyah), and hunt of a muhrim. Exceptions: fish and locusts are not carrion; animal droppings (sarjin, ba'r) are saleable, but pure dung ('adharah khalisah) is not.
The ruling on unslaughtered meat highlights the Hanafi position that tasmiyah is wajib, contrary to Shafi'is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أراد بالميتة ما مات حتف أنفه أما التي ماتت بالسبب كالخنق والجرح في غير موضع الذبح فالمبيع فاسد لا باطل، وكذلك ذبائح المجوس مال متقوم عندهم بمنزلة الخمر؛ كذا في المعراج. وحاصله أن فيما لم يمت حتف أنفه بل بسبب غير الذكاة روايتين بالنسبة إلى الكافر، وفي رواية الجواز وفي رواية الفساد، وأما البطلان فلا، وأما في حقنا فالكل سواء. قال في البدائع : ولا ينعقد بيع الميتة والدم وذبيحة المجوسي والمرتد والمشرك ومتروك التسمية عمداً عندنا وذبيحة المجنون والصبي الذي لا يعقل، وكذا ذبيحة صيد الحرم محرماً كان الذابح أو حلالاً، وذبيحة المحرم من الصيد في الحل أو الحرم لأن الكل ميتة، ولا ينعقد بيع صيد المحرم سواء كان صيد الحرم أو الحل .اهـ. وفي البزازية بيع متروك التسمية عمداً من كافر لا يجوز .اهـ. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كانت الميتة مبيعاً أو ثمناً. والدم قال في القاموس : تثنیته دمیان ودمان وجمعه دماء لغة وقطعته دمة ودمي وهي في لدم وقد دمي دماً، وأدميته ودميته وهو دامي .اهـ. وأراد بالدم الدم المسفوح، بيع الكبد والطحال فإنه جائز. وأراد بالميتة ما سوى السمك والجراد، وأشار إلى منع ما ليس بمال كبيع العذرة الخالصة، ويجوز بيع السرقين والبعر والانتفاع به والوقود به ؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج .
He meant by carrion that which died of itself. As for that which died by cause, such as strangulation or wounding in a place other than the slaughter spot, the sale is fasid (defective), not batil (void). Similarly, the slaughtered animals of the Magians are considered valuable property in their view, akin to wine; thus in al-Mi'raj. The summary is that regarding what did not die by itself but by a cause other than proper slaughter, there are two narrations with respect to the non-Muslim: in one narration it is permissible, in another it is fasid, but never batil. As for us (Muslims), all are the same. He said in al-Bada'i': The sale of carrion, blood, the slaughtered animal of a Magian, apostate, polytheist, and that on which the tasmiyah was intentionally omitted—according to us—and the slaughtered animal of an insane person or a child who does not understand reason, and likewise the slaughtered animal of the sanctuary hunt whether the slaughterer was in ihram or not, and the slaughtered animal of a muhrim from hunting in the halal or the haram, because all are carrion; and the sale of the muhrim's hunted animal is not valid whether it was the sanctuary hunt or the non-sanctuary hunt. End quote. In al-Bazzaziyya: The sale of that on which the tasmiyah was intentionally omitted by a non-Muslim is not permissible. End quote. He made it general, so it includes whether the carrion is the object of sale or the price. As for blood, he said in al-Qamus: Its dual is damian and daman, its plural is dima'; language; a piece of it is dimma and dumiy, and it is in [the word] ladam; one says 'damiya' and 'admaytuhu' and 'damaytuhu', and it is 'damin'. End quote. He meant by blood the poured-out blood; the sale of liver and spleen is permissible. He meant by carrion everything except fish and locusts. He indicated the prohibition of what is not property, like the sale of pure dung; and the sale of sarjin (dung) and ba'r (animal droppings) and their use and burning is permissible; thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explaining the types of carrion and blood that cannot be sold. The author first distinguishes between carrion that dies naturally (ma'at hafth anfihi), which is completely forbidden to sell, and carrion that dies due to an external cause (al-maitatu bil-sabab) like strangulation—this is considered fasid (defective) but not batil. For non-Muslims, there are two narrations on its permissibility, but for Muslims it is all the same. Quoting al-Bada'i', the text lists many items whose sale is not valid: carrion, blood, Magian slaughter, apostate's slaughter, polytheist's slaughter, intentionally unslaughtered meat, and slaughter by an insane person or child. Also the sale of a muhrim's hunt is invalid. The text then clarifies that 'blood' here means poured-out blood, so liver and spleen are saleable. Carrion excludes fish and locusts. Items that are not property, like pure dung, cannot be sold, but animal droppings can be sold and used for fuel.

Key Terms
الميتة (al-maytah): An animal that died without proper Islamic slaughter, including those that died naturally or by cause without slaughter.
الدم المسفوح (al-dam al-masfuh): Poured-out blood, which is impure and cannot be sold; liver and spleen are not included.
متروك التسمية (matruk al-tasmiyah): An animal slaughtered without reciting 'Bismillah' intentionally; its sale is invalid.
فاسد (fasid): Defective sale – a contract that is irregular but can be corrected if the defect is removed; in some contexts, it is distinguished from batil (void).
باطل (batil): Void sale – a contract that has no legal effect from inception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is permissible to sell?
A) Liver and spleen
B) Blood from a wound
C) Meat of a natural dead animal
D) Dung (pure, al-'adharah al-khalisah)

Key Takeaway
Selling carrion and poured-out blood is invalid; exceptions include liver, spleen, fish, locusts, and animal droppings, while pure dung and all forms of carrion remain prohibited.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Sale of Pig and Wine: Fasid vs. Batil
Explaining fasid vs. batil in sale of prohibited items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fasid and batil in the sale of prohibited items and apply the Hanafi position to cognate cases like musical instruments.

Core Idea
The sale of wine or pig is fasid when bartered for goods, batil when exchanged for cash—this distinction affects legal consequences.

When a Muslim sells a prohibited substance like wine or a pig, the contract is not simply void. The Hanafi school differentiates between fasid (defective) and batil (void) based on the consideration: if the price is a commodity (barter), the sale is fasid; if cash (dirhams/dinars), it is batil. This distinction has practical implications for the validity of subsequent acts, such as freeing a slave received in exchange. The passage also touches on the status of dhimmis and the sale of musical instruments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The text distinguishes between selling pig/wine for a commodity (barter) and for cash. State which category produces a fasid sale and which produces a batil sale.
Fasid: exchange for goods; Batil: exchange for dirhams/dinars.
2
Trace the legal consequence
Examine the example of selling wine for a slave: if the seller frees the slave, it is effective because the sale is fasid (existed in reality). Compare with batil where no ownership transfers.
The text uses this to show the practical difference.
3
Apply to dhimmis
Understand that dhimmis are not prevented from selling these items, but after embracing Islam, the contract may be rescinded depending on possession.
This shows how legal capacity changes the ruling.
4
Extend to musical instruments
Note the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa (valid but makruh) and the two companions (invalid). Recognize that the fatwa follows the companions' view on liability.
This demonstrates intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والخنزير والخمر أي في حق المسلم للنهي عن بيعهما وقربانهما. وصرح في الهداية بالفساد فيهما لوجود حقيقة البيع وهو مبادلة المال بالمال فإنه مال عند البعض، ومراده ما إذا كانا مبيعين قوبلا بعرض بيع مقايضة، أما إذا قوبلا بالدراهم أو الدنانير فالبيع باطل حتى لو بيع أحدهما بعبد فقبضه البائع وأعتقه نفذ عتقه، ولو استحقه مستحق فالمشتري خصم له بخلاف بيعه بالميتة إذا أعتقه لم ينفذ، وإذا استحق فليس بخصم كما في البناية. والفرق أن الخمر مال في الجملة في شرع ثم أمر بإهانتها في شرع آخر بطريق النسخ، وفي تمليكها بالعقد مقصوداً اعزاز له بخلاف جعله ثمناً. واعتبر في بيع المقايضة الخمر ثمناً العرض مبيعاً والعكس وإن كان ممكناً لكن ترجح هذا الاعتبار لما فيه من الاحتياط للقرب من تصحيح تصرف العقلاء المكلفين بطريق الإعزاز للعرض فاعتبرنا ذكرها لإعزاز الثوب لا الثوب للخمر فوجبت قيمة العرض لا الخمر ولا فرق بين دخول البائع على الثوب أو الخمر في جعل الثوب هو المبيع؛ كذا في فتح القدير والحاصل أن . بيع نفس الخمر باطل مطلقاً وإنما الكلام فيما قابله، فإن ديناً كان باطلاً أيضاً، وإن عرضاً كان فساداً. وجلد الميتة كالخمر في رواية وهو كالميتة في أخرى. وفي القاموس : الخمر ما أسكر من عصير العنب أو عام كالخمرة، وقد تذكر والعموم أصح لأنها حرمت وما بالمدينة خمر عنب وما كان شرابهم إلا البسر والتمر اهـ. قيد بالخمر لأن ما سواها من الأشربة المحرمة كالسكر ونقيع الزبيب والمنصف جائز عنده بيع خلافاً لهما ؛ كذا في البدائع وقيدنا بالمسلم لأن أهل الذمة ما يمنعون من بيعها، ثم اختلفوا فقال بعضهم : يباح الانتفاع بهما لهم شرعاً كالخل والشاة فكان مالاً في حقهم. وقال بعضهم : هما حرامان عليهما لأن الكفار مخاطبون بالحرمات وهو الصحيح من مذهب أصحابنا ولكن لا يمنعون من بيعهما لأنهم يعتقدون الحل والتمول وقد أمرنا بتركهم وما يدينون؛ كذا في البدائع. وأشار المؤلف إلى أن الذميين إذا تبايعا خمراً أو خنزيراً ثم أسلما أو أسلم أحدهما قبل القبض فإن البيع يفسخ لأن التسليم والقبض حرام كالبيع بخلاف ما إذا كان الإسلام بعد القبض لأن الموجود الدوام وهو لا ينافي ولو أقرض الذمي خمراً من ذمي ثم أسلم أحدهما، فإن أسلم المقرض سقطت الخمر لأن إسلامه مانع من قبضها ولا شيء له من قيمتها على المستقرض لأن العجز جاء من قبله وإن أسلم المستقرض ففيه روايتان : في رواية كالأول وفي أخرى وهو قول محمد تجب قيمتها ؛ كذا في البدائع. وقيد بالخمر والخنزير لأن بيع آلات اللهو كالبربط والطبل والمزمار والدف صحيح مكروه عند الإمام. وقالا : لا ينعقد بيعها والصحيح قوله للانتفاع بها شرعاً من وجه آخر. وعلى هذا الاختلاف بيع والشطرنج، وعلى هذا الاختلاف الضمان على من أتلفها فعنده يضمن وعندهما لا؛ كذا في البدائع. ولكن الفتوى في الضمان على قولهما كما سيأتي في الغصب ومحله ما إذا كسرها غير القاضي والمحتسب أما هما فلا ضمان اتفاقاً، وقد ذكر في أول سير اليتيمة الفرق بين المتقوم والمعصوم اهـ.
His statement: (And the pig and wine) i.e. with respect to the Muslim, because of the prohibition of selling them and approaching them. And al-Hidayah explicitly stated that they are fasid due to the existence of the reality of sale, which is the exchange of property for property, for they are property according to some. What is meant is when they are the objects of sale and are exchanged for a commodity in a barter sale. But if they are exchanged for dirhams or dinars, the sale is batil. So if one of them is sold for a slave, and the seller takes possession and frees him, his freeing is effective; and if a rightful claimant claims the slave, the buyer is his opponent. Unlike the sale of it for carrion, if he frees it, it does not take effect, and if a claim is made, he is not an opponent, as in al-Binaya. The difference is that wine is property in one legal system, then it is ordered to be degraded in another legal system by way of abrogation. And making it the subject of a contract is an honour to it; unlike making it the price. In barter sale, wine is considered as the price and the commodity as the object of sale, and the opposite, although possible, this consideration is preferred because it involves caution towards validating the transactions of sane obligated persons by honouring the commodity. So we consider mentioning it for honouring the garment, not the garment for wine. So the value of the commodity is due, not the wine. There is no difference whether the seller enters upon the garment or the wine in making the garment the object of sale; thus in Fath al-Qadir. The summary: The sale of wine itself is absolutely batil. The discussion is only about its counterpart: if it is a debt, it is also batil; if it is a commodity, it is fasid. The skin of carrion is like wine in one narration and like carrion in another. And in al-Qamus: Wine is that which intoxicates from grape juice, or generally like wine. It may be masculine, but the general meaning is more correct because it was prohibited and there was no grape wine in Madinah; their drink was only unripe and ripe dates. End quote. He restricted to wine because other prohibited drinks like sukka, naqi' al-zabib, and munassaf are permissible to sell according to him (Abu Hanifa), contrary to the two companions; thus in al-Bada'i'. And we restricted to Muslims because the People of the Covenant are not prevented from selling them. Then they differed: some said that benefit from them is legally permissible for them, like vinegar and a sheep, so they are property for them. Others said they are prohibited for them because the disbelievers are addressed by prohibitions, and this is the correct position of our school, but they are not prevented from selling them because they consider them permissible and valuable, and we are commanded to leave them and their religion; thus in al-Bada'i'. The author indicated that if two dhimmis buy and sell wine or pigs, then they embrace Islam, or one of them embraces Islam before possession, the sale is rescinded because delivery and possession are prohibited like the sale itself. Unlike if Islam occurs after possession, because what exists is continuance, which is not contradictory. And if a dhimmi lends wine to a dhimmi, then one of them embraces Islam: if the lender embraces Islam, the wine is cancelled because his Islam prevents him from taking possession, and he has nothing of its value from the borrower because the inability came from himself. If the borrower embraces Islam, there are two narrations: in one like the first, and in another—which is the saying of Muhammad—its value is due; thus in al-Bada'i'. He restricted to wine and pig because the sale of musical instruments like barbat, drum, mizmar, and duff is valid but makruh according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). The two companions said: their sale is not valid. The correct position is his saying because they can be used beneficially in some other way. And on this difference depends the sale of backgammon. And on this difference depends liability for destroying them: according to him, he must guarantee; according to them, no; thus in al-Bada'i'. But the fatwa on liability is according to their saying, as will come in the chapter on usurpation. Its place is when someone other than the judge or muhtasib breaks them; as for them, no liability by agreement. And the difference between mu'taqawwam and ma'sum was mentioned at the beginning of Sirr al-Yatimah.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof discussing the sale of pig and wine. The author explains that for Muslims, selling pig and wine is prohibited due to explicit textual prohibition. Al-Hidayah states the sale is fasid (defective) when exchanged for another commodity, but batil (void) when exchanged for cash. The reasoning involves whether wine is considered property. For dhimmis, there is a difference of opinion: some say it is permissible for them to sell, others say it is prohibited but they are not prevented. The passage also covers post-Islam effects on prior contracts. It then addresses the sale of musical instruments and backgammon, where Abu Hanifa considers the sale valid but makruh, while the two companions deem it invalid. The fatwa on liability follows the two companions' view.

Key Terms
الخمر (al-khamr): Intoxicating drink from grape juice, generally extended to any intoxicant.
آلات اللهو (alat al-lahw): Musical instruments like barbat, drum, mizmar, and duff.
فاسد (fasid): Defective sale: contract exists but with a flaw; it may transfer ownership.
باطل (batil): Void sale: no contract exists; no legal effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The sale is fasid and ownership transfers.
C) The sale is valid and permitted.
D) The sale is makruh but valid.

Key Takeaway
The sale of wine or pig by a Muslim is fasid when bartered for goods, but batil when sold for cash; this distinction has practical effects on ownership transfer and subsequent acts.

Sale of Free Persons, Mudabbar, and Umm Walad: Invalid Subjects
Prohibition of selling free persons and certain slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the sale of free persons, absolute mudabbar, and umm walad is invalid and identify the reasons based on their freedom rights.

Core Idea
Free persons and slaves with vested freedom rights cannot be sold because they are not property.

The text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that selling a free person, a mudabbar (absolute), and an umm walad is invalid. The reasons differ: free persons have no monetary value, while mudabbar and umm walad have established entitlements to freedom that would be defeated by sale. This lesson examines the legal reasoning behind each ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
List the three types of beings that cannot be sold according to the passage: free persons, mudabbar (absolute), and umm walad.
Mudabbar here refers to the absolute mudabbar only.
2
Understand the reason for each
For free persons, the reason is 'la maliyyah' (no monetary value). For mudabbar and umm walad, the reason is that they have an established right to freedom (istihqaq al-‘itq).
The passage quotes a hadith for umm walad: 'Her child frees her.'
3
Distinguish absolute vs restricted mudabbar
Note that only the absolute mudabbar (al-mudabbar al-mutlaq) is prohibited from sale; the restricted mudabbar (al-muqayyad) can be sold.
This distinction is specific to the Hanafi school.
4
Recognize consensus
These rulings are agreed upon by all schools (muttafaq alayh) for free persons and umm walad. For mudabbar, there is also consensus on the absolute type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والحر والمدبر وأم الولد والمكاتب) أي بيع هؤلاء غير جائز أي غير منعقد، أما في الحر فلعدم المالية، وأما المدبر وأم الولد فقد صرح في الهداية ببطلان بيعهما قال : لأن استحقاق العتق قد ثبت ومن الولد لقوله عليه السلام أعتقها ولدها وسبب الحرية انعقد في حق المدبر في الحال لبطلان الأهلية بعد الموت والمكاتب استحق العتق يداً على نفسه لازمة في حق المولى، ولو ثبت الملك بالبيع لبطل ذلك كله فلا يجوز ولو رضي المكاتب بالبيع ففيه روايتان والأظهر الجواز. والمراد بالمدبر المطلق دون المقيد أي فإنه يجوز بيعه اهـ. ولو بيع المكاتب بغير رضاه فأجاز بيعه لا ينفذ في الصحيح من الرواية وعليه عامة المشايخ ؛ كذا في
His statement: (And the free person, the mudabbar, the umm walad, and the mukatab) i.e. the sale of these is not permissible, i.e. not contracted. As for the free person, because he has no monetary value. As for the mudabbar and umm walad, al-Hidayah explicitly stated the nullity of their sale, saying: because the entitlement to freedom has been established, and for the umm walad due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Her child frees her.' And the cause of freedom is established for the mudabbar immediately in terms of the nullification of ownership after death. The mukatab is entitled to freedom by virtue of his contract which is binding on the master. If ownership were established by sale, all that would be nullified, so it is not permissible. If the mukatab consents to the sale, there are two narrations, and the more apparent is permissibility. What is meant by mudabbar is the absolute mudabbar, not the restricted one, for its sale is permissible. End quote. And if the mukatab is sold without his consent and he then permits the sale, it does not take effect in the sound narration, and this is the view of the majority of the mashayikh; thus in...
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This is a ruling statement explaining the prohibition of selling free persons (ahrar), mudabbar slaves (whose freedom is conditional upon the master's death), umm walad (slave mother of the master's child), and mukatab (slave who contracted to purchase his freedom). The reason is that they have rights to freedom that would be defeated by sale. For the mudabbar, it is the absolute (unrestricted) mudabbar that cannot be sold; a restricted mudabbar can be sold. For the mukatab, if he consents to the sale, some narrations allow it, others not; the more apparent view is permissibility. If he is sold without consent and later permits, that permission does not validate the sale.

Key Terms
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whose master declares that he will be free upon the master's death; his sale is invalid.
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A slave woman who has given birth to her master's child; she becomes free upon her master's death and cannot be sold.
الحر (al-hurr): A free person; cannot be sold because he has no monetary value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the sale of a free person invalid?
A) Because he has a right to freedom.
B) Because he has no monetary value.
C) Because he is not a slave.
D) Because the Prophet forbade it.

Key Takeaway
Free persons and slaves with absolute freedom rights cannot be sold; the sale is batil.

Sale of Mukatab: Consent and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on sale of mukatab and ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling a mukatab and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding his consent.

Core Idea
A mukatab cannot be sold without consent; if he consents, there is a valid difference of opinion.

The passage also covers the mukatab, a slave under contract to purchase his freedom. His sale without consent is invalid because it defeats his right to freedom. However, if he consents, the Hanafi school has two narrations: one allowing the sale and one prohibiting it. This lesson examines both views and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling
The sale of a mukatab without his consent is invalid (ghayr ja'iz). The reason is that he has a binding right to freedom (huqqatan lazimatan).
2
Identify the two narrations on consent
If the mukatab consents to the sale, there are two narrations (riwayatan): one permits it, the other prohibits it. The more apparent (azhar) view is permissibility.
The text says 'والأظهر الجواز'.
3
Understand the majority position on post-sale permission
If the mukatab is sold without consent and later permits the sale, the permission does not validate the sale according to the sound narration (sahih al-riwayah), and this is the view of the majority of mashayikh.
This shows that consent must be given at the time of sale, not after.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والحر والمدبر وأم الولد والمكاتب) أي بيع هؤلاء غير جائز أي غير منعقد، أما في الحر فلعدم المالية، وأما المدبر وأم الولد فقد صرح في الهداية ببطلان بيعهما قال : لأن استحقاق العتق قد ثبت ومن الولد لقوله عليه السلام أعتقها ولدها وسبب الحرية انعقد في حق المدبر في الحال لبطلان الأهلية بعد الموت والمكاتب استحق العتق يداً على نفسه لازمة في حق المولى، ولو ثبت الملك بالبيع لبطل ذلك كله فلا يجوز ولو رضي المكاتب بالبيع ففيه روايتان والأظهر الجواز. والمراد بالمدبر المطلق دون المقيد أي فإنه يجوز بيعه اهـ. ولو بيع المكاتب بغير رضاه فأجاز بيعه لا ينفذ في الصحيح من الرواية وعليه عامة المشايخ ؛ كذا في
His statement: (And the free person, the mudabbar, the umm walad, and the mukatab) i.e. the sale of these is not permissible, i.e. not contracted. As for the free person, because he has no monetary value. As for the mudabbar and umm walad, al-Hidayah explicitly stated the nullity of their sale, saying: because the entitlement to freedom has been established, and for the umm walad due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Her child frees her.' And the cause of freedom is established for the mudabbar immediately in terms of the nullification of ownership after death. The mukatab is entitled to freedom by virtue of his contract which is binding on the master. If ownership were established by sale, all that would be nullified, so it is not permissible. If the mukatab consents to the sale, there are two narrations, and the more apparent is permissibility. What is meant by mudabbar is the absolute mudabbar, not the restricted one, for its sale is permissible. End quote. And if the mukatab is sold without his consent and he then permits the sale, it does not take effect in the sound narration, and this is the view of the majority of the mashayikh; thus in...
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This is a ruling statement explaining the prohibition of selling free persons (ahrar), mudabbar slaves (whose freedom is conditional upon the master's death), umm walad (slave mother of the master's child), and mukatab (slave who contracted to purchase his freedom). The reason is that they have rights to freedom that would be defeated by sale. For the mudabbar, it is the absolute (unrestricted) mudabbar that cannot be sold; a restricted mudabbar can be sold. For the mukatab, if he consents to the sale, some narrations allow it, others not; the more apparent view is permissibility. If he is sold without consent and later permits, that permission does not validate the sale.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has a contract of manumission with his master; he cannot be sold without his consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale becomes valid because he gave permission.
B) The sale does not take effect in the sound narration.
C) The sale is valid if the buyer agrees.
D) The sale is automatically canceled.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's sale is default invalid; consent creates a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Selling Non-Wine Intoxicants: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on selling non-wine intoxicants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on selling non-wine intoxicants and identify the key difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa distinguishes khamr from other intoxicants; the Sahibayn prohibit selling all intoxicants.

This lesson examines a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the sale of intoxicants other than wine (khamr). The passage defines khamr as grape-wine and then notes that while selling khamr is haram by consensus, other intoxicating drinks like sakar (date-wine) are considered permissible for sale by Imam Abu Hanifa. His two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree and prohibit such sales. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi fiqh in trade.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is selling non-wine intoxicants (sakar, naqee' al-zabeeb, etc.). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the ruling is permissible (mubah). According to the Sahibayn, it is haram.
2
Locate the dalil for Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa relies on the lexical definition of khamr from Al-Qamus, which restricts khamr to intoxicating grape juice. He also notes that at the time of revelation, the common intoxicating drinks in Medina were from dates, not grapes, so the Quranic prohibition of khamr did not extend to them.
The dalil is a combination of language (lugha) and historical context (urf).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Since khamr is specifically defined as grape-wine, the prohibition applies only to it. Other intoxicants are not included in the textual prohibition; thus their sale is not forbidden by the text.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and the opponent's proof
The Sahibayn hold that all intoxicants are prohibited. Their dalil is qiyas: they equate all intoxicants with khamr due to the common illa (effective cause) of intoxication.
This is a dispute over the scope of the word 'khamr' and the method of analogical reasoning.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Later Hanafi jurists (e.g., al-Sarakhsi) preferred the Sahibayn's view for caution, making the mufta bihi (operative fatwa) that selling non-wine intoxicants is haram.
The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah; the tarjih is based on precaution (ihtiyat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكالميتة في أخرى. وفي القاموس : الخمر ما أسكر من عصير العنب أو عام كالخمرة، وقد تذكر والعموم أصح لأنها حرمت وما بالمدينة خمر عنب وما كان شرابهم إلا البسر والتمر اهـ. قيد بالخمر لأن ما سواها من الأشربة المحرمة كالسكر ونقيع الزبيب والمنصف جائز عنده بيع خلافاً لهما ؛ كذا في البدائع
And like carrion in another. In Al-Qamus: Al-khamr is what intoxicates from grape juice, or general like khamr. It may be feminine and the general meaning is more correct because it was prohibited and in Medina there was no grape wine, their drink was only unripe dates and dates. He restricted to wine because other prohibited drinks like sakar, naqee' al-zabeeb, and al-munasif are permissible for sale according to him, contrary to them (the two companions). This is in Al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary. It first defines wine (khamr) according to the Qamus as intoxicating grape juice or generally any intoxicant. The author restricts the ruling to wine specifically, because other intoxicating beverages (like sakar, naqee' al-zabeeb, and munasif) are considered permissible to sell according to Imam Abu Hanifa, contrary to his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). This is a well-known intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa differentiates between wine (whose prohibition is explicit in the Quran) and other intoxicants; the Sahibayn equate all intoxicants. The authority weight is from Bada'i' al-Sana'i', a Zahir al-Riwayah source. The practical consequence: Muslims may engage in trade of non-wine intoxicants according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
الخمر (al-khamr): Intoxicating drink from grape juice; the text notes it may be general but here restricted to wine.
السكر (al-sakar): An intoxicating beverage made from dates or other fruits, not from grapes.
نقيع الزبيب (naqee' al-zabeeb): A drink made by soaking raisins; may become intoxicating if fermented.
المنصف (al-munasif): A type of intoxicating drink (likely a mixture).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on selling 'sakar' (date wine)?
A) It is haram and the sale is invalid
B) It is makruh tahrimi
C) It is permissible (ja'iz)
D) It is mubah with dislike

Key Takeaway
The sale of non-wine intoxicants is permissible according to Imam Abu Hanifa but prohibited by the Sahibayn, with the mufta bihi being prohibition.

Ruling on Dhimmis Selling Wine and Pigs
Discusses the permissibility of Dhimmis trading in wine and pigs among themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Dhimmis trading wine and pigs and explain why they are not prevented despite the prohibition.

Core Idea
Dhimmis are bound by Islamic prohibitions but left to their own practices — the sale is haram but not prevented.

You have learned that Muslims are prohibited from selling wine and pigs. But what about Dhimmis? The Hanafi school has an internal discussion on this issue. This lesson will clarify the correct position: Dhimmis are addressed by the same prohibitions, but they are not forced to abandon their trade because of their beliefs. Understanding this distinction is crucial for dealing with interfaith transactions in a Muslim state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
The passage specifies that the previous restriction applies to Muslims; now we consider the ruling for Dhimmis. Note the phrase 'people of dhimmah' in the text.
Dhimmi = non-Muslim under Islamic protection.
2
State the two views
Some Hanafi scholars held that wine and pigs are permissible for Dhimmis (like vinegar and sheep), so they are considered property. Others said they are prohibited for them because they are addressed by Islamic prohibitions.
The first view treats them as completely separate from Islamic law.
3
Identify the correct Hanafi view
The author states that the correct view (al-sahih) is the second: wine and pigs are haram for Dhimmis. This is the relied-upon position of the school.
‘Al-sahih min madhhab ashabina’ indicates the preferred view.
4
Explain why they are not prevented
Although the act is prohibited for them, they are not forced to abandon it because they believe it is permissible and we are commanded to leave them to their own religion (la ikraha fi al-din).
The proof is the general principle of religious freedom combined with the command to respect their dhimmah contract.
5
Note the authority weight
The authority for this position is Al-Bada'i' (a Hanafi text), and it is classified as part of Zahir al-Riwayah.
Zahir al-Riwayah = the core transmitted rulings of the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيدنا بالمسلم لأن أهل الذمة ما يمنعون من بيعها، ثم اختلفوا فقال بعضهم : يباح الانتفاع بهما لهم شرعاً كالخل والشاة فكان مالاً في حقهم. وقال بعضهم : هما حرامان عليهما لأن الكفار مخاطبون بالحرمات وهو الصحيح من مذهب أصحابنا ولكن لا يمنعون من بيعهما لأنهم يعتقدون الحل والتمول وقد أمرنا بتركهم وما يدينون؛ كذا في البدائع.
And we restricted to the Muslim because the people of dhimmah are not prevented from selling them. Then they differed: some said it is permissible for them to benefit from them legally, like vinegar and a sheep, so they are property in their regard. Some said they are both prohibited upon them because disbelievers are addressed by prohibitions, and this is the correct view of our school, but they are not prevented from selling them because they believe in permissibility and valuation, and we are commanded to leave them and what they believe. This is in Al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on wine and pork. The author specifies that the previous ruling restricting sale of wine applies to Muslims; Dhimmis are not prevented from selling these items among themselves. There is an internal dispute: some scholars say these items are permissible for Dhimmis (like vinegar and sheep) and thus considered property. Others say they remain prohibited for Dhimmis (since they are addressed by Islamic laws), but they are not prevented from trading because they do not accept the prohibition. The correct position according to the Hanafi school is the latter: Dhimmis are subject to prohibitions but are left to their own religious practices. The authority weight is from Bada'i'.

Key Terms
أهل الذمة (ahl al-dhimmah): Non-Muslims living under Muslim rule with a contract of protection.
الخمر (al-khamr): Wine; any intoxicating drink made from grapes.
الخنزير (al-khinzeer): Pig; its meat is haram and its sale is prohibited for Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is permissible because it is their property
B) It is prohibited but they are not prevented
C) It is invalid and they must be stopped
D) It is makruh tanzihi

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that Dhimmis are bound by prohibitions but are left to practice their beliefs, so their trade in wine and pigs is prohibited yet unenforced.

Ruling on Dhimmis Trading Wine and Pork and Then Converting
Examines the effect of conversion to Islam on a prior sale of wine or pork between Dhimmis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of conversion to Islam on a prior sale or loan of wine/pork between Dhimmis, distinguish between the cases before and after receipt, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on the loan scenario.

Core Idea
A Muslim cannot deliver a haram item — conversion breaks the contract before qabd, but leaves it intact after.

When two non-Muslims (Dhimmis) engage in a contract involving wine or pork—items that are legally prohibited for Muslims—and then one or both convert to Islam, what happens to the contract? This lesson explores the Hanafi position, which hinges on the timing of the conversion relative to physical receipt (qabd). We also examine an intra-madhhab dispute when the contract is a loan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type and item
Determine whether the transaction is a sale or a loan (qard), and whether the subject matter is wine or pork. This affects the applicability of the ruling.
The ruling for sale is explicit; the loan case involves an additional ikhtilaf.
2
Check the timing of conversion relative to qabd
If conversion occurs before the buyer takes physical possession (qabd), the contract is rescinded because delivery of a haram item is impermissible for a Muslim. If after qabd, the contract stands as executed.
Qabd is the key condition that determines validity after conversion.
3
Apply the rule to the sale scenario
For a sale: if both or one convert before qabd, the sale is rescinded (فسخ). If after qabd, the sale remains valid.
The reasoning is that the prohibition of delivery and receipt (tahrim al-taslim wa al-qabd) nullifies the pending contract.
4
Analyze the loan of wine case
For a loan (qard) of wine: if the lender converts, the wine debt is dropped because the lender cannot receive it. If the borrower converts, there are two narrations—one drops the debt, Muhammad's view requires payment of value.
The difference hinges on whether the borrower's conversion creates a new obligation or merely extinguishes the old one.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the basis of each narration. The preferred position (mufta bihi) is that the wine debt is dropped, following the analogy to the lender's case.
This ikhtilaf demonstrates the scope of valid scholarly difference within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار المؤلف إلى أن الذميين إذا تبايعا خمراً أو خنزيراً ثم أسلما أو أسلم أحدهما قبل القبض فإن البيع يفسخ لأن التسليم والقبض حرام كالبيع بخلاف ما إذا كان الإسلام بعد القبض لأن الموجود الدوام وهو لا ينافي ولو أقرض الذمي خمراً من ذمي ثم أسلم أحدهما، فإن أسلم المقرض سقطت الخمر لأن إسلامه مانع من قبضها ولا شيء له من قيمتها على المستقرض لأن العجز جاء من قبله وإن أسلم المستقرض ففيه روايتان : في رواية كالأول وفي أخرى وهو قول محمد تجب قيمتها ؛ كذا في البدائع.
And the author indicated that when two Dhimmis sell wine or pork to each other and then both become Muslim, or one becomes Muslim before receipt, the sale is rescinded because delivery and receipt are prohibited like the sale. As opposed to if Islam occurs after receipt, because what exists is continuation and it is not contradictory. And if a Dhimmi lends wine to a Dhimmi and then one becomes Muslim, if the lender becomes Muslim, the wine is dropped because his Islam prevents its receipt and he has nothing of its value from the borrower because the inability came from his side. If the borrower becomes Muslim, there are two narrations: one like the first, and in another—which is the statement of Muhammad—its value is owed. This is in Al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: two Dhimmis trade wine or pork, then one or both convert to Islam. If conversion happens before physical receipt (qabd), the sale is void because both delivery and receipt are haram. If conversion occurs after receipt, the sale remains valid as the contract is already executed. For a loan of wine (qard), if the lender converts, he extinguishes the wine debt; the borrower owes nothing because the lender's conversion prevents him from receiving it. If the borrower converts, there is a difference: one narration says it is extinguished like the first; Muhammad's view says the borrower must pay the value. The authority weight is from Bada'i'.

Key Terms
القبض (al-qabd): Physical or constructive taking possession of the sold item.
الخمر (al-khamr): Wine or intoxicating beverages; najis and haram for Muslims.
الخنزير (al-khinzir): Pork or pig meat; najis and haram for Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two Dhimmis trade wine, then both convert to Islam before delivery. What is the ruling?
A) The sale is valid and delivery must be made.
B) The sale is void and must be rescinded.
C) The sale is suspended until they reconsider.
D) They may keep the wine as an amanah.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a contract involving haram items after conversion depends on whether receipt (qabd) occurred before conversion; if before, the contract stands; if after, it is rescinded.

Selling Musical Instruments: Valid but Disliked
Ruling with proof for intra-madhhab dispute on selling instruments of amusement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling musical instruments and backgammon, distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions, and explain how the guarantee (daman) applies.

Core Idea
Even if an item is commonly used for sin, its sale may be valid if there is a potential lawful use.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the sale of musical instruments and backgammon, highlighting an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The ruling is not simply 'invalid because prohibited' but considers the possibility of lawful utility, reflecting the Hanafi emphasis on intended use.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested items
The passage mentions specific musical instruments (lute, drum, flute, tambourine) and backgammon (al-nard). These are items commonly associated with amusement and potential sin.
The same ruling applies to chess (al-shatranj) as mentioned in the text.
2
Note Abu Hanifa's ruling
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the sale of these items is valid (the contract is effective) but makruh (disliked). He argues that there is a possible lawful benefit (e.g., using a drum for announcements).
The hukm level: the contract is sahih, but the transaction is makruh tanzihan.
3
Note the Sahibayn's ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the sale is invalid (does not take effect). They focus on the predominant sinful use, considering the items non-property.
Their view makes the sale batil (void).
4
Understand the proof and tarjih
The correct opinion (al-sahih) is Abu Hanifa's due to the possibility of lawful benefit. The text states: 'for the possibility of lawful benefit from another aspect' (للانتفاع بها شرعاً من وجه آخر).
This is an example of istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas.
5
Apply the same ikhtilaf to guarantee (daman)
If someone destroys these items, Abu Hanifa says the destroyer must guarantee (pay compensation); the Sahibayn say no guarantee. The fatwa for guarantee follows the Sahibayn, except when the destroyer is a judge or hisbah officer — then no guarantee by consensus.
The ruler's agents act for public good and are exempt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالخمر والخنزير لأن بيع آلات اللهو كالبربط والطبل والمزمار والدف صحيح مكروه عند الإمام. وقالا : لا ينعقد بيعها والصحيح قوله للانتفاع بها شرعاً من وجه آخر. وعلى هذا الاختلاف بيع والشطرنج، وعلى هذا الاختلاف الضمان على من أتلفها فعنده يضمن وعندهما لا؛ كذا في البدائع. ولكن الفتوى في الضمان على قولهما كما سيأتي في الغصب ومحله ما إذا كسرها غير القاضي والمحتسب أما هما فلا ضمان اتفاقاً، وقد ذكر في أول سير اليتيمة الفرق بين المتقوم والمعصوم اهـ.
And he restricted to wine and pork because the sale of instruments of amusement like the lute, drum, flute, and tambourine is valid but disliked according to Imam. And they (the two companions) said: their sale does not take effect. And the correct statement is his, due to the possibility of lawful benefit from another aspect. And on this difference, the sale of chess and backgammon, and on this difference, the guarantee (daman) for one who destroys them: according to him he guarantees, according to them he does not. This is in Al-Bada'i. But the fatwa regarding guarantee is according to their statement, as will come in Ghasp. And its scope is when someone other than the judge and the hisbah officer breaks them; as for those two, there is no guarantee by agreement. And it was mentioned in the beginning of Sayr al-Yatimah the difference between mutaqawwim and ma'soom.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale of musical instruments and games like backgammon and chess. According to Abu Hanifa, selling such items is valid (the contract is effective) but makruh (disliked). The Sahibayn say the sale is invalid. The correct opinion is Abu Hanifa's, because these items can have lawful uses (e.g., a drum for announcements). The same ikhtilaf applies to the guarantee (daman) if someone destroys them: Abu Hanifa holds the destroyer liable, the Sahibayn do not. The fatwa for guarantee follows the Sahibayn, except if the destroyer is a judge or hisbah officer, in which case there is no guarantee by consensus. The authority weight is from Bada'i' and references to other works.

Key Terms
آلات اللهو (alat al-lahw): Instruments of amusement, specifically musical instruments like lute, drum, flute, tambourine.
النرد (al-nard): Backgammon, a game of chance.
مكروه (makruh): Disliked; an act that is not haram but should be avoided.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; liability to compensate for damage or loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the sale of items with potential lawful use is valid, even if commonly misused, but it remains disliked; the same ikhtilaf applies to guarantee for destruction, with an exception for judicial and hisbah officers.

Liability in Combined Sale of Slave with Mudabbar/Umm Walad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on combined sale liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the sale of a mudabbar and umm walad together with a slave, explain the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding liability, and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
The mudabbar and umm walad, though included in a sale, are not fully owned by the buyer, leading to a dispute on liability if they perish.

This passage tackles a thorny intra-Hanafi dispute: when a slave is sold together with a mudabbar or umm walad as a single package, what happens if the mudabbar or umm walad dies in the buyer's hands? Is the buyer liable to pay their value? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples disagree on the reasoning, and the answer affects the practical outcome in courts. Let us walk through the reasoning of each side and the relied-upon fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
The act is a combined sale (صفقة) of a slave (قن) together with a mudabbar or umm walad. The disputed ruling is whether the buyer must guarantee (ضمان) the value of the mudabbar or umm walad if they perish in his hands.
Both sides agree the sale of the slave itself is valid; the dispute is only about the accompanying item.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and its basis
Imam Abu Hanifa (in one riwaya) holds that the buyer is not liable. His reasoning: the mudabbar and umm walad do not accept the reality of sale (they cannot be sold independently), so the sale regarding them is void (batil). The buyer holds them as a trust (amana) with the owner's permission, hence no guarantee.
This is based on a riwaya chosen by Ahmad al-Tusi.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their basis
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the buyer is liable for their value. Their reasoning: these items are included in the sale to enable the transaction; the buyer takes possession by way of sale, so they become guaranteed like other properties. There is also a riwaya from Imam Abu Hanifa agreeing with them.
This view is supported by Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi and others.
4
Analyse the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If the mudabbar or umm walad dies in the buyer's hands, according to Imam Abu Hanifa (first riwaya) the buyer pays nothing; according to the Sahibayn (and the other riwaya from Imam) the buyer must pay the value. The mufta bihi position for the mudabbar is half his value as a slave; for the umm walad it is one third her value.
The fatwa on the umm walad is less settled; some mashayikh say she is not guaranteed at all.
5
Distinguish the mukatab from the mudabbar and umm walad
The mukatab is different: he possesses himself (في يد نفسه), so the concept of buyer's possession (qabd) does not apply to him. Therefore, all agree there is no guarantee for the mukatab if he perishes. The dispute is only about the mudabbar and umm walad.
This explains why the passage mentions the mukatab but the liability debate centres on the other two.

The buyer does not guarantee, and (the sale of) fish before catching, birds in the air, the unborn, the offspring, and milk in the udder. What of validity? Al-Khaniyya corrected (the text). It was objected that if the sale of them was void, the voidness would spread to what is combined with it. It will come that if he combined a slave and a mudabbar or an umm walad and sold them as a bundle, then it is permissible for the slave. If they were like a free person, it would not be permissible in what is combined. It was answered that it is specific, so it is possible that some individuals of the void, due to its weakness, do not have their effect spread to what is combined with it. In some expressions of the mashayikh, their sale is fasid based on the validity of the combined item. It was objected that if it were fasid, they would own by taking possession, but they do not own by it by agreement. It was answered that it is specific, so it is of the type of fasid that does not allow ownership. The conclusion is that they agreed that they do not own by it and on the invalidity of voidness in the combined items. So it remains that their sale is either batil or fasid, and specification is necessary for each. Specifying the statement of al-Hidayah is better. The benefit of the two sayings is regarding what is combined with them: it is batil according to al-Hidayah, so they do not own by possession; and fasid according to al-Quduri and al-Idah, so they own by it. This is what the commentators have conveyed in this place. In Idah al-Islah, the sale of the three is batil, suspended, turning into permissible by consent for the mukatab and by court order for the latter two due to the existence of financial value. This is weak because for the mukatab, consent is necessary before the sale according to the correct view. The effect of the court order for the umm walad is weak. In Qada al-Bazzaziyya: the most apparent is that it is not sold. In Fath al-Qadir: effect by court order. The sale of a partial free person is like a free person, and the child of a mudabbar is like him, likewise the child of an umm walad, and the mukatab is like them due to the inclusion of the child in the kitaba. This is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. His statement: (If they perish in the buyer's hands, he does not guarantee because the sale is void, so it is an amana because it is taken with the owner's permission. This is a riwaya from the Imam, chosen by Ahmad al-Tusi. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi and others chose guarantee by similar or value. It was said the first is his statement and the second is the statement of the two. This is in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Quniyya and al-Siyar: he guarantees because he took it for himself, so it resembles usurpation, and that is correct. At the beginning of Sir al-Yatimah, the issue of a Harbi selling his son or father was mentioned: whether it is batil or fasid. He made it general, so it includes all that preceded. But if the mudabbar and umm walad die in the buyer's hands, there is disagreement: the Imam said no guarantee; the two said he owes their value, and this is a riwaya from him because it is taken by way of sale, so it becomes guaranteed like other properties. This is because the mudabbar and umm walad are included in the sale such that what is combined with them in the sale is owned, unlike the mukatab, because he is in his own hands, so possession is not actualized in his case, and he is not guaranteed. He has the argument that the aspect of sale attaches to its reality in a place that accepts reality, and they (mudabbar and umm walad) do not accept the reality of sale, so they become like the mukatab. Their inclusion in the sale is not in respect of themselves, but only to establish the ruling of sale regarding what is combined with them. So the buyer's money does not enter into the ruling of his contract alone, but rather the ruling of inclusion is established in what he combined with it. This is in al-Hidayah. Al-Bazzazi: the sale of one who intentionally omits naming the price from a disbeliever is not permissible. There is discussion about this in the chapter of judiciary, if Allah wills. His statement: (So the buyer's money becomes) In al-Fath: so the buyer's money does not enter into the ruling of his contract alone, but enters when the seller combines his own money with it and sells both to him as one bundle, where the sale is valid for the combined item with its share. It is apparent that there is no guarantee if the mukatab perishes in the buyer's hands by agreement, and the statement of al-Inaya points to this. In al-Mi'raj: the riwaya from him like the two is only regarding the mudabbar; as for the umm walad, she is not guaranteed according to him by agreement of the riwayat. In Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadikhan, our mashayikh considered this riwaya to be correct. We mentioned in the chapter of manumission that the value of a mudabbar is half his value if he were a slave, and that is the fatwa; and the value of an umm walad is one third of her value as a slave. If it is necessary to evaluate them considering what is combined with them, the matter is as we mentioned. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: here the value of a mudabbar is two-thirds of his value as a slave according to the correct view, and that is the fatwa. What we mentioned of the fatwa with half is transmitted in al-Fatawa al-Sughra, and al-Binaya and Fath al-Qadir explicitly stated it here. Know that the umm walad differs from the mudabbar in thirteen rulings: she is not guaranteed by usurpation, nor by manumission, nor by sale; she does not work for a creditor, and she is freed from the estate. If he gives birth to an umm walad from a shared female slave, he does not own his partner's share, and her value is one third. The court order for permissibility of selling her is not effective, and she must observe idda upon the master's death or manumission. The lineage of her child is established without the need for acknowledgement. Her tadbir is not valid. The tadbir of a mudabbar female slave is valid. The Harbi cannot sell his umm walad but can sell his mudabbar. The istilad of the female slave of his son is valid but not her tadbir. Likewise it is correct. All in al-Talqih. He threw it.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity and liability of a sale that combines a slave with a mukatab, mudabbar, or umm walad in one transaction. The text begins with the matn stating that the buyer does not guarantee for these items because the sale is void. The commentary then discusses a specific case: if someone sells a slave together with one of these three (mukatab, mudabbar, umm walad), what is the ruling? There are two main opinions: according to al-Hidayah, the sale of these three is batil (void), so the buyer does not own them by possession and is not liable if they perish. According to al-Quduri and al-Idah, it is fasid (invalid) so the buyer does own them by possession and is liable. The text then delves into the liability (daman) issue. If the items perish in the buyer's hands, Imam Abu Hanifa says no liability; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say he must pay the value for the mudabbar and umm walad, but there is a riwaya from Abu Hanifa agreeing with them. The discussion explains that the mudabbar and umm walad are included in the sale in the sense that they allow the combined item to be owned, but they themselves are not subject to the sale's reality because they are like a free person in some respects. The mukatab is different because he possesses himself. The passage concludes with a list of thirteen differences between an umm walad and a mudabbar, highlighting that they are distinct legal categories.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātib): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying a sum of money in installments.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death through a declaration called tadbir.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child from her master.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability, guarantee, or obligation to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi ruling in the passage, how much value must the buyer pay if an umm walad sold together with a slave perishes in his hands?
A) Nothing
B) Half her value as a slave
C) One third of her value as a slave
D) Full market value

Key Takeaway
The liability for a mudabbar or umm walad sold together with a slave is disputed within the Hanafi school; the mufta bihi is half value for the mudabbar and one third for the umm walad.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
