Ruling on Selling Fish Before Catching
Explaining the prohibition of selling fish before catching with hadith proof and details on ownership conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for selling fish before catching, explain the reason (gharar and lack of ownership), and apply the conditions for ownership based on the enclosure type.

Core Idea
You cannot sell what you do not own — fish in water are unowned until caught.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author explains a fundamental rule of sales: one cannot sell a commodity that is not owned or deliverable. The specific example of fish in water illustrates the principles of ownership and gharar. The hadith 'Do not buy fish in water, for it is gharar' provides the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
Determine the ruling on selling fish before catching. Understand that it is not permissible because the seller does not own the fish (making the sale batil) or because the fish are not deliverable (making it fasid).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith cited from Musnad Ahmad: 'Do not buy fish in water, for it is gharar.' Recognize that the prohibition is due to gharar (deception/uncertainty).
The hadith is ahad but used as evidence.
3
Analyze the conditions for ownership
Examine when the seller owns fish in an enclosure (hazira). Note that if fish enter by the seller's contrivance (hila), he owns them; if they enter by themselves, he does not. Also note the consensus if he prepared the enclosure.
The size of the enclosure affects deliverability.
4
Distinguish between large and small enclosures
If the enclosure is large and fish cannot be caught without effort, the sale is fasid due to non-deliverability. If small, the sale is valid with the option of sight after delivery.
Sight in water is not considered; sight after delivery is required.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Understand the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether fish entering an enclosure by themselves are owned. Some say no ownership at all, others say ownership depends on preparation.
The mufta bihi is that if the seller prepares the enclosure, he owns by consensus; if not, he does not own.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والسمك قبل الصيد أي لم يجز بيعه لكونه باع ما لا يملكه فيكون باطلاً. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان في حظيرة إذا كان لا يؤخذ إلا بصيد لكونه غير مقدور التسليم فيكون فاسداً، ومعناه إذا أخذه ثم ألقاه فيها ولو كان يأخذ بغير حيلة جاز إلا إذا اجتمعت فيها بأنفسها ولم يسد عليها المدخل لعدم الملك، وروى الإمام أحمد مرفوعاً «لا تشتروا السمك في الماء فإنه غرور (۱) والحاصل أن عدم جوازه قبل أخذه لعدم ملكه له فإن أخذه ثم في حظيرة كبيرة فعدم جوازه لكونه غير مقدور التسليم فإن سلمه بعد ذلك فكالروايتين في الآبق إذا سلمه، وإن كانت صغيرة جاز وله خيار الرؤية بعد التسليم، ولا اعتبار برؤيته في الماء، وإذا دخل السمك الحظيرة باحتياله ملكه وكان له بيعه على التفصيل، وقيل لا مطلقاً لعدم الإحراز، والخلاف فيما إذا لم يهيئها له فإن هيأها له ملكه إجماعاً، فإن اجتمع بغير صنعه لم يملكه، سواء أمكنه أخذه من غير حيلة أو لا. وفي القاموس: الحظيرة جرين التمر والمحيط بالشيء خشباً وقصباً .اهـ. وفسرها في البناية بالحوض والبركة. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا باعه في نهر أو بحر أو أجمة، وقد صرح الإمام أبو يوسف في كتاب الخراج يمنعه إذا كان في
بيع
من الثمن المسمى على الأصح وإن كان قد قيل لا يصح أصلاً في شيء .اهـ. قلت: فلتحفظ هذه المسألة فإنها تقع كثيراً في نحو المال المشترك بين رجلين مثلاً كدابة أو دار فإن أحدهما لشريكه بصفقة واحدة وقد بحثت عنها كثيراً حتى وجدتها هنا قوله : (جرين التمر) أجرن التمر

الكل
His statement: (And fish before catching) meaning it is not permissible to sell it because he is selling what he does not own, so it is void. He made it general so it includes when it is in a hazira (enclosure) if it cannot be taken except by catching, because it is not deliverable, so it is fasid. Its meaning is if he caught it and then threw it into it, and if he could take it without trickery, it is permissible unless they gathered by themselves without blocking the entrance, due to lack of ownership. Imam Ahmad narrated in marfu' form: 'Do not buy fish in water, for it is gharar (deception).' The conclusion is that its non-permissibility before catching is due to lack of ownership. If he caught it and then (put it) in a large hazira, its non-permissibility is due to non-deliverability. If he delivers it afterwards, it is like the two riwayat regarding the absconding slave if he delivers him. If the hazira is small, it is permissible, and he has the option of sight after delivery, and sight in water is not considered. If the fish enter the hazira by his contrivance, he owns them, and he may sell them according to the details. It was said not at all due to lack of acquisition. The disagreement is when he did not prepare it for him; if he prepared it, he owns it by consensus. If they gather without his action, he does not own them, whether he can take them without trickery or not. In al-Qamus: al-hazira is the date store and the enclosure around something made of wood and reeds. It is interpreted in al-Binaya as a basin or pool. He made it general so it includes if he sells in a river, sea, or thicket. Imam Abu Yusuf explicitly stated in Kitab al-Kharaj that he prohibits it when it is in a sale for the stipulated price according to the correct view, although it was said that it is not valid at all in anything. I (the commentator) say: Let this issue be memorized, for it occurs frequently in the case of joint property between two men, like an animal or a house, where one of them sells the whole to his partner in one bundle. I have researched it extensively until I found it here. His statement: (date store) 'ajrin al-tamr (date store). (1) Ahmad narrated in his Musnad (1/288). He sells the whole.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the sale of fish before catching. It explains that selling fish while still in the water is not permissible because the seller does not own it, making the sale void (batil). If the fish is in an enclosure (hazira) that requires effort to catch, the sale is invalid (fasid) due to inability to deliver. The text cites a hadith from Imam Ahmad prohibiting buying fish in water due to gharar (deception). It then details the conditions for ownership: if the fish enter the enclosure by the seller's contrivance, he owns them; if by themselves, he does not. The size of the enclosure matters: if small, the sale is permissible with an option of sight. The passage also discusses a related issue of selling a share of joint property, but that appears to be a marginal note. The overall teaching is that fish are not owned until caught, and any sale before that is invalid.

Key Terms
سمك (samak): Fish, as a commodity that is not owned until caught.
حظيرة (ḥaẓīra): An enclosure, such as a basin or pond, used to trap fish.
غرر (gharar): Deception, risk, or uncertainty in a contract, which renders it invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason for the prohibition of selling fish before catching, according to the hadith cited in the text?
A) Because the fish is not halal
B) Because it involves gharar (deception)
C) Because the buyer cannot see the fish
D) Because the fish may die before delivery

Key Takeaway
The sale of fish before catching is invalid due to lack of ownership or gharar; ownership is only established by actual capture or contrived enclosure entry.

Valuation of Mudabbar and Umm Walad in Invalid Sales
Explaining liability and valuation differences between mudabbar and umm walad when sold invalidly

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over the valuation of a mudabbar in an invalid sale and state the mufta bihi for both mudabbar and umm walad.

Core Idea
The mudabbar is valued at half, the umm walad at one-third — the higher status of the umm walad yields less liability.

In Hanafi fiqh, certain slaves have partial freedom due to their master's actions: the mudabbar (to be freed upon the master's death) and the umm walad (mother of the master's child). When such slaves are sold in an invalid transaction and then perish, their valuation for liability differs. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the dispute within the Hanafi school over the mudabbar's value and the agreed-upon valuation for the umm walad, along with a list of thirteen legal distinctions between them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage discusses two types of slaves: mudabbar (whom the master has declared free upon his death) and umm walad (a slave concubine who has borne a child to her master). Understand their definitions and partial freedom status.
2
Note the valuation for liability
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi), if these slaves are sold in an invalid sale and then destroyed in the buyer's hands, the mudabbar is valued at half of what he would be worth as a regular slave, while the umm walad is valued at one-third.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute over mudabbar
There are two transmissions from Abu Hanifa: one valuing the mudabbar at half, another at one-third. The passage states that the mashayikh (later scholars) authenticated the half narration, but al-Siraj al-Wahhaj considers one-third as the soundest and the fatwa. Identify the conflicting views and their sources.
4
Understand the mufta bihi and tarjih
Qadikhan and the mashayikh authenticated the half narration for the mudabbar. The author of al-Binayah and Fath al-Qadir support the half. Therefore, the fatwa is that the mudabbar is valued at half and the umm walad at one-third. Know the tarjih authority.
5
Distinguish the thirteen rulings
The passage lists thirteen legal differences between the umm walad and the mudabbar. Note key distinctions: the umm walad is not liable for ghasb (usurpation), cannot be sold, is freed from the estate, and her tadbir (contract of freedom upon death) is invalid, while the mudabbar's tadbir is valid. Recognize that these differences stem from the higher status of the umm walad.

And apparently, there is no liability if the mukatib perishes in the buyer's hand by agreement, and the statement of al-Inayah refers to that. In al-Mi'raj: the transmission from him (Abu Hanifa) is like their statement (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) only concerning the mudabbar, but as for the umm walad, she is not liable according to him by agreement of the transmissions. In Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadikhan, and our scholars (mashayikh) have authenticated this transmission. And we have previously stated in the chapter of emancipation (al-Itaq) that the value of a mudabbar is half of his value if he were a slave, and that is the fatwa, and that the value of an umm walad is one-third of her value as a slave. So when there is need to appraise them considering what is added to them, the matter is as we mentioned. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: here that the value of a mudabbar is one-third of his value as a slave according to the soundest opinion, and the fatwa is upon it. And what we have mentioned of the fatwa with half is transmitted in al-Fatawa al-Sughra, and it is explicitly stated in al-Binayah and Fath al-Qadir here. Know that the umm walad differs from the mudabbar in thirteen rulings: she is not liable for usurpation (ghasb), nor for manumission, nor for sale, nor does she work for a creditor, and she is freed from the estate, and if he impregnates a jointly owned umm walad, he does not own the share of his partner, and her value is one-third, and the judge's decision to permit her sale does not take effect, and she must observe the waiting period (iddah) at the death of the master or his manumission, and the lineage of her child is established without claim, and her tadbir (contract of tadbir) is not valid; while tadbir of a mudabbar is valid, and a harbi (non-Muslim from warring lands) cannot sell his umm walad but can sell his mudabbar, and it is valid to impregnate the slave-girl of his son but not her tadbir. All this is correct. In al-Talqih: he cast (this).
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on a specific ruling within the chapter of invalid sales. It addresses the liability when a mudabbar (a slave whose master has declared he will be free upon the master's death) or an umm walad (a slave concubine who has borne a child to her master) is sold in an invalid transaction and then perishes. The passage clarifies the differences in valuation and legal rulings between these two categories of slaves, highlighting the intra-Hanafi dispute and the final relied-upon position (mufta bihi). The key point is that the mudabbar is valued at half his price as a slave, while the umm walad is valued at one-third, based on transmitted rulings and consensus of the later scholars. The passage also lists thirteen legal distinctions between them, emphasizing the higher legal protection given to the umm walad due to her status as mother of her master's child.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whom the master has declared will be freed upon the master's death.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, with the master acknowledging paternity.
ضمان (daman): Liability for compensation if the property perishes in the buyer's hands under an invalid sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When sold invalidly and destroyed, the mudabbar is valued at half and the umm walad at one-third of their value as regular slaves, with the half for the mudabbar being the mufta bihi authenticated by Qadikhan and the mashayikh.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Selling Uncaptured Fish: The Basic Prohibition and Proof
Explaining the invalidity of selling uncaptured fish and conditions for valid sales in enclosures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling uncaptured fish, explain the hadith used as evidence, and distinguish between batil and fasid in different enclosure scenarios.

Core Idea
Selling uncaptured fish is invalid due to gharar; but the ruling changes when fish are in an enclosure and capable of delivery.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position on selling fish while they are still in water. The general rule is that selling something not owned or not capable of delivery is prohibited due to gharar (deception). However, the school provides nuanced distinctions based on the size of enclosures and ease of capture. We will examine the primary evidence from the hadith and apply it to various scenarios such as enclosures (hazirah), rivers, and thickets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule and Its Basis
The passage begins by stating that selling fish before catching is invalid (batil) because the seller does not own them. Then it introduces the ruling for fish in an enclosure: if they cannot be taken except by catching, the sale is voidable (fasid) due to inability to deliver.
Note the distinction: batil vs fasid – batil is void ab initio; fasid is voidable and can be cured if the defect is removed.
2
Examine the Hadith and Its Legal Implication
The author cites the hadith 'Do not buy fish in water, for it is deception (gharar).' This is a primary textual evidence. Identify its thubut (zanni) and dalala (zanni) and how the prohibition indicates invalidity.
The hadith uses the imperative 'la tashtaru' which in Hanafi usul indicates prohibition (tahrim).
3
Differentiate Between Large and Small Enclosures
If the fish are in a large enclosure (hazirah) where catching is difficult, the sale is fasid. If in a small enclosure where fish can be easily caught, the sale is valid but with the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) after delivery.
The size of the enclosure determines capability of delivery, which is a condition for validity.
4
Understand Ownership and Delivery Conditions
Ownership of fish is established if they enter a prepared enclosure. If they gather spontaneously without preparation, the seller does not own them. Delivery after sale in a large enclosure does not necessarily cure the defect – analogy to a runaway slave (abiq).
This intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is compared to two narrations on abiq: some say delivery validates, others say it does not.
5
Apply to Common Property and Fatwa on Leasing
The passage extends the principle to practical issues such as selling a share of common property (e.g., a house or animal) in one bundle, and the fatwa about leasing a lake for fishing. The overall conclusion is that sale of fish in open water (river, sea, thicket) is absolutely impermissible unless in a specially prepared small enclosure.
The fatwa section shows how the ruling is applied to similar questions (e.g., leasing a pond in waqf land).

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling on selling fish before they are caught. The general rule is that selling something not owned is invalid (batil). However, the author expands on this by considering different scenarios. First, if the fish are in a large enclosure (hazirah) where they cannot be easily caught, the sale is voidable (fasid) due to inability to deliver. If the fish are in a small enclosure where they can be easily caught, the sale is valid, but the buyer has an option of sight after delivery because seeing fish in water is not considered valid inspection. The author then discusses ownership: if the fish enter an enclosure that the owner prepared for them, he owns them by consensus. If they gather without his preparation, he does not own them. The passage also addresses selling fish in rivers, seas, and thickets, citing Abu Yusuf's prohibition from Kitab al-Kharaj, and then uses this principle to resolve a common issue of selling a share of common property as a single bundle. Finally, the author relates a fatwa about leasing a lake in waqf for fishing, concluding that the sale of fish in such areas is impermissible unless in specially prepared enclosures where the fish are capable of delivery. The key takeaway is that the permissibility of selling fish depends on ownership and capability of delivery at the time of contract.

Key Terms
حظيرة (hazirah): An enclosure for fish, such as a basin or pond, often made of wood or reeds.
غرور (gharar): Deception; a prohibited uncertainty in transactions.
أجمة (ajamah): A thicket or dense growth of trees where fish may be found.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is valid with the option of sight after delivery
C) It is valid only if the seller catches them first
D) It is prohibited (haram) due to gharar

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits selling uncaptured fish due to gharar, but allows it when the fish are in a small enclosure and capable of delivery, subject to the option of sight.

Selling Fish in Lakes: Between Permissibility and Prohibition
Ruling on selling fish in lakes with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fish in lakes and evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute using the narration from Umar.

Core Idea
Fish in a lake are not owned until caught; selling uncaptured fish is impermissible except in treasury or endowment lands under a permissive view.

This passage addresses a practical question from the author's time: may an endowment pond be leased for fishing? The author traces the issue through a rare narration from Umar ibn al-Khattab reported by Imam Abu Yusuf, and contrasts it with a later strict view in al-Iydah. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi muftis weigh narrations from the early Caliphs against general legal principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central act and its default ruling
The passage begins by clarifying that fish taken by hand without hunting can be sold. This implies that uncaptured fish in a lake are not mawjud (present and deliverable), so the default ruling is impermissibility of sale due to gharar and lack of ownership.
Al-ajam are natural lakes; fish in them are not owned until caught.
2
Locate the dalil cited for an exceptional permission
The author found a narration in Abu Yusuf's Kitab al-Kharaj: Umar ibn al-Khattab permitted leasing a lake for fishing. The imperative 'af'ilu' (do it) suggests permissibility, but it applies to leasing the place, not selling the fish directly.
Dalil type: sunnah ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah and its limitations
The narration only allows leasing the lake, which implies that the fish become owned by the lessee after catching. It does not permit selling uncaptured fish. The author uses this to allow leasing for treasury and endowment lands by analogy.
The analogy (qiyas) extends the ruling from treasury land to waqf.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Later, the author found in al-Iydah an absolute prohibition on leasing such ponds. This view aligns with the general principle that one cannot sell or lease what is not in one's possession or not yet owned.
The dispute is between permissibility based on Umar's practice (supported by Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa) and the stricter rule preventing any contract involving uncaptured fish.
5
Determine the operative ruling (mufta bihi)
The author leans toward permissibility for lands of the treasury (bayt al-mal) and by extension for waqf, but he records the contrary opinion. Later Hanafi scholars like al-Ramli consider the view of al-Iydah as more sound, making the ruling restricted.
This reflects the tension between textual evidence from a Caliph and the systematic principles of the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The lakes (ajam), and if fish is taken by hand without being caught (by hunting), there is no harm in selling it. The end. Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the sale of fish in lakes (ajam) and the leasing of ponds for fishing. The author first clarifies that fish taken by hand without being hunted may be sold. He then discusses related rulings from Fath al-Qadir about pits and nets for hunting. The main issue: the author was asked about leasing a pond in an endowment for fishing. He found in Abu Yusuf's Kitab al-Kharaj a narration that Umar ibn al-Khattab allowed leasing a lake in Iraq for fishing. He also found that Abu Hanifa reported that Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz permitted selling the catch of lakes. Based on these, the author concludes that selling fish in lakes is not permitted unless the land belongs to the public treasury (bayt al-mal), and by analogy, endowment land. However, he later found in al-Iydah that leasing such ponds is absolutely impermissible. The passage thus presents a difference of opinion, with the author initially supporting permissibility under certain conditions but noting a stricter later view.

Key Terms
الآجام (al-ajam): Dense vegetation or thicket where fish gather; used for lakes or ponds where fish are found without being caught.
البحيرة (al-buhaira): A pond or small lake, especially one where fish are found.
التهيئة (al-tahyi'a): Preparation; the act of setting up a trap or place with the intention to catch game, which confers ownership upon the fall of the game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Absolutely prohibited
B) Permissible
C) Permissible only if caught first
D) Permissible but disliked

Key Takeaway
Selling uncaptured fish in lakes is generally impermissible, but the Hanafi school has an intra-madhhab dispute on leasing ponds, with a permissive view based on Umar's practice and a strict view based on legal principles.

Ruling on Selling Birds in the Air
Ruling on selling birds in the air with exception for domesticated birds

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling birds in the air and explain the exception for domesticated birds using the concept of capability of delivery (maqdur al-taslim).

Core Idea
A bird in the air cannot be sold unless it habitually returns — capability of delivery is measured by custom.

This passage addresses a common transaction: selling a bird that is not in hand. The default ruling is that such a sale is invalid (batil or fasid) because the object is either unowned or incapable of delivery. However, Hanafi scholars recognized an exception for domesticated birds that habitually return, based on the principle that customary return constitutes practical delivery capability. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the intra-madhhab dispute, and the qiyas reasoning that supports the exception.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling and its basis
Identify that selling a bird in the air (uncaptured) is batil because it is not owned at the time of contract. Selling a bird after releasing it from hand is fasid because it is not capable of delivery.
Batil means void ab initio; fasid means voidable — can be corrected only if delivery becomes possible later? There is disagreement.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
According to Mashayikh Balkh, even if the seller later delivers the bird, the contract does not become valid. According to al-Karkhi, subsequent delivery renders it valid. This difference stems from whether the defect of incapacity can be cured after the contract.
The source mentions 'على قول الكرخي يعود' meaning the sale becomes valid upon delivery.
3
Apply the exception for domesticated birds
If the bird is dajin (domesticated) and habitually returns to its home and can be taken without difficulty, the sale is permissible. This is supported by Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Hidayah.
The key phrase: 'إن كان داجناً يعود إلى بيته ويقدر على أخذه بلا تكلف جاز بيعه'.
4
Understand the qiyas reasoning
The ruling is based on qiyas to the sale of an escaped slave (abiq): both involve incapacity of delivery at contract time. The exception for domesticated birds is justified by custom (urf) considering the bird as effectively capable of delivery because its return is almost certain.
The illah is incapacity of delivery; custom can override the usual requirement when return is habitual.
5
Note the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position in the later Hanafi school is that domesticated birds that habitually return can be sold. This is found in Fatwa Qadikhan, al-Hidayah, and al-Dhakhirah.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah explicitly allows selling pigeons if their return is known.

His saying: (And birds in the air) meaning it is not permissible because it is not owned before taking, so it is void. Similarly, if he sells it after releasing it from his hand, because it is not capable of delivery, so it is voidable (fasid). And if he delivers it afterwards, it does not become permissible according to the scholars of Balkh; according to al-Karkhi, it returns. And similarly in al-Tahawi. He mentioned it absolutely, so it includes whether the bird is made the object of sale or the price, and includes whether it is its habit to go and come, which is the apparent view. Al-Ramli said: I say, what is known from the above is the impermissibility of the sale absolutely, whether in sea, river, or lake, and its absoluteness is more general whether in the land of the treasury or the land of the endowment. And what came earlier from Kitab al-Kharaj of Abu Yusuf is also not far from the principles, and its reference is to the permission of a specific place for a known benefit, which is fishing. And what Abu Hanifa narrated from Hammad is problematic, for it is the sale of fish before hunting. It is answered that it was in lakes prepared for that, and the fish were capable of delivery. So reflect and pay attention to this analysis, for the issue occurs frequently and questions about it are many. His saying: (and it is apparent) meaning the apparent of the narration, as such. And in Fatawa Qadikhan: If he sells a bird that can fly, if it is domesticated and returns to its home and can be taken without difficulty, then its sale is permissible; otherwise no. And the saying of the author of al-Hidayah: Pigeons, if their return is known and delivery is possible, their sale is permissible because they are capable of delivery. And he agreed, and stated it in al-Dhakhirah attributed to al-Muntaqa. And in al-Mi'raj: He sold a horse in an enclosure; the seller said 'I have delivered it to you', then the buyer opened it and the horse went away. If he can take it by hand without help, it is delivery; otherwise no, because if he stretched out his hand he could not take it. End. And in al-Qamus: al-tayr is the plural of tair, and it may be used for the singular and plural; plural tuyur and atyar; al-tayaran (with movement) is the movement of the owner of a wing in the air with its wing. End. Most of it is feminine, and may be masculine; as in al-Misbah. And al-hawa' (with madd) is the subjugated space between heaven and earth; plural a'wiyah; and al-hawa' also means empty thing; and al-hawa (with qasr) is the inclination and deviation of the soul towards something, then used for blameworthy inclination; it is said 'he follows his hawa' and he is from the people of ahwa'; in al-Misbah.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the sale of birds while they are in the air. The basic ruling: it is not permissible because the bird is not owned before capture (batil), and if sold after being released, delivery is impossible, making the contract voidable (fasid). There is a difference of opinion among early Hanafis: according to Mashayikh Balkh, even if the seller later delivers the bird, the contract does not become valid; but according to al-Karkhi, it becomes valid upon delivery. The author then mentions that the ruling applies equally whether the bird is the object of sale or the price. The passage then digresses to discuss the leasing of ponds for fishing (a separate issue but related to capability of delivery). Al-Nahr and al-Ramli comment that the sale of fish in any body of water is absolutely impermissible, contrary to the earlier narration from Umar; they argue that the narration from Umar only applied to a specific place prepared for fishing where fish are effectively capable of delivery. The bird ruling is then resumed with a reference to the 'apparent' view, which is that birds are considered capable of delivery if they are domesticated and habitually return. Fatawa Qadikhan states that domesticated birds that return can be sold. Al-Hidayah also allows sale of pigeons if their return is known. The passage ends with dictionary definitions of tayr and hawa.

Key Terms
الطير (al-tayr): Birds, especially those that fly; in this context, the bird being sold while in the air.
الداجن (al-dajin): A domesticated bird that is raised in the home and returns to it.
مقدور التسليم (maqdur al-taslim): Capable of being delivered; a condition for the validity of a sale.

Selling Unborn Animals (Haml and Nataj): Ruling and Evidence
Ruling on sale of unborn offspring with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling unborn animals (haml and nataj) and explain the dalil (hadith evidence) and the reasoning based on gharar.

Core Idea
Sale of unborn animals is batil due to explicit prohibition and gharar.

This passage addresses a key issue in Islamic commercial law: the validity of selling unborn offspring. The Hanafi school, relying on explicit hadith evidence and the principle of eliminating gharar (uncertainty), rules that such sales are void. We will examine the definitions, the evidence, and the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subjects
The passage discusses the sale of 'haml' (fetus) and 'nataj' (offspring of a pregnant animal). First, recognize what these terms refer to.
2
State the ruling
The author explicitly says the sale of both is 'batil' (void). This is the Hanafi position.
3
Locate the dalil
The evidence is the Prophet's prohibition of selling 'habal' (fetus) and 'habal al-habalah' (fetus of the fetus), as recorded in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
The hadith also mentions 'al-madamin' and 'al-malaqih' which are related concepts.
4
Understand the reasoning
The prohibition is due to gharar (uncertainty) – the object of sale is non-existent or uncertain at the time of contract.
5
Note the scope
The ruling applies to all animals, as stated in the passage: 'nataj al-nataj includes beasts and humans.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والحمل والنتاج أي لا يجوز بيعهما والحمل - بسكون الميم - الجنين والنتاج حمل الحبلة والبيع فيهما باطل لنهي النبي الله عن بيع الحبل وحبل الحبلة ولما فيه من الغرر. وفي مصنف عبد الرازق نهى عن المضامين والملاقيح وحبل الحبلة. المضامين جمع مضمونة ما في أصلاب الإبل، والملاقيح جمع ملقوح ما في بطونها وقيل بالعكس، وحبل الحبلة ولد ولد الناقة . وفي البناية : الحبل - بفتح الباء الموحدة - يطلق ویراد به المصد ویراد به الاسم كما يقال له الحمل أيضاً، وأما دخول تاء التأنيث في الحبلة فإنما للإشعار هي بالأنوثة، وقيل إنها للمبالغة كما في سخرة، ويحتمل أن يكون جمع حابلة ففي المحكم امرأة حابلة من نسوة حبلة، وروى بعض الفقهاء حملت بكسر الميم ولم يثبت اهـ. وفي تلخيص النهاية : بفتح الحاء والباء وقد تسكن نتاج النتاج وهو يعم الدواب والناس. وفي السراج الوهاج : لا يجوز بيع الحمل وحده دون الأم ولا الأم دونه فلو باع الحمل وولدت قبل الشرنبلالية وعزاه . البرهان.
His saying: (And the fetus and the offspring) meaning it is not permissible to sell both. Al-haml (with sukun on the meem) is the fetus, and al-nataj is the pregnancy of a pregnant animal. The sale of both is void due to the Prophet's prohibition of selling the 'habal' (fetus) and 'habal al-habalah' (fetus of the fetus) and because of the gharar (uncertainty). In the Musannaf of Abd al-Razzaq: He forbade 'al-madamin', 'al-malaqih', and 'habal al-habalah'. Al-Madamin (plural of madmunah) is what is in the loins of camels, al-malaqih (plural of maluqah) is what is in their wombs, and some say the opposite. Habal al-habalah is the offspring of the offspring of the she-camel. In al-Binayah: al-habl (with fath on the ba) is used for the verbal noun and the noun, as it is also called al-haml. As for the feminine ta in al-habalah, it is to indicate femininity, or it may be for exaggeration as in 'sakhrah', or it may be a plural of 'habilah' (pregnant woman). Some jurists narrated 'hamilt' with kasr on the meem, but it is not confirmed. End. In Talkhis al-Nihayah: bi-fath al-ha' wa al-ba' and may be sukun; nataj al-nataj, and it includes beasts and humans. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: It is not permissible to sell the fetus alone without the mother, nor the mother without the fetus. So if he sells the fetus and it is born before... (text cut off).
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the sale of unborn animals (haml and nataj). The ruling: the sale is invalid (batil) because the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited selling the 'habal' (fetus) and 'habal al-habalah' (the unborn of the unborn), and because it involves gharar (uncertainty). The author defines the terms: al-haml is the fetus, al-nataj is the pregnancy of a pregnant animal. The hadith from Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq mentions three prohibited types: al-madamin (what is in the loins), al-malaqih (what is in the wombs), and habal al-habalah (the offspring of the offspring). The difference between madamin and malaqih is that some say madamin are what is in the loins (i.e., male), and malaqih are what is in the wombs (i.e., female). The commentary in al-Binayah discusses the linguistic nuances: the word 'habl' can be pronounced with fat'ha or sukun, and 'habalah' refers to the female. The author then quotes al-Siraj al-Wahhaj stating that it is not permissible to sell the fetus alone without the mother, nor the mother without the fetus. The passage ends with a cut-off hypothetical: 'if he sells the fetus and it is born before...' likely discussing the effect of delivery before the sale.

Key Terms
الحمل (al-haml): The fetus in the womb of an animal.
النتاج (al-nataj): The pregnancy of a pregnant animal, i.e., the offspring of a mother that is already pregnant.
حبل الحبلة (habal al-habalah): The offspring of the offspring, i.e., the unborn of an unborn animal.
المضامين والملاقيح (al-madamin wa al-malaqih): Al-madamin: what is in the loins of male camels (i.e., future offspring); al-malaqih: what is in the wombs of female camels (i.e., unborn). Some say the opposite.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The sale of unborn animals (haml, nataj, habal al-habalah) is batil due to explicit prohibition and gharar.

Selling a Bird that Flies: Conditions for Valid Sale
Ruling on selling a bird that flies with custom-based proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid sale of a flying bird and explain how Hanafi custom (urf) establishes deliverability de jure, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on actual vs. de jure delivery.

Core Idea
A bird that habitually returns is deliverable de jure—custom makes the sale valid.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi ruling on selling a bird that can fly. The key issue is whether the bird is considered deliverable (maqdūrat al-taslīm) at the time of contract. The Hanafi school relies on custom (urf) to deem delivery possible, provided the bird is domesticated and habitually returns. You will learn the conditions, the proof based on istihsan, and an important intra-Hanafi disagreement about the nature of delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling a bird that can fly. The ruling is that the sale is valid (sahih/jā'iz) if the bird is domesticated (dājin) and habitually returns to its owner such that it can be taken without trouble. Otherwise, the sale is invalid.
This is the position of the majority of later Hanafi scholars, as recorded in Qadikhan's Fatawa.
2
Pinpoint the conditions for validity
Three conditions: (1) the bird must be domesticated (dājin, meaning raised in the house); (2) it must habitually return to its owner; (3) it must be possible to catch it without extraordinary effort. These conditions are derived from custom and reasonable expectation.
Linguistic definitions of 'tayr' and 'hawā'' are given to clarify the subject matter.
3
Understand the proof (dalīl) based on custom
The proof is istihsan, applying the principle 'al-ma'lūmu 'ādatan ka al-wāqi' (what is known customarily is like reality). Custom treats the habitual return as actual delivery at the time of contract. The possibility of non-return is ignored, just as the possibility of destruction of any sold item before delivery is ignored. If the bird fails to return before delivery, the contract is rescinded—similar to a slave sent on an errand.
The argument is analogical qiyas: the sale of a slave on an errand is valid because the custom is that he returns; the bird case is analogous.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi objection
Some later Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Nahr) object, arguing that ability to deliver must be actual at the time of contract, citing the analogy of a runaway slave (ābiq). They claim that if delivery is not actually possible at the moment of sale, the contract is invalid.
This objection would make any sale where the item is not physically present invalid, which is too strict.
5
Analyze the author's tarjih and the mufta bihi
The author (Ibn Nujaym) rebuts the objection: if 'actual ability' is required, it would be rejected because no one demands the item's presence in the contract session. If 'de jure ability' is meant, the custom of return establishes it. Thus, the majority view is sound (wajīh) and is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
The analogy to a slave on an errand is decisive: the custom of return makes delivery de jure possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب البيع الفاسد
وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : وإن باع طيراً له يطير إن كان داجناً يعود إلى بيته ويقدر على أخذه بلا تكلف جاز بيعه وإلا فلا. وقول صاحب الهداية والحمام إذا علم عودها وأمكن تسليمها جاز بيعها لأنها مقدورة التسليم يوافقه، وصرح به في الذخيرة معزياً إلى المنتقى. وفي المعراج : باع فرساً في حظيرة فقال البائع سلمته إليك ففتح المشتري فذهب الفرس، فإن أمكنه أخذه بيده من غير عون كان تسليماً وإلا فلا لأنه لو مد يده لا يمكنه الأخذ اهـ. وفي القاموس : الطير جمع طائر وقد يقع على الواحد والجمع طيور وأطيار والطيران محركة حركة ذي الجناح في الهواء بجناحه .اهـ. والأكثر فيها التأنيث وقد تذكر ؛ كذا في المصباح. والهواء ممدوداً المسخر بين السماء والأرض والجمع أهوية والهواء أيضاً الشيء الخالي، والهوا مقصوراً ميل النفس وانحرافها الشيء ثم استعمل في ميل ،مذموم يقال اتبع هواه وهو من أهل الأهواء؛ في المصباح. قوله : (إن كان داجناً قال الرملي : الداجن المربي في البيت. قوله : (جاز بيعها) قال في الفتح : لأن المعلوم عادة كالواقع وتجويز كونها لا تعود أو عروض عدم عودها لا يمنع جواز البيع كتجويز هلاك المبيع قبل القبض، ثم إذا عرض الهلاك انفسخ كذا هنا إذا فرض وقوع عدم المعتاد من عودها قبل القبض انفسخ .اهـ. قال في النهر وأقول: فيه نظر لأن من شرط صحة البيع القدرة على التسليم عقبه ولذا لم يجز بيع الآبق .اهـ. وتعقبه بعض الفضلاء بأن ما ادعاه من اشتراط القدرة على التسليم عقبه إن أراد به القدرة حقيقة فهو ممنوع وإلا لا يشترط حضور المبيع مجلس العقد ولا يقول به أحد، وإن أراد به القدرة حكماً كما ذكره بعد هذا فما نحن فيه كذلك لحكم العادة بعوده .اهـ. قلت: وهو وجيه فهو نظير بيع العبد المرسل في حاجة المولى فإنه يجوز، وعللوه بأنه مقدور التسليم وقت العقد حكماً إذ الظاهر عوده ولو أبق بعد البيع قبل القبض خير المشتري في فسخ العقد كما سيأتي قوله : (بخلاف القوائم) أي قوائم
Book of Sales, Chapter on Invalid Sales. In the Fatawa of Qadikhan: If one sells a bird that flies, if it is domesticated and returns to its owner and can be taken without trouble, its sale is valid; otherwise not. And the statement of the author of al-Hidayah and pigeons: if their return is known and delivery is possible, their sale is valid because they are deliverable; he agrees with it. And it is stated explicitly in al-Dhakhirah, attributed to al-Muntaqa. In al-Mi'raj: If someone sold a horse in an enclosure, and the seller said 'I have delivered it to you,' and the buyer opened (the gate) and the horse fled, then if he could have taken it by hand without assistance, it is delivery; otherwise not, because if he extended his hand he could not take it. In al-Qamus: al-Tayr is the plural of Ta'ir, and it can also be used for the singular; the plurals are Tuyur and Atyar. Al-Tayaran (with fat-ha on the ya) is the movement of a winged creature in the air with its wing. Mostly it is feminine, but sometimes masculine; similarly in al-Misbah. Al-Hawa' (with madd) is the space between heaven and earth, and its plural is Ahwiyah; also, al-Hawa' means empty space. Al-Hawa (with qasr) is the inclination of the soul and its deviation; then it is used for a blameworthy inclination; it is said 'he followed his hawa' and 'he is among the people of hawa'. In al-Misbah. His saying: 'if it is domesticated' – al-Ramli said: al-Dajin is the one raised in the house. His saying: 'its sale is valid' – said in al-Fath: because what is known customarily is like reality, and the possibility that it may not return or the occurrence of its not returning does not prevent the validity of the sale, like the possibility of the destruction of the sold item before delivery; then if destruction occurs, the contract is rescinded. Similarly here, if the occurrence of the non-return (contrary to the habitual) is assumed to happen before delivery, the contract is rescinded. He said in al-Nahr: 'I say: there is consideration in this, because among the conditions for the validity of a sale is the ability to deliver at the time of the contract, and therefore the sale of a runaway slave is not valid.' And some virtuous person objected: 'What he claims about the condition of ability to deliver at the time of the contract – if he means actual ability, it is not accepted; otherwise, the presence of the sold item at the contract session is not required, and no one says so. If he means ability de jure (as he mentions later), then our case is similar due to the custom that it returns.' I said: It is sound, and it is like the sale of a slave sent on an errand by the master; it is valid, and they explained it as being deliverable de jure at the time of contract because the apparent custom is that he returns. If the slave runs away after sale but before delivery, the buyer has the option to rescind the contract (khiyar) as will come. His saying: 'contrary to the legs' – i.e., legs.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling on the validity of selling a bird that can fly. The author opens with a fatwa from Qadi Khan: if a bird is domesticated (dājin) and habitually returns to its owner, making it possible to catch without trouble, the sale is valid; otherwise it is not. This is supported by a statement from al-Hidayah regarding pigeons, where knowledge of their return and ability to deliver justifies the sale on the grounds that the bird is deliverable (maqdūrat al-taslīm). The commentary then cites al-Mi'raj with an analogous case: selling a horse in an enclosure; if the buyer could take it by hand without help, delivery is effective. Linguistic definitions of 'tayr' (bird) and 'hawā'' (air) follow from al-Qamus and al-Misbah, establishing the scope of the terms. Then the author explains the matn phrase 'in kāna dājinā' (if it is domesticated): al-Ramli defines it as 'raised in the house.' Next, the phrase 'jāza bay'uhā' (its sale is valid) is expanded with a rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī) from al-Fath: the principle 'what is known customarily is like reality' (al-ma'lūmu 'ādatan ka al-wāqi') means that habitual return is treated as actual delivery; the mere possibility of non-return does not invalidate the sale, just as the possibility of destruction of any sold item before delivery does not invalidate it. If the habitual does not occur, the contract is rescinded. Then a counter-argument from al-Nahr appears: the ability to deliver must exist at the time of the contract, otherwise it is like selling a runaway slave. This is rebutted by a virtuous scholar: if actual ability is required, that would be too strict (since the presence of the item at the contract session is not required); if ability de jure is meant, then our case qualifies because custom deems it deliverable. The author (Ibn Nujaym) endorses this rebuttal, comparing it to selling a slave sent on an errand by the master, which is valid due to apparent custom of return. If the slave runs away after the contract but before delivery, the buyer has the option of rescission (khiyar). The passage ends by noting that legs (qawā'im) are different (will be explained later). Thus, the Hanafi position is that sale of a bird that flies is valid if it is domesticated and customarily returns; the ability to deliver is judged by custom and reasonable expectation. The commentary guards against the misunderstanding that any uncertainty about return automatically invalidates the sale; rather, the rule is based on the prevailing custom.

Key Terms
داجن (dājin): A domesticated bird raised in the house, as defined by al-Ramli.
تسليم (taslīm): Delivery of the sold item to the buyer, either actually or de jure (based on custom).
مقدور التسليم (maqdūrat al-taslīm): An item that is considered deliverable, either actually or by reasonable expectation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view in this passage, what is the basis for considering a domesticated bird deliverable at the time of sale?
A) The bird is physically held by the seller.
B) The custom that the bird habitually returns makes it deliverable de jure.
C) The buyer's willingness to take the risk.
D) The bird's wings are clipped.

Key Takeaway
The sale of a flying bird is valid if it is domesticated and habitually returns, because custom (urf) establishes deliverability de jure; the buyer may rescind if the bird does not actually return before delivery.

Prohibited Sales Due to Gharar
List of six invalid sales from Kanz al-Daqaiq matn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six prohibited sales due to gharar from Kanz al-Daqaiq and explain why each is invalid due to uncertainty.

Core Idea
Six types of sales are invalid due to gharar: unknown existence, quantity, or deliverability.

This lesson covers the initial list from Kanz al-Daqaiq on prohibited sales due to gharar. The matn succinctly lists six cases to illustrate the principle that uncertainty invalidates a sale. Understanding these cases helps you identify invalid contracts in everyday transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify each prohibited sale item
Read the Arabic list: pearl in shell, wool on sheep, beam in ceiling, cubit of cloth, stroke of separation, and salam.
2
Determine the type of uncertainty for each
For each case, ask: Is the existence unknown? Is the quantity unknown? Is delivery possible? Example: pearl in shell – existence uncertain; wool on sheep – quantity uncertain; beam in ceiling – deliverability uncertain.
3
Link to the principle of gharar
Gharar is any uncertainty that leads to potential dispute. Each case involves gharar that makes the sale fasid.
4
Note the exception of salam
Salam (advance payment sale) is listed here but is actually permissible under specific conditions (fully specified future delivery). The matn includes it to remind that improper salam is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللؤلؤ في الصدف والصوف على ظهر الغنم والجذع في السقف وذراع من ثوب وضربة الافتراق وسلم لا يجوز
And the pearl in the shell, and the wool on the back of the sheep, and the beam in the ceiling, and a cubit of cloth, and the stroke of separation, and salam are not permissible.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage states a list of sales that are invalid due to gharar (uncertainty). Each item involves unknown quantity, existence, or delivery: pearls inside a shell (unknown existence and value), wool on a live sheep (unknown amount and cutting location), a beam fixed in a ceiling (cannot be delivered without damage), a cubit of cloth from an unspecified part (unknown which part), a 'stroke of separation' (ambiguous), and salam (advance payment) without proper specifications. The ruling is a straightforward prohibition as part of the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq.

Key Terms
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk that leads to invalidity of a sale, such as unknown existence, quantity, or deliverability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is not yet cut
B) Because the quantity and cutting position are unknown
C) Because the sheep might die
D) Because it is forbidden by hadith

Key Takeaway
Sales involving uncertainty in existence, quantity, or deliverability are invalid due to gharar.

Why Selling Milk in the Udder is Invalid
Commentary on matn phrase 'milk in the udder' with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling milk in the udder and explain the gharar reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
Selling milk in the udder is invalid due to gharar—uncertainty in quantity and extraction.

The matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq states that selling milk while it is still in the udder is not permissible. This lesson unpacks the commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, which explains that the prohibition stems from gharar (uncertainty) in the sale. We will examine the specific reasons for the ruling and the lexical clarification of the term 'dar' (udder).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling milk that is still inside the udder of a cloven-hoofed animal. The ruling from the matn and commentary is that such a sale is not permissible (invalid/fasid).
The ruling is from the relied-upon position of the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the cause (illah) of the ruling
The cause is gharar (uncertainty/risk). The commentary elaborates three factors that create gharar: possible swelling of the udder (making quantity uncertain), dispute about the method of milking, and the possibility that the milk increases during extraction, causing mixture.
Gharar is a well-established reason for prohibition in sales.
3
Understand the specific gharar factors
Read the commentary: 'perhaps it is swollen, dispute about the method of milking, and perhaps it increases, so the sold item gets mixed with other.' These show that both the quantity and the process are unknown.
Each factor independently creates uncertainty, but combined they make the sale clearly invalid.
4
Note the lexical clarification
The commentator quotes al-Misbah to define 'dar' (udder) as the milk-storing organ of cloven-hoofed animals, analogous to a woman's breast. This clarifies that the subject matter (milk) is contained within the udder, not the udder itself.
Understanding the terms is crucial for correct application.
5
Recognize the mufta bihi and any ikhtilaf
The relied-upon position is that the sale is invalid (fasid) and thus forbidden. However, some later Hanafis permitted it if customary, but that view is not the operative one.
The majority and the tarjih in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq favor prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( واللبن) في الضرع أي لا يجوز بيعه للغرر فعساه انتفاخ ولأنه ينازع في كيفية الحلب وربما يزداد فيختلط المبيع بغيره. وفي المصباح : الضرع لذات الظلف كالثدي للمرأة والجمع ضروع مثل فلس وفلوس
His saying: (and milk) in the udder means its sale is not permissible due to gharar, for perhaps it is swollen, and because there is dispute about the method of milking, and perhaps it increases, so the sold item gets mixed with other. And in al-Misbah: The udder is for cloven-hoofed animals like the breast for a woman; plural is duru' like fals and fulus.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This lesson explains the matn 'wal-laban fi al-dar' (milk in the udder). The commentator states that selling milk in the udder is invalid due to gharar: the milk might be swollen (so quantity uncertain), the method of milking is disputed, and the milk might increase during milking causing mixture with other milk. The commentator then quotes al-Misbah to define 'dar' (udder) as the milk organ of cloven-hoofed animals, analogous to a woman's breast, and gives the plural form. The proof is rational (gharar) and lexical clarification.

Key Terms
الضرع (al-dar'): The udder of a cloven-hoofed animal, where milk is stored; analogous to a woman's breast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the milk is not yet in existence
B) Because of gharar: uncertainty about the amount and extraction
C) Because the animal is alive and cannot be sold
D) Because the buyer cannot taste the milk beforehand

Key Takeaway
Selling milk in the udder is invalid due to gharar, exemplifying that any sale with unknown quantity is prohibited.

Selling Pearls in the Shell: Gharar and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on matn 'pearls in the shell' with Abu Yusuf's view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling pearls in the shell, explain why it is invalid due to gharar, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference of opinion on this issue.

Core Idea
Selling a pearl inside a shell is invalid due to gharar—unknown existence, quantity, and harmful delivery.

The sale of a pearl in its shell is a classic example of prohibited gharar (uncertainty) in Hanafi fiqh. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the prohibition, examines Abu Yusuf's minority view that permits it, and distinguishes similar sales that are valid. By understanding this ruling, you will sharpen your ability to identify gharar in commercial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling a pearl still inside its shell. The Hanafi ruling is that the sale is invalid (fasid) due to gharar.
The matn phrase 'wal-lu'lu' fi al-sadaf' is the subject.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence) for the ruling
The reasoning is based on the prohibition of gharar: the pearl's existence, size, and quality are unknown, and delivery requires breaking the shell, which causes harm.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) rooted in the legal maxim 'al-gharar al-fahish yumna'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the dalil proves the ruling)
The pearl is not known in existence (maybe empty), quantity (size), or quality. Moreover, delivery necessitates breaking the shell, which is a form of damage (darar). Both uncertainty and harm invalidate the sale.
The harm is explicit: 'la yumkin taslimuhu illa bi darar wa huwa kasr al-sadaf'.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) dissents, permitting the sale because the shell has no benefit except by breaking, so breaking is not considered harm.
The text states: 'wa 'an Abi Yusuf al-jawaz li-anna al-sadaf la yuntafa'u bihi illa bi al-kasr fala yu'addu daran'.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The majority of the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold the prohibition. The mufta bihi (operative position) is prohibition, as upheld by Ibn Nujaym and later scholars.
The tarjih is based on stronger analogy and avoidance of gharar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (واللؤلؤ في الصدف) للغرر وهو مجهول لا يعلم وجوده ولا قدره ولا يمكن تسليمه إلا بضرر وهو كسر الصدف وعن أبي يوسف الجواز لأن الصدف لا ينتفع به إلا بالكسر فلا يعد ضرراً. قيد به لأنه لو باع تراب الذهب والحبوب في غلافها جاز لكونها معلومة وتعلم بالقبض. وفي السراج الوهاج : لو اشترى دجاجة فوجد في بطنها لؤلؤة فهي للبائع ولو باع كرش شاة مذبوحة لم تسلخ جاز وإخراجه على البائع والمشتري بالخيار إذا رآه، واللؤلؤ الدر واحده بهاء ؛ كذا في القاموس. والصدف محركة غشاء الدر الواحد بهاء والجمع أصداف؛ منه أيضاً
His saying: (and the pearl in the shell) due to gharar, for it is unknown, not known its existence nor its quantity, nor can it be delivered except with harm, which is breaking the shell. And from Abu Yusuf: permissibility because the shell is not benefited from except by breaking, so it is not considered harm. He restricted it because if one sells gold dust and grains in their husks, it is permissible because they are known and become known by taking possession. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If one buys a chicken and finds a pearl in its belly, it belongs to the seller. And if one sells the stomach of a slaughtered sheep that has not been skinned, it is permissible, and its removal is upon the seller, and the buyer has option upon seeing it. And al-lu'lu' is the pearl, its singular with ta' marbuta; thus in al-Qamus. And al-sadaf (with fatha on the letters) is the covering of the pearl, its singular with ta' marbuta, plural asdaf; from it also.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn phrase 'wal-lu'lu' fi al-sadaf' (pearls in the shell). The ruling of invalidity is due to gharar: the pearl's existence, size, and quality are unknown, and delivery requires breaking the shell, causing damage. The commentator notes Abu Yusuf's dissenting view that the sale is permissible because breaking the shell is not harm since the shell is only used by breaking. The commentary adds that selling gold dust or grains in husks is permissible because they are known through possession. Then a quote from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj discusses finding a pearl inside a purchased chicken (belongs to seller) and selling a sheep's stomach (permissible with delivery obligation on seller and buyer's option). Lexical notes define lu'lu' and sadaf.

Key Terms
الصدف (al-sadaf): The shell that contains a pearl; its singular is sadafa, plural asdaf.
اللؤلؤ (al-lu'lu'): Pearl; the singular in Arabic is lu'lu'a with ta' marbuta.
الغرر (al-gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a contract that leads to invalidity when excessive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is Abu Yusuf's view on selling pearls in the shell, as mentioned in the passage?
A) He prohibits it absolutely
B) He permits it because breaking the shell is not considered harm
C) He permits it only if the seller breaks the shell before delivery
D) He considers it makruh tanzihi

Key Takeaway
Selling a pearl inside a shell is invalid due to gharar (unknown existence and quantity, harmful delivery), though Abu Yusuf permits it based on a different assessment of harm; the majority and mufta bihi position is prohibition.

Selling Inseparable Parts: The Harm Condition
Commentary on matn ruling with conditions for selling inseparable parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which selling an indivisible part of a larger object is valid in Hanafi fiqh, and apply that condition to new cases.

Core Idea
Selling part of an object is invalid if delivery damages it, but valid if no harm occurs.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on selling a beam in a ceiling or a cubit of cloth. The key is whether delivery causes damage to the sold item or other property. The commentary refines the absolute statement of the matn, showing that if the cloth is not harmed by cutting (like karbas), the sale is permissible. We will explore the reasoning and its application to various cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and rationale
The matn states that selling a beam in a ceiling or a cubit of cloth is invalid because the seller cannot deliver without causing harm (breaking the ceiling or cutting the cloth). Understand that harm here refers to damage to the property being sold or to another's property.
The rationale is the legal principle that one must not cause harm.
2
Distinguish between harmed and unharmed objects
The commentary explains that the ruling is not absolute: if the cloth is not damaged by cutting, like coarse karbas, the sale is permissible. Compare this to a turban or shirt, which are ruined by cutting.
The distinction hinges on whether the sold part is defective after separation.
3
Apply the condition to other examples
The text lists similar cases: selling an ornament from a sword, half a crop, a mounted gem, a share of a joint garment without consent, and a cubit of wood. For each, delivery requires damage to the whole, so the sale is invalid.
Note that the harm may be to the sold item (ornament from sword) or to other property (walls from removing dovetails).
4
Examine the role of custom
The commentary notes that selling legs (qawa'im) is invalid by analogy but permitted by custom (ta'amul). This shows that custom can override qiyas in Hanafi fiqh.
Custom is a secondary source that can create exceptions.

Source Text

It is countered by the sale of the dovetails (habab) that cannot be removed except by removing doors, according to those who allow it, but some prohibit it. The response is that the harm is to the walls, not to the dovetails. This indicates that the consideration in prohibition is defect of the sold item, while the previous speech indicates it is defect of other than sold, which is apparent. Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This lesson is a commentary on the matn phrase 'al-jidh' fi al-saqf wa dhira' min thawb' (the beam in the ceiling and a cubit of cloth). The ruling of invalidity is because delivery requires damage (breaking the ceiling or cutting cloth). However, the commentator restricts this: if the cloth is not harmed by cutting (like cheap karbas fabric), the sale is permissible. He critiques al-Tahawi's blanket prohibition, arguing that karbas not defective is fine, analogous to selling a specific measure from a heap. The author then lists other cases: selling a sword ornament (requires breaking the sword), half an unripe crop (needs full cutting), a mounted gem (must disassemble), a share of a joint garment without co-owner consent, a cubit of wood (damage in cutting). The harm is to the sold item or others? The discussion clarifies that the prohibition considers damage to the sold item according to some, or to others according to others. Examples: selling dovetails (habab) that require removing doors; if walls are damaged, it's impermissible. The passage then discusses buying trees for cutting: if cutting harms the ground, compensation is given. The author references other works for further details and notes that selling legs (qawa'im) is prohibited by analogy but permitted by custom.

Key Terms
الكرباس (al-karbas): A coarse cotton cloth that is not significantly damaged by cutting a piece from it.
الحباب (al-habab): Dovetails or fasteners used in construction, requiring removal of doors for extraction.
القوائم (al-qawa'im): Legs of an object (e.g., table legs) sold separately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is selling a cubit of cloth permissible according to the commentary?
A) Only if the cloth is expensive
B) If the cloth is not harmed by cutting, like karbas
C) Only if the buyer cuts it himself
D) It is never permissible

Key Takeaway
The validity of selling a part of an object depends on whether its delivery causes damage to the sold item or other property; if not, it is valid.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Invalid Sales Due to Necessary Damage in Delivery
Expands the ruling on sales requiring damaging delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for sales requiring damaging delivery, apply it to specific cases, and distinguish exceptions based on customary practice.

Core Idea
Sales where delivery spoils the item or attached property are fasid, unless custom allows.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks a key principle in Hanafi sales law: if selling something requires damaging either the sold object or the property it's attached to, the sale is invalid (fasid). The commentary navigates numerous examples—from a beam in a roof to a cubit of cloth—showing when damage is fatal and when customary practice (ta'amul) overrides the general prohibition. Understanding these nuances prevents void transactions and highlights the role of istihsan in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the underlying principle
Recognize that the core rule is: any sale where the sold item cannot be delivered without causing damage (darar) to the item itself or to what it is attached to is invalid (fasid).
This is based on the prohibition of gharar and harm.
2
Apply the principle to the rule of ghilaf
Items sold in their natural covering (ghilaf) – like milk in the udder or meat on a live animal – cannot be delivered without spoiling their form; thus their sale is fasid. Grains in husk are an exception.
The exception is due to facility (ta'amul).
3
Analyze part-of-property examples
When selling a part of a whole that cannot be separated without damage (e.g., a beam from a roof, a cubit from a garment that cutting damages), the sale is invalid. If separation causes no damage (e.g., coarse cloth, silver ingot) the sale is valid.
The key is whether the division causes defect to the sold part or the remainder.
4
Consider the role of custom (ta'amul)
Customary practice can override the analogy (qiyas) and permit sales that would otherwise be invalid, such as selling leeks, qawa'im (wooden beams in buildings), or trees to be cut.
This is an application of istihsan.
5
Evaluate the effect of delivery after contract
If the seller cuts and delivers the item (e.g., cuts the cubit) before the buyer rescinds, the invalidating factor is removed and the sale becomes valid. This differs from cases like wool or milk delivered post-contract, which do not become valid.
The beam is a real existing entity; wool and milk are not separable without altering the subject.

Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a Hanafi commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expands on invalid sales (al-bay' al-fasid) due to the inability to deliver the sold item without causing damage (darar) to either the sold item itself or to the property it is attached to. The commentator first clarifies that selling a tree on condition that the buyer cuts it is invalid because the place of cutting is unknown (jahalah), which leads to dispute. However, there is a difference of opinion among scholars: some prohibit it because cutting requires damaging the ground, while others permit it based on customary practice (ta'amul), unlike a plant stalk (qasil) which is simply uprooted without conflict. The author then states a general principle: everything sold in its covering (ghilaf) — such as milk in the udder, meat on a live sheep, fat, tail, legs, skins, flour in wheat, ghee in milk, etc. — cannot be delivered without spoiling the natural form (ifsad al-khilqah), so such sales are invalid. An exception is made for grains in their husk, as explained earlier. Similarly, selling gold and silver in their soil is invalid, unlike selling them in a pure state. The commentator cites Fath al-Qadir. He then addresses the case where the seller delivers the wool or milk after the contract; even then, the sale does not become valid (as per al-Siraj al-Wahhaj). However, the sale of leeks (kurrath) is permissible despite growing from its base, due to customary practice. A lengthy digression discusses the etymology of 'khilaf' (willow tree). Returning to the main topic, the commentator explains the matn phrase 'al-jidh' fi al-saqf wa dhira' min thawb' (the beam in the roof and a cubit of cloth). The prohibition is because delivery requires damage. This is restricted to garments that are damaged by cutting, like turbans and shirts; for materials like coarse cloth (karbas) that are not damaged, the sale is permissible. The commentator rejects al-Tahawi's blanket prohibition for karbas, restricting it to cases where cutting causes defect. He then lists other cases where delivery causes damage: selling a sword's ornament, half a crop before ripening, a gem set in a ring, one's share of a jointly-owned garment without the partner, a cubit of wood, etc. The key consideration is the damage to the sold item itself (ta'yib al-mabi') or to something else. A story illustrates someone buying trees for cutting; if cutting damages the ground, the seller must compensate. The passage then discusses what happens if the seller cuts and delivers the item before the buyer rescinds: the sale becomes valid. Several opinions are cited from al-Mujtaba. Finally, the commentator discusses the permissibility of selling straw before threshing, etc., noting two narrations. Throughout, the underlying principle is that if delivery necessarily causes damage, the sale is invalid, unless custom (ta'amul) permits it.

Key Terms
غلاف (ghilaf): The covering or husk in which something is naturally encased, e.g., milk in udder, meat on live animal, grain in husk.
ضرر (darar): Damage or harm caused to the sold item or the property it is attached to during delivery.
تسليم (taslim): Delivery of the sold item to the buyer, which must be possible without causing damage.
جذع في السقف (jidh' fi al-saqf): A beam that is part of a roof; selling just the beam is invalid because removing it damages the roof.
تعامل (ta'amul): Customary practice or usage among people, which can override the general prohibition in certain cases like selling leeks or qawa'im.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling 'a cubit of cloth' from a garment (like a turban) invalid in the Hanafi school?
A) Because the cloth is not yet cut.
B) Because cutting the cloth damages the garment, making delivery impossible without harm.
C) Because the price is unknown.
D) Because the buyer must own the whole garment.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: any sale requiring damage to the sold item or attached property for delivery is invalid (fasid), unless customary practice (ta'amul) allows an exception.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Invalid Sales: Diver's Catch and Muzabana
Explains prohibition of selling unknown catch (diver/hunter) and muzabana (date exchange)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ضربة القانص and المزابنة, and explain why each is batil due to gharar and explicit prohibition.

Core Idea
Sales with unknown or unowned objects are batil; muzabana is explicitly prohibited by hadith.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Ibn Nujaym explains two specific types of prohibited sales that involve uncertainty (gharar) and explicit textual prohibition. The first is the sale of a hunter's or diver's catch where the outcome is unknown; the second is muzabana—the exchange of fresh dates on the tree for dry dates. The commentator clarifies the linguistic readings and legal reasoning behind their invalidity, citing the hadith prohibition and the principle that the seller must own the object at the time of contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two prohibited sales
Name both sales and their typical formulations. For ضربة القانص: the seller says 'I sell you whatever I catch in one attempt.' For المزابنة: selling fresh dates on the tree for dry dates.
Recognize that both involve uncertainty in the object of sale.
2
Analyze the first sale (ضربة القانص)
Why is it batil? The seller does not own the specific catch at contract time, and the quantity is unknown—both constitute gharar (uncertainty) and jahalah (ignorance).
The commentary explains that the seller's lack of ownership and the unknown outcome render the contract void.
3
Analyze the second sale (المزابنة)
Why is it batil? The hadith explicitly prohibits it. The exchange involves unknown equivalence and potential riba, leading to disputes.
The term 'muzabana' is derived from 'zabn' (pushing), indicating the conflict it causes.
4
Understand the linguistic discussion
The word 'qanis' (hunter) vs 'gha'is' (diver). The commentator authenticates the reading 'gha'is', but both refer to the same invalid sale.
This shows the depth of Hanafi fiqh in clarifying the transmitted wording.
5
Apply the general principle
From these two cases, derive that any sale with significant gharar (uncertainty about the object or its delivery) or explicitly prohibited by the Prophet is batil.
This principle guards wealth and prevents disputes.

Source Text

And muhaqala is from haql, which is the field, meaning the good soil pure from impurities for cultivation; from it haqala yahqulu when he cultivates. Muhaqala is a verbal noun from that, and it is sharecropping (muzara'ah) Salty suitable. And even though it grows from its base, it is permissible due to customary practice, and by that the answer is obtained regarding what al-Fadli argued for prohibition in al-qawa'im. Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage explains two categories of invalid sales: ضربة القانص (sale of what a hunter/diver catches) and المزابنة (muzabana). First, the commentator (Ibn Nujaym) elucidates the matn phrase 'ضربة القانص' (strike of the hunter). The word 'qanis' is with qaf and nun, meaning a hunter. The sale is phrased as 'I sell you what comes out from casting this net one time for such price.' Some recitation has 'gha'is' (with ghain and ya), meaning a diver. The commentator quotes Tahdhib al-Azhari that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade the 'strike of the gha'is' (diver's strike), where one says 'I dive once and whatever I bring up of pearls is yours for such amount.' This sale is batil (void) because the seller does not own the item before the contract (so the object is non-existent or unknown), leading to gharar (uncertainty) and jahalah (ignorance). The same applies to the hunter's catch. The passage then corrects the linguistic issue: al-Binaya authenticates the reading 'gha'is' (with ghain), explaining that 'qanis' means hunter, while 'gha'is' means diver, with two usages. Lexical details are given from various dictionaries. Next, the commentator addresses 'المزابنة' (muzabana). It is a type of sale where fresh dates on the tree are exchanged for dried dates, which is explicitly prohibited by the Prophet (hadith). The term 'muzabana' is derived from 'al-zabn' meaning pushing, because the transaction often leads to dispute and arguments. Similarly, 'المحاقلة' (muhaqala) is the sale of standing grain for harvested grain, also prohibited. Muhaqala is related to 'haql' (field) and essentially covers forbidden sharecropping arrangements. The passage ends with a note about selling things that grow from the base (like leeks) being permissible due to customary practice, and a brief reference to a difference of opinion in al-Mujtaba regarding selling straw before threshing, etc., where al-Walwalji gave a decisive ruling. The overall theme is that sales with excessive uncertainty or those specifically prohibited by hadith are invalid.

Key Terms
ضربة القانص (darbat al-qanis): A sale where the buyer pays a fixed amount for whatever the hunter/diver catches in one attempt; the object is unknown and not owned at contract.
مزابنة (muzabana): The sale of fresh dates on the tree for dry dates (or similar exchange of unknown quantity).
محاقلة (muhaqala): The sale of standing grain for harvested grain, or certain sharecropping arrangements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the buyer is not allowed to sell pearls.
D) Because the price is too low.

Key Takeaway
Two common invalid sales are the sale of uncertain future catch (ضربة القانص) and the exchange of fresh fruit for dry fruit of the same kind (المزابنة); both are batil due to gharar or explicit prohibition.
