Sale of a Specified Part: Conditions for Validity and Hanafi Opinions
Explains ruling and multiple Hanafi opinions on selling a specified portion of a defined object

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid sale of a specified part of a defined object and distinguish the four Hanafi opinions on its validity when the seller separates the part.

Core Idea
A sale of a part of a whole is valid if the part is separable without harm, yet the buyer's consent remains essential.

Selling a part of a whole object—like a cubit of cloth or a palm trunk—raises questions of gharar and uncertainty. Hanafi jurists discuss when such a sale becomes valid, especially if the seller separates the part. This lesson unpacks the conditions and the internal Hanafi dispute on the point of validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its initial ruling
Recognize that selling a specified part of a defined object (e.g., a cubit of cloth) is initially a fasid (invalid) sale due to gharar (uncertainty).
The default assumption is invalidity unless the uncertainty is removed.
2
Note the condition that removes the defect
If the seller separates the part (e.g., cuts the cubit) before the buyer revokes the contract, the vitiating factor is removed and the sale becomes valid. This is the core ruling.
The separation must be of a part that can be delivered without significant harm to the whole.
3
Examine the four Hanafi opinions on the precise moment of validity
The souce lists four views: (a) buyer is not compelled to accept unless he consents; (b) a new contract is required; (c) it is concluded by mutual taking upon receipt; (d) it is valid from the beginning. Understand each view.
The difference is about when the contract becomes binding and whether a new offer is needed.
4
Apply the qiyas analogy to other cases
The text analogizes selling a cubit of cloth to selling ten dirhams from a silver nugget (both permissible) and contrasts with selling pits in dates or seeds in watermelon (invalid). The key is absence of harm in separation.
The illa (effective cause) is the absence of harm in dividing and delivering the part.
5
Distinguish cases where specification is required
The sale is valid only if the part is specified (e.g., 'a cubit from the corner')? The text says if a cubit is sold without specifying which side, it is not permissible due to ignorance. Also, selling a part that is not separable without harm (like wool on a sheep) remains invalid even if delivered.
Specification of the part and harmlessness are both conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في فتح القدير. فلو قطع البائع الذراع أو قلع الجذع قبل فسخ المشتري عاد صحيحاً لزوال المفسد. وذكر في المجتبى فيه أقوالاً فقيل لم يجبر على القبول إلا أن يقبل برضاه، وقيل لم يجز إلا بتجديد البيع، وقيل ينعقد تعاطياً عند أخذه وقيل ينعقد من الأصل بخلاف ما إذا باع النوى في التمر أو البزر في البطيخ حيث لا يصح وإن شقهما وأخرج المبيع لأن في وجودهما احتمالاً، أما الجذع فعين موجودة وبخلاف الصوف فإنه لا ينقلب صحيحاً بالتسليم. وقيد بذراع من ثوب لأنه لو باع عشرة دراهم من نقرة فضة جاز لأنه لا ضرر في تبعيضه، ولو لم يكن معيناً لا يجوز لما ذكرنا وللجهالة أيضاً كما في الهداية وخرج أيضاً ما لا ضرر في تسليمه كبيع نخل أو شجر على أن يقطعه المشتري أو زرعاً على أن يحصده؛ كذا في المعراج . وأطلقه أيضاً فشمل ما إذا باع ذراعاً وعين الجانب فلا يجوز أيضاً كما في المعراج.
In Fath al-Qadir: If the seller cuts the cubit or uproots the trunk before the buyer's annulment, it becomes valid because the vitiating factor has been removed. And it is mentioned in al-Mujtaba regarding this: there are sayings: some said he is not compelled to accept unless he accepts willingly; some said it is not permissible except with a renewal of the sale; some said it is contracted by mutual taking upon receipt; and some said it is valid from the beginning, contrary to if he sold the pits in dates or seeds in watermelon, which is not valid even if he splits them and takes out the sold item because their existence involves probability. As for the trunk, it is an existing entity. And contrary to wool, it does not become valid by delivery. And it is restricted to a cubit from a cloth because if he sold ten dirhams from a silver nugget, it is permissible because there is no harm in dividing it. And if it is not specified, it is not permissible due to what we mentioned and also due to ignorance, as in al-Hidayah. And also excluded is that which involves no harm in its delivery, like selling a palm tree or a tree on condition that the buyer cuts it, or standing crop on condition that he harvests it; as in al-Mi'raj. And it was also left unrestricted, so it includes if he sold a cubit and designated the side, then it is not permissible also, as in al-Mi'raj.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on selling a specified portion of a defined object, such as a cubit of cloth or a palm trunk, which is initially a fasid (invalid) sale due to gharar. If the seller separates the portion before the buyer revokes, the defect is removed and the sale becomes valid. The text presents four Hanafi opinions: (1) the buyer is not compelled to accept unless he consents; (2) a new contract is required; (3) it is concluded by mutual taking upon receipt; (4) it is valid from the outset. The correct view is that it becomes valid if the portion is clearly separable without significant loss. The key condition is that the sale must be of a part that can be delivered without harm to the whole, like a measure from a silver nugget or a tree to be cut by the buyer. However, if the part is not specified (e.g., 'a cubit' without indicating which part), the sale is invalid due to ignorance.

Key Terms
ذراع (dhira'): A cubit; a unit of length used to specify a portion of cloth to be sold
تبعيض (tab'id): Dividing the sold item into parts; the permissibility depends on absence of harm
مفسد (mufsid): Vitiating factor; in this context, gharar (uncertainty) that makes the sale fasid

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The sale becomes valid.
B) The sale remains invalid.
C) The buyer must accept.
D) A new contract is required.

Key Takeaway
A sale of a specified part of a defined object is initially invalid due to gharar, but becomes valid if the seller separates the part before the buyer revokes, provided the part is clearly specified and separable without harm.

The Prohibition of Selling a Single Strike/Dive Catch
Defines and rules on the prohibited sale of a single throw/dive catch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the sale of a hunter's single throw (ضربة القانص) is batil and identify the key conditions of ownership and certainty required for a valid sale in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A sale is void if the item is not owned at contract, due to gharar and ignorance.

In this lesson, we examine a specific prohibited sale known as ضربة القانص or ضربة الغائص. The Hanafi school rules that it is batil because the seller does not own the catch at the time of contract, leading to uncertainty (gharar) and ignorance (jahl) of the subject matter. Understanding this ruling clarifies a fundamental condition for the validity of any sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sale type
Recognize the transaction: a hunter/diver sells whatever emerges from a single action (a throw of the net or a dive) for a fixed price.
The key is the sale of an unknown outcome of a single future action.
2
Spot the missing ownership
The seller does not own the item at contract time, violating the condition that the sold item must be owned and possessed (or its risk assumed) at the time of sale.
Ownership is a condition for the subject matter of a sale (مالكية المبيع).
3
Recognize gharar and jahl
The uncertainty of what will be caught, its quantity, quality, and existence constitutes excessive uncertainty (gharar fahish) and ignorance (jahl) of the subject matter.
Gharar fahish invalidates the contract; minor gharar (like unknown weight of a known article) may be tolerated in some cases.
4
Conclude the ruling
Such a sale is batil (void ab initio), not merely fasid (defective). It does not transfer ownership even after delivery.
Batil contracts are null from the start and cannot be cured.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage defines and rules on the sale known as ضربة القانص or ضربة الغائص. In this type of sale, a hunter or diver says: 'I sell you whatever comes out from this one throw/dive for a fixed price.' The extensive lexicographical discussion confirms that the prohibition applies to both terms, and the sale is utterly void.

ضربة القانص (darbat al-qanis): A sale in which a hunter sells whatever he catches in a single throw of his net
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk that renders a contract invalid

Key Takeaway

Muzabanah, Muhaqalah, and the 'Araya Dispute
Defines muzabanah/muhaqalah, states prohibition, and refutes Shafi'i view on 'araya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi position on muzabanah and 'araya from the Shafi'i position, and explain the three Hanafi arguments for interpreting 'araya as a gift.

Core Idea
Muzabanah is haram; the 'araya exception is a gift, not a sale — the Hanafi school limits it to a promise-keeping license.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the prohibition of muzabanah and muhaqalah, and the controversial 'araya transaction. While both schools agree on the general prohibition, they differ on the exception for 'araya. The Hanafi school carefully interprets the hadith permission to avoid opening a general exception that could undermine the prohibition.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on muzabanah and muhaqalah
Identify the definitions and the ruling: muzabanah is selling fresh fruit on trees for dried dates; muhaqalah has multiple interpretations (sharecropping, renting land for wheat, etc.). Both are haram due to explicit prohibition.
The source gives multiple definitions of muhaqalah; al-Hidayah adopts one.
2
Recognize the Shafi'i objection through the 'araya tradition
Shafi'i cites the hadith permission ('rukhkhisa fi al-'araya') to allow a real sale of estimated fresh dates for dried dates when less than five wasaq, making an exception to the prohibition.
This appears to directly contradict the general prohibition.
3
Trace the threefold Hanafi response
(1) 'Araya linguistically means gift; (2) 'permission' is a license to fulfill a promise, not a new sale; (3) the five-wasaq limit is incidental to the specific incident, not a general permission.
This response preserves the general prohibition while accommodating the hadith.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi debate on abrogation
Some Hanafi scholars claimed the permission was abrogated by the prohibition; the majority refute this because the permission is connected to the prohibition in the same narration (Bukhari).
This shows that even within the Hanafi school, there was discussion.
5
Apply the dispute to a practical case
Take a scenario: a person gifts a palm tree to a needy person, then wants to buy back the fruit with dried dates. Under Hanafi, this is a gift transaction; under Shafi'i, it is a real sale up to five wasaq. The practical difference affects riba and the permissibility of similar exchanges.
The thamarah al-khilaf is in the legal characterization of the transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والمزابنة) هو بالجر في الكل عطفاً على الميتة أي لم يجز بيع المزابنة لنهيه عن بيع المزابنة والمحاقلة. أما المزابنة فقال في الفائق : بيع الثمر في رؤوس النخل بالتمر لأنها تؤدي إلى النزاع والمدافعة من الزبن وهو الدفع. والمحاقلة من الحقل وهو القراح من الأرض وهي الطيبة التربة الخالصة من شائبة للزرع، ومنه حقل يحقل إذا زرع، والمحاقلة مفاعلة ذلك من وهي المزارعة بالثلث أو الربع وغيرهما، وقيل هي اكتراء الأرض بالبر، وقيل بيع الطعام في سنبله بالبر، وقيل بيع الزرع قبل إدراكه، وفي رواية ورخص في العرايا قال العرية النخلة التي يعريها الرجل محتاجاً أي يجعل له ثمرتها فرخص للمعري أن يبتاع ثمرتها من المعرى بتمر لموضع حاجته . سميت عرية لأنه إذا وهب ثمرتها فكأنه جردها من الثمرة وعراها منها ثم اشتق منها الإعراء اهـ. واقتصر في الهداية في تفسير المحاقلة على القول الثالث، وجوز الشافعي بيع المزابنة فيما دون خمسة أوسق لنهيه عن المزابنة ورخص في العرايا وهي أن يباع بخرصها تمراً فيما دون خمسة أوسق وأجاب أصحابنا بأن العرية العطية لغة وتأويله أن المعرى له ما يبيع على النخيل من المعرى بتمر مجذوذ وهو بيع مجاز لأنه لا يملكه فيكون براً مبتدأ؛ كذا في الهداية. وأصحابنا خرجوا عن الظاهر من ثلاثة أوجه الأول إطلاق البيع على الهبة. الثاني قوله رخص يخالف ما قرروه وجوابه أنه رخصة في الوفاء بالوعد والعزيمة أن يفي بالموعود فأعطى غيره مع كونه ليس بإخلاف للوعد رخصة. الثالث التقييد بما دون خمسة أوسق فائدة، وعلى مذهبنا لا فائدة له، وجوابه لأن الواقعة في القليل. ومن مشايخنا من ادعى أن الترخيص في بيع العرايا منسوخ بالنهي عن بيع العرايا ومنهم من قال تعارض المحرم والمبيح فقدم المحرم وهو مردود بأن الرخصة متصلة بالنهي فلا يصح القول بنسخ لترخيص للاتصال، وقد ثبت في البخاري أنه نهى عن بيع المزابنة ثم رخص بعد ذلك في بيع العرايا، فبطل القول بالنسخ والله الموفق. والخرص الحزر. وكذا لا يجوز بيع العنب بالزبيب، ومعنى أنه مال الربا فلا يجوز بيعه بجنسه مع الجهل كما لو كانا موضوعين على الأرض. ثم اعلم أن تعريف المزابنة بأنها بيع الثمر بالتمر خلاف التحقيق، والأولى أن يقال الرطب بتمر إلى آخره لأنه الثمر بالمثلثة حمل الشجر رطباً أو غيره، وإذا لم يكن رطباً جاز لاختلاف الجنس، ولو كان الرطب على الأرض كالتمر لم يجز بيعه متساوياً عند العلماء إلا أبا حنيفة لما سيأتي في باب الربا.
His saying: 'And al-muzabanah' – it is in the accusative in all, coordinated with maitah, meaning: muzabanah sale is not permissible due to the prohibition of muzabanah and muhaqalah. As for muzabanah, it is said in al-Fa'iq: selling fruit on top of palm trees for dried dates, because it leads to dispute and conflict, from zaban, meaning push. And muhaqalah is from haql, which is cultivated land that is good and pure soil, from which haqala yahqulu if he cultivates, and muhaqalah is the mutual form of that, and it is sharecropping by third or fourth, etc., and it is said: renting land for wheat, and it is said: selling food in its ears for wheat, and it is said: selling crops before ripening. And in one narration: 'and he permitted 'araya.' He said: al-'ariyyah is the palm tree that a man gives to a needy person, i.e., he gives him its fruit, so he permitted the giver to buy its fruit from the receiver with dates for his need. It is called 'ariyyah because when he gives its fruit, it is as if he stripped it of the fruit; then 'ira' is derived from it. Al-Hidayah confined the interpretation of muhaqalah to the third opinion. Al-Shafi'i permitted muzabanah for less than five wasaq based on the prohibition of muzabanah, and he permitted 'araya, which is that the fruits are estimated and sold for dates for less than five wasaq. Our companions (Hanafis) responded that 'ariyyah linguistically means gift, and its interpretation is that the giver has what is sold on the palm trees from the receiver for cut dates, and it is a metaphorical sale because the receiver does not own it, so it is a new gift. Our companions deviated from the apparent meaning in three ways: first, using the term 'sale' for gift; second, his saying 'he permitted' contradicts what they established; the answer is that it is a license to fulfill a promise, and the resolve is to fulfill the promised, so giving something else while not breaking the promise is a license; third, the condition of less than five wasaq: according to our madhhab it has no benefit; the answer is that the incident occurred in a small amount. Some of our scholars claimed that the permission for selling 'araya was abrogated by the prohibition of 'araya, and some said the prohibition and permission conflict, so the prohibition is preferred. But this is rejected because the permission is connected to the prohibition, so abrogation cannot be claimed. It is established in Bukhari that he prohibited muzabanah, then later permitted 'araya, so the claim of abrogation is false. And al-kharas is estimation. Similarly, it is not permissible to sell grapes for raisins, meaning that it involves riba, so it is not permissible to sell a genus for the same genus with ignorance, as if both are placed on the ground. Then know that the definition of muzabanah as the sale of fruit for dates is not accurate; rather, it should be said: fresh dates for dried dates, because fruit (thamar) refers to the fruit of the tree, whether fresh or not. When it is not fresh, it is permissible due to different genus. And if fresh dates are on the ground like dried dates, it is not permissible to sell them equally according to most scholars except Abu Hanifa, as will come in the chapter on riba.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage discusses the prohibition of muzabanah (selling fresh fruit on trees for dried dates) and muhaqalah (various forms of exchange involving agricultural produce). These are prohibited because they lead to dispute, uncertainty, and potential riba. The Hanafi school holds that muzabanah is haram, but an exception is made for 'araya, where a person who has gifted a palm tree's fruit to a needy person may buy back the estimated fruit with dried dates to meet the giver's need. However, Hanafis interpret this not as a real sale but as a gift (hibah) metaphorically called a sale. The passage contrasts the Shafi'i view, which permits muzabanah for less than five wasaq based on the hadith of 'araya. The Hanafi response is threefold: the term 'sale' is metaphorical, the permission is a license to fulfill a promise, and the limit of five wasaq is incidental. The passage also clarifies that the prohibition of muzabanah applies specifically to exchanging fresh dates for dried dates, not to dry fruit for dried dates (which is different genus). It further notes that selling grapes for raisins is also prohibited because it involves riba due to ignorance of equivalence. The accurate definition of muzabanah is selling fresh dates (rutab) for dried dates (tamr).

Key Terms
مزابنة (muzabanah): Selling fresh fruit on trees for dried dates of the same type, leading to dispute
عرية (ariyyah): A palm tree whose fruit is gifted to a needy person; the giver may buy it back with dried dates
خرص (kharas): Estimation of the amount of fruit on trees

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how is the 'araya transaction interpreted?
A) As a valid sale.
B) As a gift (hibah) that is metaphorically called a sale.
C) As a void contract.
D) As a conditional sale.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits muzabanah and muhaqalah, and restricts the 'araya exception to a gift transaction, not a sale.

Prohibited Pre-Islamic Sales: Mulamasah, Munabadhah, and Qa' al-Hajar
defines and rules on sales of mulamasah, munabadhah, and qa' al-hajar as prohibited due to uncertainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the three prohibited sales (mulamasah, munabadhah, qa' al-hajar), explain why they are batil in the Hanafi school, and distinguish them from valid sales.

Core Idea
A sale concluded by touching, throwing, or placing a stone is batil because it lacks clear offer and acceptance, leading to gharar.

In pre-Islamic Arabia, certain sales were conducted without proper consent and clarity, relying on physical acts. The Prophet ﷺ prohibited these forms of sales. This lesson defines mulamasah, munabadhah, and qa' al-hajar and explains the Hanafi ruling that they are batil (void).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
For each of the three sales, state the physical act that concludes the sale and the ruling (batil).
Mulamasah: touching; Munabadhah: throwing; Qa' al-Hajar: placing a stone.
2
Understand the reason for prohibition
Explain that these sales are batil because they rely on a contingent event (touching, throwing, stone) without mutual consent and proper offer and acceptance, causing gharar (uncertainty).
The hadith prohibition is the primary evidence.
3
Note the role of prior negotiation
The source mentions that prior conversation about price (al-kalam 'ala al-thaman) is necessary in these sales, but even with prior negotiation, the sale remains batil because the binding act is not proper ijab wa qabul.
Prior negotiation does not salvage the sale.
4
Distinguish from valid sales
Valid sales require clear offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) with mutual consent, no gharar, and certainty of subject matter. Contrast these prohibited sales with a normal sale where terms are agreed before a physical act.
For example, examining an item by touch is not mulamasah if done before a contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والملامسة والقاء الحجر) ومثلها المنابذة وهذه بيوع كانت في الجاهلية فنهى عنها أن يتراوض الرجلان على سلعة أي يتساوما فإذا لمسها المشتري أو نبذها إليه البائع أو وضع المشتري عليها حصاة لزم البيع رضي البائع أو لم يرض، والأول : بيع الملامسة، والثاني بيع المنابذة، والثالث القاء الحجر ولأن فيه تعليقاً بالحظر ولا بد في هذه البيوع أن يسبق - الكلام منهما على الثمن قوله : (وثوب من ثوبين لجهالة المبيع، وتقدم في خيار الشرط أنه القطن بالجواز والأوجه في بيع نوى التمر ولو تمراً بعينه الفساد قوله : أن يبتاع ثمرتها من المعرى بتمر الأول بالثاء المثلثة والمراد به الرطب والثاني بالتاء المثناة . قوله : ولأن فيه تعليقاً بالخطر) فإنه في معنى إذا وقع حجري على ثوب فقد بعته منك أو بعتنيه بكذا أو إذا نبذته أو لمسته؛ كذا في الفتح.
His saying: 'And al-mulamasah and al-qa' al-hajar' and similar is al-munabadhah. These were sales in the Jahiliyyah, so they were forbidden. Two men haggle over a commodity, i.e., they negotiate, then when the buyer touches it, or the seller throws it to him, or the buyer places a pebble on it, the sale is binding whether the seller is pleased or not. The first is the sale of mulamasah, the second is the sale of munabadhah, and the third is qa' al-hajar. And because it involves a conditional suspension of the contract. In these sales, prior conversation about the price is necessary. His saying: 'And a garment from two garments due to ignorance of the sold item, and it was mentioned in the chapter on the option of condition that cotton is permissible, and the most apparent in selling date pits, even if they are in dates, is corruption. His saying: 'that he buys its fruit from the giver with dates' – the first with thartha (ث) meaning rutab, and the second with ta (ت) meaning tamr. His saying: 'and because it involves a conditional suspension of the contract' – it is like if my stone falls on a garment, then I have sold it to you or you sold it to me for such-and-such, or if I threw it or touched it; as in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage defines three prohibited types of sales from the pre-Islamic period: mulamasah (touching the item to conclude the sale), munabadhah (throwing the item to the buyer), and qa' al-hajar (placing a stone or pebble on the item). In all these, the sale becomes binding by a physical action alone, regardless of the seller's consent, which leads to gharar and lack of mutual agreement. The Hanafi ruling is that these are batil (void) because the contract is contingent on an uncertain event. The passage also mentions the sale of 'a garment from two garments' which is invalid due to ignorance of which garment is being sold. A reference is made to cotton being permissible in some contexts, but the focus remains on the prohibition. The passage ends with a note that the sale is like saying 'if my stone falls on a garment, I have sold it to you' – a clear condition that invalidates the contract.

Key Terms
ملامسة (mulamasah): A sale concluded by the buyer touching the item, making it binding unconditionally
منابذة (munabadhah): A sale concluded by the seller throwing the item to the buyer
إلقاء الحجر (ilqa' al-hajar): A sale concluded by throwing a pebble or stone onto the item of sale

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale of mulamasah, when does the contract become binding?
A) When the buyer touches the item.
B) When the buyer and seller agree on price.
C) When the buyer throws a stone at the item.
D) It is never binding.

Key Takeaway
The three prohibited sales (mulamasah, munabadhah, qa' al-hajar) are batil due to lack of proper offer and acceptance and reliance on uncertain events.

Inter-Madhhab Dispute on 'Araya: Sale or Gift?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school interprets the concession of 'araya as a gift rather than a sale, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school preserves the prohibition of muzabanah by interpreting the 'araya concession as a gift, not a sale.

The prohibition of muzabanah—selling fresh dates for dried dates—is well-known. However, a hadith grants a concession for 'araya. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i position that 'araya is a valid sale, showing how the Hanafis depart from the apparent meaning to uphold the principle.

Guided Path
1
State the prohibition and the concession
Muzabanah is the sale of fresh fruit on the tree for dried fruit of the same kind, prohibited due to riba and gharar. The hadith in Bukhari says the Prophet forbade muzabanah and then permitted 'araya.
The concession is specific to 'araya, defined as a palm tree given to a needy person.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i takes the apparent meaning: the concession allows selling fresh dates by estimation for dried dates up to five wasqs. This is a direct permission to do what was prohibited.
The Shafi'i view hinges on the word 'sale' in the hadith.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Our companions (Hanafis) depart from the apparent in three ways: (1) 'Sale' is a metaphor for gift; (2) 'Concession' means relaxation to fulfill a promise, not a sale; (3) The five wasqs limit is specific to the incident, not a general rule.
These departures reconcile the hadith with the principle of avoiding riba.
4
Refute the claim of abrogation
Some claimed the permission was abrogated by the prohibition, but the author refutes this because the concession is directly connected to the prohibition in the same narration, so it remains valid.
The connection (ittisal) prevents abrogation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب البيع الفاسد
القانص والمزابنة والملامسة والقاء الحجر وثوب من ثوبين والمراعي وإجارتها والنحل ويباع دود بالثلث أو الربع وغيرهما، وقيل هي اكتراء الأرض بالبر، وقيل بيع الطعام في سنبله بالبر، وقيل بيع الزرع قبل إدراكه، وفي رواية ورخص في العرايا قال العرية النخلة التي يعريها الرجل محتاجاً أي يجعل له ثمرتها فرخص للمعري أن يبتاع ثمرتها من المعرى بتمر لموضع حاجته . سميت عرية لأنه إذا وهب ثمرتها فكأنه جردها من الثمرة وعراها منها ثم اشتق منها الإعراء اهـ. واقتصر في الهداية في تفسير المحاقلة على القول الثالث، وجوز الشافعي بيع المزابنة فيما دون خمسة أوسق لنهيه عن المزابنة ورخص في العرايا وهي أن يباع بخرصها تمراً فيما دون خمسة أوسق وأجاب أصحابنا بأن العرية العطية لغة وتأويله أن المعرى له ما يبيع على النخيل من المعرى بتمر مجذوذ وهو بيع مجاز لأنه لا يملكه فيكون براً مبتدأ؛ كذا في الهداية. وأصحابنا خرجوا عن الظاهر من ثلاثة أوجه الأول إطلاق البيع على الهبة. الثاني قوله رخص يخالف ما قرروه وجوابه أنه رخصة في الوفاء بالوعد والعزيمة أن يفي بالموعود فأعطى غيره مع كونه ليس بإخلاف للوعد رخصة. الثالث التقييد بما دون خمسة أوسق فائدة، وعلى مذهبنا لا فائدة له، وجوابه لأن الواقعة في القليل. ومن مشايخنا من ادعى أن الترخيص في بيع العرايا منسوخ بالنهي عن بيع العرايا ومنهم من قال تعارض المحرم والمبيح فقدم المحرم وهو مردود بأن الرخصة متصلة بالنهي فلا يصح القول بنسخ لترخيص للاتصال، وقد ثبت في البخاري أنه نهى عن بيع المزابنة ثم رخص بعد ذلك في بيع العرايا، فبطل القول بالنسخ والله الموفق. والخرص الحزر. وكذا لا يجوز بيع العنب بالزبيب، ومعنى أنه مال الربا فلا يجوز بيعه بجنسه مع الجهل كما لو كانا موضوعين على الأرض. ثم اعلم أن تعريف المزابنة بأنها بيع الثمر بالتمر خلاف التحقيق، والأولى أن يقال الرطب بتمر إلى آخره لأنه الثمر بالمثلثة حمل الشجر رطباً أو غيره، وإذا لم يكن رطباً جاز لاختلاف الجنس، ولو كان الرطب على الأرض كالتمر لم يجز بيعه متساوياً عند العلماء إلا أبا حنيفة لما سيأتي في باب الربا.
The Book of Sales, Chapter on Invalid Sales. Al-Muhaqalah, al-Muzabanah, al-Mulamasah, the throwing of the stone, a garment from two garments, pastures and their leasing, and the bee (??) and it is said it is leasing land with wheat, and it is said it is selling food in its ears with wheat, and it is said it is selling the crop before it ripens. And in a narration: 'And a concession was granted in 'araya.' He said: 'Al-'ariyyah is a palm tree which a man gives to a needy person, i.e., makes its fruit for him. So the giver is permitted to buy its fruit from the recipient with dried dates due to his need. It is called 'ariyyah because when he gives its fruit, it is as if he stripped the tree of its fruit and bared it; then derived from it al-i'ra'.' Al-Hidayah confined the interpretation of al-muhaqalah to the third opinion. Al-Shafi'i permitted the sale of muzabanah for less than five wasqs due to his prohibition of muzabanah and the concession in 'araya, which is that it is sold by estimation with dried dates for less than five wasqs. Our companions answered that 'ariyyah linguistically means a gift, and its interpretation is that the recipient has what he sells from the palm trees to the giver with cut dried dates, which is a figurative sale because he does not own it, so it is an initial act of devotion. Our companions departed from the apparent in three ways: first, applying the term 'sale' to a gift; second, his statement 'conceded' contradicts what they established, and the answer is that it is a concession for fulfilling a promise, and the determination is to fulfill the promise, so he gave someone else, and since it is not a breach of promise, it is a concession; third, the restriction to less than five wasqs has a benefit on their view, but on our view it has no benefit, and the answer is because the incident occurred in small quantities. Among our teachers are those who claimed that the permission to sell 'araya was abrogated by the prohibition of selling 'araya, and among them are those who said that the forbidden and the permissive conflicted, so the forbidden was given precedence, but this is rejected because the concession is connected to the prohibition, so it is not correct to say abrogation due to connection. It is established in al-Bukhari that he forbade the sale of muzabanah then later conceded in the sale of 'araya, so the claim of abrogation is invalid. And Allah is the Provider of success. And al-kharas is estimation. Similarly, selling grapes with raisins is not permissible, meaning it is property of riba, so it is not permissible to sell it with its kind with ignorance, as if both were on the ground. Then know that the definition of muzabanah as selling fruit with dried dates is contrary to verification; it is better to say fresh dates with dried dates, etc., because 'thamar' (with thaa) refers to the fruit of the tree, fresh or otherwise, and if it is not fresh, it is permissible due to difference of kind. And if the fresh dates are on the ground like dried dates, it is not permissible to sell them equally according to the scholars except Abu Hanifah, as will come in the Chapter on Riba.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the chapter on invalid sales, focusing on the definition and ruling of muzabanah and the concession of 'araya. The author first lists several types of invalid sales, then explains al-muhaqalah briefly but moves quickly to al-muzabanah. The main discussion is the inter-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i regarding the concession of 'araya. The Hanafi position is that 'araya is a gift (hibah) in origin, not a sale, so the apparent concession is actually a permission to fulfill a promise by giving dried dates in exchange for fresh dates, which is not a sale but a gift. The author refutes three objections: (1) calling it a sale is figurative, (2) the word 'concession' is explained as a relaxation in fulfilling a promise, (3) the restriction to five wasqs is specific to the incident. Then the author addresses the claim of abrogation, showing that the concession is still valid because it is connected to the prohibition. After the dispute, the author clarifies the definition of muzabanah: it is selling fresh dates (ruttab) for dried dates (tamr), not fruit (thamar) for tamr, because fruit includes fresh and dry. The ruling on selling grapes with raisins is also mentioned as impermissible due to riba, except for a known exception of Abu Hanifah when the fresh fruit is on the ground.

Key Terms
المزابنة (al-muzabanah): Sale of fresh fruit on the tree for dried fruit of the same kind, prohibited due to riba and gharar.
العرية (al-'ariyyah): A palm tree given to a needy person, allowing the giver to buy back the fruit with dried dates.
الخرص (al-kharas): Estimation of the fruit on the tree to determine the amount of dried dates to be exchanged.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A valid sale up to five wasqs.
B) A gift transaction.
C) An abrogated ruling.
D) A temporary permission only for the companions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds the prohibition of muzabanah by reinterpreting the 'araya concession as a gift, not a sale.

The Invalidity of Sales Based on Chance and Ambiguity
Detailing the invalidity of sales based on chance and ambiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three prohibited chance-based sales and explain why they are invalid, and also explain the ruling and legal consequences of selling an unspecified item (bay' al-mubham) in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Sales based on chance (gharar) or ambiguity (jahalah) are invalid in Hanafi fiqh.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies three pre-Islamic transactions (mulamasah, munabadhah, ilqa' al-hajar) that were prohibited because they make the sale binding upon a chance action rather than mutual agreement. Additionally, it addresses the sale of an unspecified item from a set (e.g., one of two garments), which is invalid due to ignorance of the subject matter. Understanding these rulings helps guard against contracts involving prohibited uncertainty and clarifies the legal effects if possession is taken under such an invalid sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Prohibited Sales
From the passage, list the three types: al-mulamasah (buyer touches the item to bind), al-munabadhah (seller throws the item to bind), and ilqa' al-hajar (buyer throws a stone to select).
These were pre-Islamic customs.
2
Understand the Legal Basis for Invalidity
The passage states these sales involve a suspensive condition (ta'liq) on chance (khatar), leading to gharar (uncertainty). Also, they lack proper consent. This makes the transaction void (fasid/haram).
The primary reason is gharar and ta'liq.
3
Recognize the Requirement of Prior Price Discussion
The author emphasizes that for these sales, there must be prior speech (al-kalam) between the parties regarding the price. Without it, the sale is even more defective.
This ensures some level of certainty.
4
Apply the Ruling to Ambiguous Sales (Bay' al-Mubham)
The passage then discusses selling one unspecified item from two (e.g., a garment from two garments, a slave from two slaves). Such sales are invalid due to ignorance (jahalah) of the subject matter. The Hanafi position is that it is not permissible except perhaps with an option of specification for less than three.
This extends the principle of avoiding ambiguity.
5
Examine Legal Consequences if Possession is Taken
If the buyer takes possession of both items under an invalid sale, specific rules apply: if both perish together, he guarantees half the value of each; if they die sequentially, he guarantees the first; if he frees one, it may be valid under conditions. These rulings show that even an invalid sale has legal effects regarding possession and liability.
The invalid sale is compared to a valid sale in terms of guarantee.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The prohibition, which is the sale. His saying: '(And al-Mulamasah and the throwing of the stone)' and similar to them is al-Munabadhah. The first is the sale of touching, the second is the sale of throwing, the third is the throwing of the stone. And because it involves a suspensive condition on chance. For this reason, if the sale was valid, such as when the buyer has an option, he guarantees half the price of each. If they die in sequence, he guarantees the value of the first because it became determined as guaranteed due to inability to return it. If he frees both together, one becomes free because he owns one by possession. If he takes possession of both, he owns one and the other is a trust fulfilling the contract...' etc.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains three types of invalid sales: al-mulamasah (touching), al-munabadhah (throwing), and ilqa' al-hajar (throwing a stone). These were pre-Islamic practices that make the sale binding upon the action without proper consent, thus involving gharar (uncertainty) and ta'liq (suspensive condition). The author also discusses the sale of a garment from two garments or a slave from two slaves (bay' al-mubham), which is invalid due to ignorance of the specific item. A detailed discussion follows on the consequences if the buyer takes possession of both items: if they perish together, he guarantees half the value of each; if one perishes after the other, he guarantees the first; if he frees one, it may be valid under certain conditions. The ruling is nuanced: the sale is invalid, but contracts of gharar may still have some legal effects regarding possession and guarantee.

Key Terms
الملامسة (al-mulamasah): A sale where the buyer touches the item to make the sale binding.
المنابذة (al-munabadhah): A sale where the seller throws the item to the buyer to make it binding.
ثوب من ثوبين (thawb min thawbayn): Selling one unspecified garment from two, leading to ambiguity about the subject matter.
بيع المبهم (bay' al-mubham): An ambiguous sale where the subject matter is not specified.
التعليق بالخطر (al-ta'liq bi al-khatar): Suspensive condition based on chance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because it is based on a pre-Islamic custom.

Key Takeaway
Sales that depend on chance (mulamasah, munabadhah, ilqa' al-hajar) or involve ambiguity (bay' al-mubham) are invalid in Hanafi fiqh, and if possession is taken under such sales, specific liability rules apply.

Selling and Leasing Common Pasture is Invalid
Establishing the invalidity of selling or leasing common pasture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling and leasing common pasture and explain the two proofs: the hadith of common partnership and the qiyas on consuming non-owned objects.

Core Idea
Selling common pasture is haram because it violates the community's shared right to the herbage.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a classic fiqh question: can you sell or lease the vegetation growing on common land? The answer is no, because the herbage (kalā') is not owned by any individual; it is a common resource. The author provides two independent proofs: a direct hadith and an analogy (qiyas) for leasing. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
What is being ruled upon (selling and leasing pasture vegetation) and what is the hukm (impermissible/haram)?
Note the distinction between selling the vegetation and selling the land itself.
2
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith 'People are partners in three: water, pasture, and fire' establishes common ownership. Selling pasture to an individual violates this partnership.
The hadith is ahad but sufficient for prohibition.
3
Analyze the qiyas for leasing
Leasing pasture is analogized to hiring a cow to drink its milk: both involve paying for consumption of a non-owned (or common) object. The asl (hiring for milk) is invalid, so the far' (leasing pasture) is even more clearly invalid.
The illa is consumption of something without proper ownership rights.
4
Distinguish the vegetation from the land
Selling or leasing the land itself is permissible; only the vegetation (herbage) is common.
This avoids confusion.
5
Understand the scope of common partnership
The hadith also applies to water (drinking, watering animals) and fire (warming, drying clothes) but not to taking embers without permission.
The partnership is limited to use, not ownership of the substance itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والمراعي وإجارتها) أي لا يجوز بيع الكلأ وإجارته، أما البيع فلأنه ورد على ما لا يملكه لاشتراك الناس فيه بالحديث الناس شركاء في ثلاث في الماء والكلأ والنار»(۱) وأما الإجارة فلأنها عقدت على استهلاك عين مباح، ولو عقدت على استهلاك عين مملوكة بأن استأجر بقرة ليشرب لبنها لا يجوز فهذا أولى. وفي المصباح والرعي بالكسر والمرعى بمعنى وهو ما ترعاه الدواب والجمع المراعي .اهـ. قيد بالمراعي بمعنى الكلأ لأن رقبة الأرض بيع وإجارتها جائزان ومعنى الشركة في النار الاصطلاء بها، وتجفيف الثياب يعني إذا أوقد رجل ناراً فلكل أن يصطلي بها، أما إذا أراد أن يأخذ الجمر فليس له ذلك إلا بإذن صاحبه. ومعناه الماء الشرب وسقي الدواب والاستقاء من الآبار والحيض والأنهار المملوكة، وفي الكلأ أن له احتشاشه وإن كان في أرض مملوكة غير أن لصاحب الأرض أن يمنع من الدخول في
His saying: '(And pastures and their leasing)' i.e., selling the herbage and leasing it is not permissible. As for sale, because it is directed at what is not owned due to people's common share in it according to the hadith: 'People are partners in three: water, herbage, and fire.' As for leasing, because it is contracted for the consumption of a permissible object, and if it were contracted for the consumption of an owned object, such as hiring a cow to drink its milk, it is not permissible, so this is even more so. In al-Misbah: 'al-Ra'y (with kasr) and al-Mar'a are synonymous, meaning what animals graze, plural al-Marā'ī.' This is restricted to pastures meaning herbage, because selling and leasing the land itself is permissible. The meaning of partnership in fire is warming oneself and drying clothes, i.e., if a man lights a fire, everyone may warm themselves, but if he wants to take embers, he may not do so except with the owner's permission. Its meaning regarding water is drinking, watering animals, and drawing from wells, tanks, and owned rivers. Regarding herbage, he may gather it even if it is on owned land, except that the landowner may prevent entry into

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on selling and leasing pasture (al-marā'ī). It is not permissible to sell or lease the vegetation (kalā') because it is common property shared by all people, as evidenced by the hadith 'People are partners in three: water, pasture, and fire.' Leasing it is also invalid because it involves the consumption of a non-owned object, analogous to hiring a cow to drink its milk, which is not allowed. However, selling or leasing the land itself (the root) is permissible. The author clarifies the meanings of the common partnership: in fire, one may warm oneself but not take embers; in water, one may drink and water animals but not take ownership; in pasture, one may gather it even on owned land, but the landowner may prevent entry into his land.

Key Terms
المراعي (al-marā'ī): Pasture; the herbage that animals graze.
الكلأ (al-kalā'): Herbage, pasture vegetation.
الرعي (al-ra'y): Grazing, the act of pasturing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Qiyas on water.
B) The hadith 'People are partners in three'.
C) Ijma.
D) The need of the community.

Key Takeaway
Selling or leasing common pasture is haram because the herbage is collectively owned by all people.

Rulings on Khiyar al-Ta'yin – Consequences of an Invalid Sale
Rulings with proof: consequences of khiyar al-ta'yin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-ta'yin to scenarios of death, emancipation, and possession of multiple items.

Core Idea
When the buyer takes possession of both items, one is guaranteed and the other a trust; the invalid sale is analogized to a valid one.

The option of specification (khiyar al-ta'yin) allows the buyer to choose one of two items. However, in Hanafi law, specifying one of two in the contract is invalid due to ignorance. Yet, legal consequences arise if the buyer takes possession of both. This lesson unpacks those consequences through clear analogy to a valid sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State that khiyar al-ta'yin for one of two items is fasid (invalid) because of jahalah (ignorance). This is the foundation for all subsequent rules.
There is no dispute among scholars on this invalidity.
2
Understand the analogy (qiyas)
The invalid sale is analogized to a valid sale: 'al-fasid mu'tabar bil-sahih'. The consequences are derived by comparing to a valid sale with a buyer's option.
Value (qimah) in the invalid sale corresponds to price (thaman) in a valid sale.
3
Apply to scenarios of loss and emancipation
If both die together, half the value of each is guaranteed. If successively, only the first is guaranteed. For emancipation, only one becomes free if done simultaneously; sequential emancipation by buyer and seller results in both being free.
The explanation depends on which item is determined as guaranteed.
4
Distinguish between mithli and qimi
The ruling applies only to non-fungibles (qimiyyat). For fungibles (mithliyyat), the sale of an unspecified item is valid, so different rules apply.
This distinction is crucial for application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا جعل للمشتري خيار التعيين جاز فيما دون الثلاثة فلذا أطلقه هنا. وفي المعراج : وكذا عبد من عبدين لا يجوز، ولا خلاف فيه لأحد حتى لو قبضهما وماتا معاً يضمن نصف قيمة كل واحد منهما لأن أحدهما مضمون بالقيمة لأنه مقبوض بحكم البيع الفاسد والآخر أمانة وليس أحدهما بأولى من الآخر فشاعت الأمانة والضمان، ولهذا لو كان البيع صحيحاً بأن كان فيه خيار المشتري يضمن نصف ثمن كل واحد والفاسد معتبر بالصحيح والقيمة هنا كالثمن ثمة ولو ماتا مرتبين ضمن قيمة الأول لأنه تعين مضموناً لتعذر الرد فيه، ولو حررهما معاً عتق أحدهما لأنه ملك أحدهما بالقبض، وإن حرر أحدهما لم يصح أي لو قال البائع أو المشتري أحدهما حر ولو قالا متعاقباً عتقا لأن كل واحد أعتق ملكه وملك غيره فيصح في ملكه والبيان إلى المشتري لأن من نفذ فيه عتقه مضمون بالقيمة والقول في المضمون قول الضامن، ولو قبض أحدهما بإذن البائع فهلك غرم قيمته اهـ. وقيد بالقيمي إذ بيع المبهم في المثلي جائز . قال في التلخيص من باب بيع المبهم : لو اشترى أحد عبدين أو ثوبين فسد لجهل يورث نزاعها ضد المثلي، فلو قبضهما ملك أحدهما والآخر أمانة وفاء بالعهد إلى آخره.
If the buyer is given the option of specification, it is permissible for less than three (days?), so it was left unrestricted here. In Al-Mi'raj: likewise, one of two slaves is not permissible, and there is no dispute on this. Even if he takes possession of both and they die together, he guarantees half the value of each, because one of them is guaranteed by value as it is taken under an invalid sale, and the other is a trust; neither has priority over the other, so trust and guarantee intermingle. For this reason, if the sale were valid (with a buyer's option), he would guarantee half the price of each; the invalid is considered by the valid, and value here is like price there. If they die successively, he guarantees the value of the first because it became determined as guaranteed due to inability to return it. If he emancipates both together, one of them is free because he owns one by possession. If he emancipates one, it is not valid; i.e., if the seller or buyer says 'one of them is free' — if they say so successively, both become free because each freed his own property and the property of another, so it is valid in his own property. The explanation is given to the buyer because the one whose emancipation is executed is guaranteed by value, and in matters of guarantee, the word of the guarantor is taken. If he takes possession of one with the seller's permission and it perishes, he pays its value. It is restricted to non-fungibles (qimiyyat) because the sale of unspecified in fungibles (mithliyyat) is permissible. It is said in Al-Talkhis from the chapter of unspecified sale: If one buys one of two slaves or two garments, the sale is invalid due to ignorance that leads to dispute, as opposed to fungibles. Then if he takes possession of both, he owns one and the other is a trust for fulfilling the contract, etc.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the consequences of a sale where the buyer has the option to specify one of two items (khiyar al-ta'yin). The Hanafi position is that such a sale for an unspecified item among two is fasid (invalid) due to ignorance (jahalah). The passage then explains the legal effects: if the buyer takes possession of both items, he owns one (though his ownership is determined only after specification), and the other is held as an amanah (trust). If both die, he guarantees half the value of each because the guarantee and trust are intermingled. If they die successively, only the first is guaranteed (since it was determined as guaranteed upon its death). If both are emancipated together, one becomes free; if one is emancipated alone, it is not valid. The passage also distinguishes between fungible (mithli) and non-fungible (qimi) goods, noting that the sale of an unspecified fungible is valid. This is all based on analogy (qiyas) to a valid sale, as the invalid is considered equivalent to the valid in terms of consequences.

Key Terms
خيار التعيين (khiyar al-ta'yin): The option given to the buyer to specify which of two or more items he intends to purchase.
مبهم (mubham): An unspecified item from a set; its sale is invalid due to ignorance causing dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Half the value of each.
D) The price of the cheaper one.

Key Takeaway
The invalid sale with khiyar al-ta'yin imposes a guarantee of half the value of each item if both perish together, analogized from a valid sale.

Prohibition of Selling and Leasing Grass and Common Property
Ruling with proof on prohibition of selling grass

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling wild grass, cite the hadith proof, distinguish the preferred view on cultivated grass, and apply the concepts of common property (mubah) and possession (hiyazah).

Core Idea
Selling unowned common property like wild grass is haram; it becomes owned only by possession or cultivation effort.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the sale and lease of grass (kala') and similar common resources. The source text from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the prohibition based on the hadith of shared resources, and then discusses the nuanced exception when the land is cultivated. You will learn the primary ruling, its proof, and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on cultivated grass.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is selling or leasing grass (kala') that is still attached and unpossessed. The ruling stated in the text is that it is not permissible (la yajuz), i.e., haram.
The prohibition applies to the grass itself, not the land.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith: 'الناس شركاء في ثلاث في الماء والكلأ والنار' (People are partners in water, grass, and fire). This is a hadith ahad (single narration) but its meaning is clear.
Reference: Abu Dawud, Ibn Majah, Ahmad.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit declaration of partnership (shirkah) indicates that these items are common property (mubah) owned collectively. Therefore, no individual may sell or lease them as their own property.
The hadith's text uses the word 'shuraka'' (partners), implying shared ownership.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on cultivated grass
There is a difference of opinion when the land is prepared and grass grows due to cultivation. Al-Dhakhira maintains the prohibition, while Al-Muhit and Al-Nawazil allow sale because the cultivator owns it. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is permissibility.
The majority, including al-Sadr al-Shahid, support the permissive view.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The later authorities (Fath al-Qadir, etc.) favour ownership when effort is expended, analogizing to digging a well. The textual operation is expansion: the base ruling is expanded to include an exception.
The legal trick (hila) for leasing is to frame it as a lease of land, not grass.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والمراعي وإجارتها) أي لا يجوز بيع الكلأ وإجارته، أما البيع فلأنه ورد على ما لا يملكه لاشتراك الناس فيه بالحديث الناس شركاء في ثلاث في الماء والكلأ والنار» وأما الإجارة فلأنها عقدت على استهلاك عين مباح، ولو عقدت على استهلاك عين مملوكة بأن استأجر بقرة ليشرب لبنها لا يجوز فهذا أولى. وفي المصباح والرعي بالكسر والمرعى بمعنى وهو ما ترعاه الدواب والجمع المراعي .اهـ. قيد بالمراعي بمعنى الكلأ لأن رقبة الأرض بيع وإجارتها جائزان ومعنى الشركة في النار الاصطلاء بها، وتجفيف الثياب يعني إذا أوقد رجل ناراً فلكل أن يصطلي بها، أما إذا أراد أن يأخذ الجمر فليس له ذلك إلا بإذن صاحبه. ومعناه الماء الشرب وسقي الدواب والاستقاء من الآبار والحيض والأنهار المملوكة، وفي الكلأ أن له احتشاشه وإن كان في أرض مملوكة غير أن لصاحب الأرض أن يمنع من الدخول في أرضه، وإذا منع فلغيره أن يقول أن لي في أرضك حقاً فإما أن توصلني إليه أو تحشه أو تستقي وتدفعه لي وصار كثوب رجل وقع في دار رجل إما أن يأذن المالك في دخوله ليأخذه، وإما أن يخرجه إليه أما إذا أحرز الماء بالاستقاء في آنية والكلأ بقطعه جاز حينئذ بيعه لأنه ملكه بذلك، وظاهر أن هذا إذا نبت بنفسه، فأما إذا كان سقى الأرض وأعدها للإنبات فنبت ففي الذخيرة لا والمحيط والنوازل يجوز بيعه لأنه ملكه وهو مختار الصدر الشهيد، وكذا ذكر في اختلاف أبي حنيفة فيحمل كلام المصنف على ما إذا لم يعدها للإنبات، ومنه لو حدق حول أرضه وهيأها للإنبات حتى نبت القصب صار ملكاً له. والقدوري منع بيعه. وإن ساق الماء إلى أرضه ولحقه مؤنة لبقاء الشركة وإنما تنقطع بالحيازة وسوق الماء إلى أرضه ليس بحيازة لكن الأكثر على الأول إلا أن على هذا القائل أن يقول ينبغي إن حاز البئر يملك بناءها ويكون بتكلفه الحفر والطي لتحصيل الماء يملك الماء كما يملك الكلأ بتكلفه سوق الماء إلى الأرض لينبت فله منع المستقي وإن لم يكن في أرض مملوكة له؛ كذا في فتح القدير، وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى بقية الكلام عليه في كتاب الشرب والحيلة في جواز إجارته أن يستأجرها أرضاً لإيقاف الدواب فيها أو لمنفعة أخرى بقدر ما يريد صاحبه من الثمن أو الأجرة فيحصل به غرضهما. ويدخل في الكلأ جميع أنواع ما ترعاه المواشي رطباً كان أو يابساً بخلاف الأشجار لأن الكلأ ما لا ساق له والشجر له ساق فلا تدخل فيه حتى يجوز بيعها إذا نبتت في أرضه لكونها ملكه والكمأة كالكلأ وفي القاموس : الكم نبات والكمأة للواحد والكمؤ للجمع أو هي تكون واحدة وجمعاً اهـ.
His statement: 'And pastures (al-marāʿī) and their lease' meaning: the sale of grass (kala') and its lease are not permissible. As for sale, it is because it is upon what he does not own due to people's common share in it by the hadith: 'People are partners in three: water, grass, and fire.' As for lease, it is because it is contracted for consumption of a mubah (unowned) object; if it were contracted for consumption of an owned object, such as hiring a cow to drink its milk, it is not permissible, so this is more so. In Al-Misbah: 'raʿy' (with kasra) and 'marʿā' are synonymous, meaning what animals graze, plural 'marāʿī'. The restriction to pastures (marāʿī) means grass (kala'), because the sale and lease of the land itself are permissible. The meaning of partnership in fire is using it for warmth and drying clothes: if a man lights a fire, anyone may warm himself, but if he wants to take embers, he may not without permission. Its meaning for water is drinking and watering animals, drawing from wells, and [using] owned rivers and streams. For grass, it means one may gather it even if on owned land, but the landowner may prevent entry. If he prevents, another may say: 'I have a right to your land; either you let me reach it, or you gather/water and give it to me.' It becomes like a garment that falls into someone's house: either the owner permits entry to retrieve it, or he returns it. However, if one takes possession of water by drawing it into a vessel, or grass by cutting it, then its sale becomes permissible because he owns it by that. This is apparent when it grows naturally. But if he irrigates the land and prepares it for growth, and grass grows, then according to Al-Dhakhira it is not permissible, but according to Al-Muhit and Al-Nawazil it is permissible because he owns it; this is the choice of al-Sadr al-Shahid. Similarly, it is mentioned in Ikhtilaf Abi Hanifa, so the author's statement is interpreted as when he did not prepare for growth. Included is if he surrounds his land and prepares it for growth until reeds grow, they become his property. Al-Quduri prohibited its sale. If he brings water to his land and incurs expense, the partnership remains; it is only cut off by possession (hiyazah), and bringing water to land is not possession. But the majority hold the first view, except that this proponent should say: if one digs a well, he owns its structure, and by his effort of digging and lining to obtain water, he owns the water, just as he owns grass by his effort of bringing water to land to grow it; so he may prevent the drawer even if it is not on his owned land. This is in Fath al-Qadir. The rest of the discussion will come in the chapter on irrigation (kitab al-shurb). The legal trick (hila) to allow its lease is to rent it as land for stopping animals or other benefit according to the amount of price or rent desired by its owner, achieving both goals. Grass includes all types that animals graze, fresh or dry, unlike trees because grass has no trunk; trees have trunks, so they are not included; thus their sale is permissible if they grow on his land because he owns them. Truffles (kam'a) are like grass. In Al-Qamus: 'kamm' is a plant; 'kam'ah' is singular, 'kamu' plural, or it can be both singular and plural.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on the sale and lease of grass (kala') and other common resources. The author states plainly that both are prohibited (la yajuz). The proof is the famous hadith that people are partners in water, grass, and fire — meaning these are common property (mubah) shared by all, hence no individual may sell or lease them as their own. Leasing grass is even more severely prohibited because it involves the consumption of a mubah object, similar to hiring a cow to drink its milk, which is also prohibited. The passage then explains the scope: the terms 'marʿā' and 'kala' are synonymous. The prohibition applies only to the grass itself, not the land; land can be sold or leased. The hadith's mention of 'fire' means using it for warmth, not taking embers. For water, it covers drinking and watering animals, drawing from wells, and using rivers. The landowner can prevent entry, but if he prevents, the person has the right to demand access or collection. Once grass is cut or water is drawn into a vessel, it becomes owned and can be sold. However, if the land is cultivated and prepared for growth, there is ikhtilaf: Al-Dhakhira says the grass remains common, while Al-Muhit and Al-Nawazil say it becomes owned. The majority, including al-Sadr al-Shahid, support ownership. The commentator leans towards the view that cultivation with effort creates ownership, analogizing to digging a well. A legal trick (hila) for leasing is to frame it as a lease of the land for a specific purpose, such as stopping animals. Finally, grass includes all types of forage, fresh or dry, but not trees. Truffles (kam'a) are treated like grass.

Key Terms
كلأ (kala'): The herbage or grass that animals graze, fresh or dry.
مرعى (marʿā): Pasture; the place where animals graze, often synonymous with kala' in this context.
شركة في ثلاث (shirkah fi thalath): The legal principle that water, grass, and fire are common property shared by all people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith mentioned, what three things are people partners in?
A) Water, wood, and fire
B) Water, grass, and fire
C) Water, grass, and land
D) Grass, fire, and trees

Key Takeaway
Wild grass is common property; selling it is haram. However, if one cultivates the land and grass grows, it becomes owned and sale is permissible according to the preferred Hanafi view.

Honey and Water: Ownership through Possession in Hanafi Fiqh
Intra-Hanafi dispute on honey sale and water ownership rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale of honey and the ownership of well water, and explain the role of physical possession (hiyazah) in acquiring ownership of mubah objects.

Core Idea
Honey and water in nature are mubah; ownership requires physical possession, not just digging or effort.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses two interconnected fiqh issues: the permissibility of selling honey (tahl) and the ownership of water in a well. Underlying both is the Hanafi principle that mubah (common property) becomes owned only through physical possession (hiyazah). The text presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on honey and clarifies the rule for water, distinguishing between natural wells and constructed cisterns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling honey (tahl) before it is collected. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, such a sale is haram. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan permits it.
Discipline: Fiqh (buyu' – sales).
2
Understand the analogy to grass (kala')
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf analogize honey to grass: both are mubah until possessed. Watering grass causes it to grow, but honey is not directly produced by the beekeeper's effort; bees collect it from common flowers.
The differentiation: effort (sowing) creates ownership in grass; honey remains common until collected.
3
State the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the majority view, one cannot sell honey while it is still in the comb; it must first be collected and possessed. Under Muhammad's view, sale in the comb is permissible.
The mufta bihi position is the majority view (prohibition).
4
Apply the same principle to water
The well owner does not own the water in the well. Water becomes owned only upon extraction (possession in a container). Digging the well does not confer ownership because water existed naturally before digging.
Exception: water in cisterns (saharij) is owned because it is collected and possessed.
5
Distinguish between well (bi'r mu'ayyan) and cistern (sahrij)
A well contains natural water; a cistern is constructed to hold collected water. The Hanafi rule applies only to natural water sources; possessed water in containers is owned and its like must be returned if taken without permission.
Al-Walwaljiya: drawing water from a well without permission incurs no liability; pouring water from a container requires compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والتحل) أي لم يجز بيعه وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد: يجوز في النهر وأقول : يمكن أن يفرق بينهما بأن سقى الكلأ كان سبباً في إنباته فنبت بخلاف الماء فإنه موجود قبل حفره فلا يملكه بالحفر .اهـ. وقال الرملي : أصح القولين عند الشافعي أنه يملكه، سواء حفرها في أرض موات أو ملك، وعندنا لا يملكه فيهما وأقول : المنقول أن صاحب البئر لا يملك الماء وقدمه هذا الشارح في كتاب الطهارة في شرح قوله وانتفاخ حيوان وتفسخه عن الولوالجية فراجعه. وهذا ما دام في البئر أما إذا أخرجه منها بالاحتيال كما . في السواقي التي ببلادنا فلا شك ملكه له بذلك لحيازته له في الكيزان التي نسميها القواديس أولاً ثم صبه في البرك بعد حيازته تأمل وأقول : البئر في كلام الفقهاء غالباً للمعين وأما غيره فيقال فيه صهريج وجب ونحو ذلك، وقد يطلق على غير المعين والذي يجب التعويل عليه في الماء أن يقال بالحيازة يملك فيضمن وعلى يملك في الصهاريج المتخذة في البيوت للحيازة قطعاً لأنها بمنزلة الحباب، وقد أفتيت في هذا يجب أن به ولا يخالفه ما في الولوالجية من قوله : ولو نزح ماء بئر رجل بغير إذنه حتى يبست لا شيء عليه لأن صاحب البئر غير مالك للماء، ولو صب ماء رجل كان في الحب يقال له املأ الماء لأن صاحب الحب مالك للماء وهو من ذوات الأمثال فيضمن مثله .اهـ لأن كلامه في البئر المعين، وأما
His statement: 'And honey (al-tahl)' means: its sale is not permissible, and this is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: it is permissible. In Al-Nahr, I say: it is possible to differentiate between them by [the fact that] watering the grass was a cause for its growth, so it grew, unlike water, for it existed before digging, so one does not own it by digging. Al-Ramli said: The more correct of the two opinions according to al-Shafi'i is that he owns it, whether he dug it in dead land or owned land; according to us (Hanafis), he does not own it in either case. I say: The transmitted position is that the owner of a well does not own the water; this has been presented by this commentator in the book of Taharah when explaining the statement 'and the swelling of an animal and its decay' from Al-Walwaljiya, so refer to it. This is as long as it is in the well. However, if he extracts it by means (such as in the waterwheels in our country), there is no doubt that he owns it by that, due to his possession in the buckets we call 'qawadis' first, then pouring it into the basins after possession. Reflect. I say: The well in the speech of jurists is usually for specific (mu'ayyan) water; other than that, it is called 'sahrij', 'jabb', etc. It may also be used for non-specific. What should be relied upon regarding water is that by possession (hiyazah) one owns and guarantees; similarly, one owns in cisterns (saharij) made in houses for possession definitively, because they are like containers (hibab). I have given a fatwa that this must be adhered to. And it does not contradict what is in Al-Walwaljiya from his statement: 'If one draws water from a man's well without permission until it dries, there is no liability because the well owner is not the owner of the water; but if one pours water that was in a container (hubb), it is said to him: fill the water because the owner of the container owns the water, and it is among the fungibles (mithliyyat), so he guarantees its like.' That is because his statement is about a specific well (bi'r mu'ayyan). And as for...
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage contains an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of honey (tahl). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf prohibit it, analogizing honey to grass (kala') because both are common property until possession. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan permits its sale, possibly because honey is produced from flowers that may be on one's own land or through effort. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym?) attempts to reconcile by noting that watering grass causes it to grow, so effort creates ownership, whereas water exists naturally and digging does not create ownership. The passage then transitions to water ownership: according to the Hanafi school, the owner of a well does not own the water in it while it remains in the well. This is based on the principle that water is shared (mubah). However, once water is physically extracted (by drawing or using mechanical means like waterwheels), it becomes owned through possession (hiyazah). The commentator distinguishes between wells (bi'r) and cisterns (sahraj); water in cisterns is owned because it is collected and possessed. This is confirmed by the fatwa of the commentator. The passage ends with a discussion of Al-Walwaljiya's ruling: if someone draws water from another's well without permission, there is no liability because the well owner is not the owner; but if someone pours water from a container (hubb), that water is owned and its like must be returned.

Key Terms
تحل (tahl): Honey; the context treats it as a natural product similar to grass.
بئر معين (bi'r mu'ayyan): A well that contains naturally occurring water, not a constructed cistern.
حيازة (hiyazah): Physical possession or taking control; by this, mubah objects become owned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which party owns water that is in a natural well (bi'r mu'ayyan)?
A) The owner of the land on which the well is dug.
B) The person who dug the well.
C) No one; it is mubah (common property).
D) The community that has access to the well.

Key Takeaway
Honey and water are mubah; ownership is established only through physical possession (hiyazah), not by effort or land ownership.

Selling Water from Wells: Possession and Ownership
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale of well water and the principle of ownership by possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on selling well water, explain the intra-madhhab dispute, and apply the condition of possession (ihraz) to determine ownership.

Core Idea
You do not own well water by digging; only physical possession makes it yours to sell.

This lesson unpacks a dispute within the Hanafi school about whether water from a well can be sold. The key principle is that permissible things (mubah) like water are not owned until taken into possession. This distinction affects rulings on well water versus water collected in cisterns. We'll follow the commentary to see how the imams differed and how the later fatwa resolves the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is selling water that seeps into a well (al-tahall). The ruling is contested: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the sale is not permissible; Muhammad says it is permissible.
The base matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) states the ruling as 'not permissible' and the commentator clarifies it is the view of the majority.
2
State the positions of the three imams
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: sale not permissible because the water existed before the well was dug, so the digger does not own it. Muhammad: sale is permissible because the effort of digging creates ownership.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the basis: ownership requires possession (ihraz)
The commentary explains that permissible things (mubah) become owned only through physical possession. Water in a well is still in its natural state, not yet possessed. Therefore, the owner of the well does not own the water itself.
This is a key Hanafi principle.
4
Distinguish well water from cistern water
Water collected in cisterns (saharij) made for storage in houses is considered possessed because it is intentionally placed there. Thus, the water in such cisterns is owned by the house owner. The fatwa at the end confirms this: the lessee of a house cannot use water from the cistern without the lessor's permission.
Cisterns are like vessels (hubab) for holding water.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, water from a well can only be sold after it is taken out into containers (possession). According to Muhammad, it can be sold directly from the well. The later fatwa leans toward the requirement of possession.
The Shafi'i school, as cited by al-Ramli, allows sale even without possession because of the effort of digging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والتحل) أي لم يجز بيعه وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد: يجوز
في النهر وأقول : يمكن أن يفرق بينهما بأن سقى الكلأ كان سبباً في إنباته فنبت بخلاف الماء فإنه موجود قبل حفره فلا يملكه بالحفر .اهـ. وقال الرملي : أصح القولين عند الشافعي أنه يملكه، سواء حفرها في أرض موات أو ملك، وعندنا لا يملكه فيهما وأقول : المنقول أن صاحب البئر لا يملك الماء وقدمه هذا الشارح في كتاب الطهارة في شرح قوله وانتفاخ حيوان وتفسخه عن الولوالجية فراجعه. وهذا ما دام في البئر أما إذا أخرجه منها بالاحتيال كما . في السواقي التي ببلادنا فلا شك ملكه له بذلك لحيازته له في الكيزان التي نسميها القواديس أولاً ثم صبه في البرك بعد حيازته تأمل وأقول : البئر في كلام الفقهاء غالباً للمعين وأما غيره فيقال فيه صهريج وجب ونحو ذلك، وقد يطلق على غير المعين والذي يجب التعويل عليه في الماء أن يقال بالحيازة يملك وفيضمن على يملك في الصهاريج المتخذة في البيوت للحيازة قطعاً لأنها بمنزلة الحباب، وقد أفتيت في هذا يجب أن به ولا يخالفه ما في الولوالجية من قوله : ولو نزح ماء بئر رجل بغير إذنه حتى يبست لا شيء عليه لأن صاحب البئر غير مالك للماء، ولو صب ماء رجل كان في الحب يقال له املأ الماء لأن صاحب الحب مالك للماء وهو من ذوات الأمثال فيضمن مثله .اهـ لأن كلامه في البئر المعين، وأما الصهاريج التي توضع لاحراز الماء في الدور فلا شك في أن ماءها يصير مملوكاً لأصحابها بمنزلة الحباب والأواني فتأمل. وصورة ما رفع إلي من بيت المقدس فيما إذا استأجر داراً للسكن في بيوتها وفي الدار صهريج معد لجمع ماء الأشتية وفيه ماء قبل الإجارة، فهل هذا الماء ملك المؤجر ليس للمستأجر فيه إلا ما أباحه المؤجر ؟ فأجبت نعم الصهاريج التي في الدور المعدة لجمع ماء الأشتية الموضوعة لاحراز الماء يملك ماؤها وهي بمنزلة الحباب كما . هو مستفاد من تعليلهم في مسألة الأنهار المملوكة والآبار والحياض بقولهم لأنها لم توضع للإحراز، والمباح لا يملك إلا بالإحراز، وأنت على يقين بأن الصهاريج التي في الدور إنما وضعت للإحراز فليس للمستأجر إلا ما أباحه المؤجر
His saying: (And the water seepage) means its sale is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: It is permissible. In al-Nahr, I say: The difference between them is that watering grass was a cause for its growth, so it grew; unlike water, which existed before digging the well, so he does not own it by digging. Al-Ramli said: The sounder of the two opinions according to al-Shafi'i is that he owns it, whether he dug it in dead land or owned land, while according to us he does not own it in either case. I say: The transmitted view is that the owner of the well does not own the water, and the commentator has presented this in the book of Taharah. This is as long as it is in the well. As for if he takes it out by artifice, as in the waterwheels in our country, there is no doubt that he owns it by that because he possesses it in the buckets we call qawadis first, then pours it into pools after possession. I say: The well in the words of the jurists is mostly for still water, while other than that is called a cistern, tank, etc. What must be relied upon regarding water is that it is owned by possession, and one is liable for it. As for cisterns made in houses for possession, definitely they are like vessels. I have issued a fatwa on this in what was raised to me from Jerusalem regarding a house rented for residence where there was a cistern for collecting rainwater and water before the lease. Is this water owned by the lessor, and the lessee has only what the lessor permits? I answered: Yes, cisterns in houses made for collecting rainwater and placed for possession, their water is owned, and they are like vessels, as is understood from their reasoning in the issue of owned rivers, wells, and reservoirs because they were not placed for possession, and permissible things are owned only by possession, and you are certain that cisterns in houses are placed for possession, so the lessee has only what the lessor permits.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of water from a well. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that selling such water is not permissible, while Muhammad al-Shaybani allows it. The reason for the difference lies in the concept of ownership: Abu Hanifa argues that water in a well is not owned by the digger because it existed before the digging; the well merely accesses it. Muhammad, however, considers that through effort (digging and maintaining the well) the water becomes owned. The commentary then expands on the condition of possession (ihraz): once water is physically taken from the well into containers, it becomes property that can be sold. This distinction is crucial: water in its natural state (e.g., in a well) is not owned, but water collected in cisterns (saharij) for storage is owned because it is placed there for possession. The fatwa at the end confirms that water in a house cistern is owned by the owner of the house, and a tenant cannot use it without permission.

Key Terms
التحل (al-tahall): The water that emerges or seeps into a well; the object of sale in this dispute.
إحراز (ihraz): Taking physical possession of a permissible thing, which establishes ownership.
صهريج (sahrij): A cistern or reservoir built in houses to collect and store water, often for rainwater.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the key factor that determines whether water from a source is owned?
A) Whether the water is from a well or a river.
B) Whether the source was dug with effort.
C) Whether the water has been placed in a container for possession (ihraz).
D) Whether the water is used for drinking or irrigation.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of water from wells requires physical possession (ihraz); simply digging does not confer ownership.
