Selling Silk Worms and Eggs: Three Hanafi Opinions
Ruling on sale of silk worms and eggs with three Hanafi opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on selling silk worms and eggs, explain the reasoning behind each, and identify the mufta bihi (operative opinion) for fatwa.

Core Idea
A beneficial insect can be sold; a vermin cannot—Hanafi imams differ on classification.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute over the sale of silk worms and their eggs. The three founding imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—each offer a distinct ruling based on whether the item is considered vermin (hawamm) or beneficial (muntafa' bih). Understanding this dispute reveals how later Hanafi jurists weigh evidence and select the operative opinion for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the opinion of each imam regarding the sale of silk worms and separately regarding the eggs. Note that Abu Yusuf has a conditional position for worms.
For worms: Abu Hanifa prohibits; Abu Yusuf permits if silk appears; Muhammad permits unconditionally. For eggs: Abu Hanifa prohibits; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad permit.
2
Trace the legal reasoning (dalil) for each opinion
Abu Hanifa classifies silk worms as vermin (hawamm), whose sale is impermissible. Muhammad argues they are beneficial (muntafa' bih), so sale is valid. Abu Yusuf's conditional view treats the sale as a subsidiary (tab'an) of the silk.
The key distinction is whether the worm itself is considered muntafa' bih independently or only as a means to the silk.
3
Identify the tarjih (preferment) mechanism
The commentator states that the fatwa is on Muhammad's opinion for both worms and eggs, citing Al-Dhakhira and Al-Khulasa. He questions why the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq did not follow Muhammad's view for bees as well.
Later fatwa collections are the authority for mufta bihi, not just the early texts.
4
Analyze the commentator's critique
The commentator (Ibn Nujaym or similar) criticizes the author of Kanz for inconsistency: accepting Muhammad's view for worms but not for bees. Other commentators (al-Nahr, al-Ramli) defend Kanz by suggesting stronger evidence regarding bees.
This shows the living tradition of taqyim (evaluation) within the school.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, a contract to sell silk worms is invalid; under Muhammad's, it is valid and enforceable. The mufta bihi (Muhammad's view) makes such sales permissible in modern practice.
The practical difference affects everyday transactions in sericulture.

Source Text

[قوله : (ويباع دود القز وبيضه)] أما الدود فلا يجوز بيعه عند أبي حنيفة لأنه من الهوام وعند أبي يوسف يجوز إذا ظهر فيه القز تبعاً، وعند محمد يجوز كيفما كان لكونه منتفعاً . وأما بيضه فلا يجوز بيعه عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يجوز لمكان الضرورة، وقيل أبو يوسف مع أبي حنيفة كما في دوده وإنما اختار المؤلف قول محمد في الدود والبيض لكونه المفتى به ولكن يرد عليه أن الفتوى على قول محمد أيضاً في بيع النحل كما في الذخيرة والخلاصة فلم اختار قوله في الدود دون النحل بلا مرجح، ولعله لم يطلع على أن الفتوى على قوله فيهما. وفي المصباح: القز معرب. قال المرزوقي: هو فارسي وهو الذي يسمونه بالفارسية كرم أبرشم. وسيأتي أن الفتوى على قول محمد قوله : (ويباع دود القز وبيضه) وأما دود النحل فلا يجوز بيعه عند أبي حنيفة لأنه من الهوام، وعند أبي يوسف يجوز إذا ظهر فيه العسل تبعاً، وعند محمد يجوز كيفما كان لكونه منتفعاً به، والفتوى على قول محمد. قوله : (ولعله لم يطلع على أن الفتوى على قوله فيهما) استبعده في النهر واعتذر عن بقوله وكأنه لقوة المدرك في النحل. وكذا استبعده الرملي ثم قال: وإنما الجواب عنه أنه ربما قام المصنف
And the silk worm and its eggs are sold. [His saying: (And the silk worm and its eggs are sold)] As for the worm, its sale is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa because it is among vermin. According to Abu Yusuf, it is permissible if the silk appears in it as a subsidiary. According to Muhammad, it is permissible in any case because it is beneficial. As for its eggs, their sale is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa, but according to both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, it is permissible due to necessity. It is said that Abu Yusuf agrees with Abu Hanifa regarding the worms. The author chose Muhammad's opinion regarding the worms and eggs because it is the mufta bihi, but it is objected that the fatwa is also on Muhammad's opinion regarding the sale of bees as in al-Dhakhira and al-Khulasa, so why did he choose his opinion regarding the worms but not the bees without a preferred reason? Perhaps he did not know that the fatwa is on his opinion in both. In al-Misbah: Al-qazz is Arabized. Al-Marzuqi said: It is Persian, and it is what is called in Persian kirm-i abrisham. It will come that the fatwa is on Muhammad's opinion. His saying: (And the silk worm and its eggs are sold) And as for the bee worm, its sale is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa because it is among vermin. According to Abu Yusuf, it is permissible if honey appears in it as a subsidiary. According to Muhammad, it is permissible in any case because it is beneficial, and the fatwa is on Muhammad's opinion. His saying: (Perhaps he did not know that the fatwa is on his opinion in both.) Al-Nahr considered this unlikely and apologized for him, saying it is as if due to the strength of the evidence regarding bees. Similarly, al-Ramli considered it unlikely, then said: The answer is that perhaps the author

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute over the sale of silk worms (dud al-qazz) and their eggs, and also touches on bees (nahl). The three imams differ: Abu Hanifa prohibits the sale of silk worms because they are considered vermin (hawamm). Abu Yusuf allows the sale only if the silk has already appeared, treating it as a subsidiary (tab'an) of the silk. Muhammad allows the sale unconditionally because the worm is beneficial (muntafa' bih). For eggs, Abu Hanifa prohibits, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow due to necessity. The author of this commentary (likely Ibn Nujaym or similar) mentions that the fatwa is on Muhammad's opinion for both worms and eggs, but he questions why the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq didn't also adopt Muhammad's view for bees. The commentator speculates that perhaps the author was unaware that the fatwa is on Muhammad's opinion for both, or that there is a stronger evidence for bees. The passage illustrates how later Hanafi jurists reconcile different rulings within the school.

Key Terms
دود القز (dud al-qazz): Silk worms, the larvae that produce silk. Considered beneficial by Muhammad, vermin by Abu Hanifa.
هوام (hawamm): Vermin or harmful insects, the sale of which is generally prohibited.
متفع به (muntafa' bih): Something that can be utilized beneficially; a condition for permissibility of sale.

Selling Silkworms and Their Eggs: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on silkworm sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on selling silkworms, explain the basis for each, and identify the mufta bihi opinion.

Core Idea
Silkworm sale is valid if beneficial; Abu Hanifa says no, Abu Yusuf conditionally yes, Muhammad unconditionally yes.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of silkworms and their eggs. While the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq declares it permissible, the commentators clarify that this is only the view of Imam Muhammad. We will explore the perspectives of the three founding imams and understand why Muhammad's view is preferred for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the three positions
The act is selling silkworms (dud al-qazz) and their eggs. Abu Hanifa prohibits, Abu Yusuf permits if silk is visible, Muhammad permits unconditionally.
2
Locate the dalil for each imam
Abu Hanifa: the worm is vermin (hawamm) and not property. Abu Yusuf: necessity (darurah) and common practice. Muhammad: the worm is beneficial (muntafa' bihi) as it produces silk.
The proof for Muhammad is qiyas with honeybees, which are also beneficial.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the sale is void (fasid). Under Abu Yusuf, only sale of a worm with visible silk is valid. Under Muhammad, sale is valid in all states.
This difference affects commercial transactions involving silkworm eggs or larvae.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The author of Kanz chose Muhammad's view, and it is confirmed by later authorities (al-Dhakhira, al-Khulasa). The commentator notes an inconsistency regarding bees but suggests the author may have been unaware.
Thus, the fatwa position is unconditional permissibility.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: 'And silkworms and their eggs are sold.' As for the worm, it is not permissible to sell according to Abu Hanifa because it is among vermin. According to Abu Yusuf, it is permissible if the silk appears in it as a subsidiary. According to Muhammad, it is permissible in any case because it yields benefit. Abu Hanifa's view is that they are vermin, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it permissible due to necessity. It is said that Abu Yusuf agrees with Abu Hanifa regarding the worm itself. As for khazz, it is the name of an animal, then used for cloth made from its fur, plural khuzzan. Restriction to bees and silkworms indicates that other vermin like snakes, scorpions, geckos, hedgehogs, and lizards cannot be sold by agreement. Nothing from the sea can be sold except fish, like frog, crab, turtle, seahorse, etc. However, in al-Dhakhira, if one buys leeches (called 'mara'l in Persian), it is permissible, and al-Sadr al-Shahid took this due to people's need for them. Likewise if the labor is from both and the silk is split half and half. In Fatawa al-Walwaliji: a woman gave another silkworm seeds (filq) on halves, and she tended them until they matured. The filq belongs to the owner of the seeds because it came from her seeds. She has the value of the leaves and her wage. Similarly, if someone gives a cow to another to feed so that the offspring be between them half and half, the offspring all belong to the owner of the cow, and he owes the cost of fodder and wage. Likewise, if someone gives chickens for eggs to be split half and half. This is in Fath al-Qadir, and its place is the book of Ijarah.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the sale of silkworms (dud al-qazz) and their eggs. The matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq states that they are sold, and the commentator expands on the positions. Abu Hanifa prohibits selling the worm because it is considered vermin (hawamm), which are generally not considered property. Abu Yusuf permits it only if the silk is already visible, as the worm becomes a subsidiary (tab') of the valuable silk. Muhammad permits the sale unconditionally because the worm itself is beneficial (muntafa' bihi) since it produces silk. The author chooses Muhammad's view as mufta bihi. The passage then notes a contradiction: the fatwa is also on Muhammad's view for selling bees, yet the author of Kanz did not choose it there. The commentator suggests the author may have been unaware. The rest of the passage discusses related issues: other vermin that cannot be sold, leeches (permitted due to need), and partnerships involving silkworm cultivation, where the product belongs to the owner of the seeds regardless of labor, following the rule that offspring follow the origin. This aligns with Hanafi principles of property and partnership.

Key Terms
الهوام (al-hawamm): Vermin or harmful insects like scorpions, snakes, centipedes—generally not considered permissible to sell due to lack of benefit and impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because it is considered property like honeybees.
D) Because the silk is visible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi permits the sale of silkworms unconditionally because they are beneficial, despite Abu Hanifa's prohibition.

Selling Bees and Honeycombs: The Hanafi Intra-School Dispute
Explores whether bees and honeycombs can be sold as part of a hive or independently

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on selling bees and identify which view is mufta bihi, along with the legal basis for each.

Core Idea
Bees can be sold subsidiary to a honeycomb; the fatwa permits independent sale following Imam Muhammad.

The sale of bees raises a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute touching on the concept of tab'iyyah (subsidiary status in a sale). While Abu Hanifa deemed bees unsellable, his two disciples permitted them with a honeycomb, and Muhammad even allowed independent sale. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and traces the evolution of the fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested act and the three positions
Recognise that the dispute is about selling bees (nahl) with or without a honeycomb (kawara). State the three views: Abu Hanifa: invalid; Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): valid as subsidiary; Muhammad: valid in all cases.
The kawara is the clay hive containing honey and bees.
2
Analyse the proofs for each position
Examine the dalil for each: Abu Hanifa considers bees as hawamm (creeping insects) not ownable; Sahibayn rely on the subsidiary principle (tabi') for what accompanies the hive; Muhammad emphasises benefit (manfa'ah) and custom (urf).
Al-Karkhi denied the subsidiary principle for bees, but the commentator rebuts with keys as an example.
3
Examine the linguistic definition of kawara
The passage clarifies the linguistic variants and confirms that kawara includes the honey and bees within. This definition affects the scope of the subsidiary relationship.
The term technically refers to the structure, but in fiqh it implies the bees inside.
4
Trace the tarjih to the mufta bihi
Later authorities (al-Dhakhira, al-Khulasa) prefer Muhammad's view for independent sale, and the fatwa is on his opinion. The author of Kanz may have chosen the Sahibayn's view for bees but Muhammad for silkworms.
A reconciliation suggests selling at night (when bees are gathered) versus day (dispersed).
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Given a beekeeper selling a hive, determine validity under each view and conclude with the mufta bihi.
Under Muhammad, the sale is sahih even without the honeycomb; under Sahibayn it is sahih only as subsidiary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is absolute, so it includes when it is subsidiary to honeycombs that contain honey. This is the saying of al-Karkhi. Al-Quduri mentioned that selling it as a subsidiary to a honeycomb containing honey is permissible, but al-Karkhi denied it, saying that a thing enters sale as a subsidiary to another only if it is among its rights, like right of drink or right of way, and this is not among its rights. So it is in al-Fawa'id al-Zuhayriyya. It was answered that subsidiary is not confined to rights, as with keys. So honey is subsidiary to bees in existence, and bees are subsidiary to honey in purpose of sale. Al-kawara (with damma on kaf and shadda on waw) is the beehive when made of clay. In al-Tahdhib: kawara al-nahl with takhfif. In al-Mughrib: with kasr without tashdid, and al-Zamakhshari recorded it with fatha on kaf. In al-Gharibayn: with damma. So in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Misbah: kawara al-nahl with damma and takhfif and tathqil all mean its honey in the wax, or its house if it has honey, or the beehive. Breaking the kaf with takhfif is a language. End. It will come that the fatwa is on the saying of Muhammad. His statement: 'It is not permissible to sell according to Abu Hanifa.' He said in al-Nahr: Know that according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) one needs to differentiate between bees and silkworms, as he permitted its sale as a subsidiary but not the silkworm. There is no problem based on what is related from al-Karkhi that it is not permissible for bees as a subsidiary either. His statement: 'Perhaps he was unaware that the fatwa is on his view in both' was deemed unlikely in al-Nahr, and he apologized saying it is as if due to strength of evidence for bees. Similarly, al-Ramli deemed it unlikely, then said: The answer is that perhaps the author had evidence for choosing the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding bees, and Muhammad's view on silkworms and eggs. They differentiate by a distinction apparent from the saying of some: it is permissible to sell it at night but not during the day because it is gathered at night and dispersed during the day in pastures.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale of bees (nahl) together with honeycombs (kawara). The matn apparently allowed the sale of bees as a subsidiary (tab') to the honeycomb. Al-Karkhi disagreed, restricting subsidiary to inherent rights (like water rights) rather than physical objects. The commentator rebuts that subsidiary can include physical accompaniments like keys with a door. The honey is subsidiary to bees in existence, but bees are subsidiary to honey in the purpose of sale. The passage then discusses the linguistic forms of 'kawara'. Crucially, the fatwa is declared to be on the view of Muhammad, who permits sale of bees even independently. The later part attempts to reconcile why the author of Kanz chose Muhammad's view for silkworms but not for bees—suggesting that perhaps he found stronger evidence for the Sahibayn's view on bees (that bees can be sold as part of a hive but not independently) while following Muhammad on silkworms. A distinction is offered: bees can be sold at night when they are in the hive, but not during the day when they scatter, analogizing to the sale of pigeons. This reflects Hanafi adaptation of rulings to practical circumstances.

Key Terms
الكوارة (al-kawara): The beehive (honeycomb) made of clay, containing honey and bees. Linguistic variants include damma/kasr, tashdid/takhfif.
النحل (al-nahl): Bees; in this context, the insects kept in hives for honey production.
التبعية (al-tab'iyyah): The principle of being subsidiary or accessory in a sale; something sold as part of another item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa in this passage, which view is relied upon for selling bees independently?
A) Abu Hanifa's view
B) Abu Yusuf's view
C) Muhammad's view
D) Al-Karkhi's view

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the sale of bees is valid according to the relied-upon view of Imam Muhammad, recognizing their economic value and custom.

Prohibition of Selling a Runaway Slave
States the ruling that selling an escaped slave is not permissible due to prophetic prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling a runaway slave and identify the basis for the prohibition in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The sale of a runaway slave is invalid due to prophetic prohibition and inability to deliver.

This lesson addresses the sale of a runaway slave (al-abiq). The Hanafi school holds that such a sale is invalid because it is prohibited by a hadith of the Prophet (SAW). The underlying cause is the seller's inability to deliver the subject matter, which is a condition for a valid sale. We will examine the textual evidence and the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sale of a runaway slave. The ruling is that it is not permissible (la yajiz), i.e., haram and invalid.
The source explicitly states 'لم يجز' meaning 'is not permissible'.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites the prohibition of the Prophet (SAW) as the evidence.
The phrase 'لنهي النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عنه' indicates the proof.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit prohibition indicates the ruling is haram. In Hanafi usul, a prohibition generally indicates forbiddance.
No contrary indicator, so it is taken as definitive in meaning (qati al-dalala).
4
Note the condition for validity
A valid sale requires the seller to be able to deliver. Since the slave is absent, delivery is impossible, making the sale invalid.
This condition is implicit in the definition of 'al-abiq'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والأبق) أي لم يجز بيع الآبق لنهي النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عنه
His statement: 'and the runaway slave' meaning the sale of a runaway slave is not permissible due to the prohibition of the Prophet (peace be upon him) from it.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq. The matn mentions that among invalid sales is the sale of an escaped slave (abaq). The commentator explains that the reason is the prophetic prohibition: the Prophet (SAW) forbade the sale of a runaway until it is recovered. The underlying legal cause is that the seller cannot deliver the subject matter, which is a condition for a valid sale. This ruling is clear and based directly on a hadith prohibition, making it a strong, agreed-upon ruling within the Hanafi school. No ikhtilaf is mentioned here.

Key Terms
الآبق (al-abiq): A slave who has run away from his master and whose whereabouts are unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of a runaway slave prohibited?
A) Because it is not allowed to sell human beings.
B) Because the Prophet (SAW) explicitly forbade it.
C) Because the buyer might mistreat the slave.
D) Because the seller does not own the slave.

Key Takeaway
The sale of a runaway slave is prohibited because the Prophet (SAW) forbade it, and the underlying cause is the impossibility of delivery.

Selling Pigeons: The Condition of Ability to Deliver
Explains the ruling on selling pigeons based on time of day for deliverability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition of ability to deliver (maqdur al-taslim) to determine the validity of a sale in scenarios where the subject matter may not be physically accessible.

Core Idea
A sale is valid only if the seller can deliver the item; pigeons at night are deliverable, during day they are not.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling from al-Hidayah and al-Dhakhira on the sale of pigeons. The key principle is that the subject matter of a sale must be within the seller's power to deliver. The time of day matters because it determines whether the pigeons are physically accessible. By understanding this principle, you will learn to assess deliverability in various contractual settings.

Guided Path
1
State the general principle
A sale requires the subject matter to be within the seller's ability to deliver. This is a condition for validity in Hanafi fiqh.
The condition is known as maqdur al-taslim.
2
Apply to the sale of pigeons
If the number of pigeons is known and delivery is possible, the sale is valid. The passage cites al-Hidayah.
The pigeons are considered property (mal).
3
Analyze the temporal distinction
Al-Dhakhira adds a nuance: selling a dovecote with pigeons is valid at night because all pigeons are inside and can be taken; during the day, some are outside, making delivery uncertain.
The distinction is based on physical accessibility.
4
Identify the legal cause (illa)
The illa for validity is the actual ability to seize the pigeons without trickery. At night, that condition is met; during the day, it is not.
Trickery (ihtiyal) is required during the day to catch them.
5
Derive the ruling
Thus, sale is sahih at night, fasid during the day. This shows how the condition of ability to deliver operates in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المؤلف بيع الحمام وذكره في الهداية فقال : والحمام إذا علم عددها وأمكن تسليمها جاز بيعها لأنه مال مقدور التسليم. وفي الذخيرة إذا باع برج حمام مع الحمام فإن باع ليلاً جاز لأن في الليل يكون الحمام بجملته داخل البرج ويمكن أخذه منه من غير الاحتيال فيكون بائعاً ما يقدر على تسليمه، وفي النهار يكون بعضه خارج البيت فلا يمكن أخذه إلا بالاحتيال فلا يجوز اهـ
The author did not mention the sale of pigeons, but it was mentioned in al-Hidayah: 'And pigeons, if their number is known and delivery is possible, their sale is permissible because they are property that can be delivered.' In al-Dhakhira: If one sells a dovecote with pigeons, if he sells by night, it is permissible because at night all the pigeons are inside the dovecote and can be taken from it without trickery, so he is selling that which he is able to deliver. During the day, some are outside the house and cannot be taken except by trickery, so it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage discusses the sale of pigeons (hamam). The author of Kanz did not include it, but al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah addressed it. The condition for a valid sale is that the pigeons must be known in number and possible to deliver (maqdur al-taslim). The general principle is that any property that can be delivered is saleable. The specific ruling from al-Dhakhira adds a temporal condition: selling a dovecote with its pigeons is valid at night because all pigeons are inside and can be taken reliably. During the day, since some pigeons are flying outside, delivery is uncertain and the sale is invalid unless the pigeons can be lured back. This distinction is based on the ability to deliver (al-qudra 'ala al-taslim), which is a fundamental requirement for any sale in Hanafi fiqh. The commentator here expands on the matn by referencing the Hidayah and Dhakhira.

Key Terms
مقدور التسليم (maqdur al-taslim): The condition that the subject matter of sale is within the seller's power to deliver to the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Dhakhira, when is the sale of a dovecote with pigeons invalid?
A) At night, because pigeons are asleep.
B) During the day, because some pigeons are outside and delivery is uncertain.
C) If the number of pigeons is unknown.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires the seller to have actual physical ability to deliver the subject matter at the time of sale; otherwise the contract is fasid.

Sale of Non-Fish Sea Creatures and Harmful Insects: General Rule and Exception
Expands on permissible and impermissible sales of specific animals and clarifies silk terminologies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule for selling insects and sea creatures, and identify the exception for leeches based on need.

Core Idea
Only bees and silkworms among insects, and only fish among sea creatures, are permissible to sell; others are unanimously prohibited.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq expands on the general rule of permissible and impermissible sales of animals. The author first clarifies key terms related to silk, then establishes a clear principle: harmful insects and non-fish sea creatures cannot be sold, with a notable exception for leeches due to human need. Understanding these boundaries helps prevent invalid transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for insects
The text states that besides bees and silkworms, all other vermin (e.g., snakes, scorpions) are unanimously impermissible to sell.
This includes any insect that is harmful or not commonly used.
2
Identify the general rule for sea creatures
Only fish from the sea can be sold; other creatures like frogs, crabs, and turtles are not permissible.
The term 'fish' here refers to the common category of sea animals that are fish.
3
Note the exception for leeches
Leeches are permissible to sell due to human need and their medicinal use, as adopted by al-Sadr al-Shahid.
This is based on necessity and custom (urf).
4
Distinguish terminologies
Qazz (raw silk) and ibrism (silk thread) are like wheat and flour—the same substance at different stages.
This clarifies that the sale of qazz and ibrism are governed by the same rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الليث : هو ما يعمل منه الإبريسم ولهذا قال بعضهم : القز والإبريسم مثل الحنطة والدقيق اهـ. وأما الخز فاسم دابة ثم أطلق على الثوب المتخذ من وبرها والجمع خزان مثل صرد وصردان منه أيضاً. قيد بالنحل والدود لأن ما سواهما من الهوام كالحيات والعقارب والوزغ والقنافذ والضب لا يجوز بيعه اتفاقاً، ولا يجوز بيع شيء من البحر إلا السمك كالضفدع والسرطان والسلحفاة وفرس البحر وغير ذلك ولكن في الذخيرة إذا اشترى العلق الذي يقال له بالفارسية مرعل يجوز وبه أخذ الصدر الشهيد لحاجة الناس إليه لتمول الناس له. وفي المصباح: العلق شيء أسود شبيه الدود يكون في الماء يعلق بأفواه الإبل عند الشرب اهـ. وقيد بالبيع لأنه لو كان الدود وورق التوت من واحد والعمل من آخر على أن يكون القز بينهما نصفين أو أقل أو أكثر لا يجوز عند محمد، وكذا لو كان العمل منهما وهو بينهما نصفان. وفي فتاوى الولوالجي امرأة أعطت امرأة بزر القز وهو بزر الفيلق بالنصف فقامت عليه حتى أدرك فالفيلق لصاحبة البزر لأنه حدث من بزرها ولها على صاحبة البزر قيمة الأوراق وأجر مثلها، ومثله إذا دفع بقرة إلى آخر يعلفها ليكون الحادث بينهما بالنصف فالحادث كله لصاحب البقرة وله على صاحب البقرة ثمن العلف وأجر مثله، وعلى هذا إذا دفع الدجاج ليكون البيض بالنصف ؛ كذا في فتح القدير ومحلها كتاب الإجارات .
Al-Layth: It is what ibrism is made from, so some said: qazz and ibrism are like wheat and flour. As for khazz, it is the name of an animal, then applied to clothing made from its fur; plural khizzan like surud and surdan from it. It is restricted to bees and worms because the rest of the vermin like snakes, scorpions, lizards, hedgehogs, and lizards cannot be sold by agreement. And nothing from the sea can be sold except fish, like frogs, crabs, turtles, sea horses, and other than that. But in al-Dhakhira: if one buys the leech, which is called in Persian 'mar'al', it is permissible, and al-Sadr al-Shahid adopted it due to people's need for it and their wealth. In al-Misbah: the leech is a black thing resembling a worm that is in the water, clinging to the mouths of camels when drinking. And it is restricted to sale because if the worms and mulberry leaves are from one person, and the work from another, on condition that the raw silk be between them in halves or less or more, it is not permissible according to Muhammad. Likewise if the work is from both and it is between them in halves. And in Fatawa al-Walwaliji: a woman gave another woman silkworm seeds, which are the seeds of the filq, in half, and she raised them until they matured; the filq belongs to the owner of the seeds because it came from her seeds, and she is owed the value of the leaves and her labor. Similarly, if one gives a cow to another to feed it for the produce to be between them in half, the entire produce belongs to the owner of the cow, and the owner of the cow owes the price of the fodder and labor. And likewise if one gives chickens for the eggs to be in half. As in Fath al-Qadir, and its place is the Book of Ijarah.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding on permissible and impermissible sales of insects and sea creatures. The commentator first clarifies terminology: al-qazz (raw silk) and ibrism (silk thread) are like wheat and flour—the same substance in different stages. Al-khazz is a type of fabric from a specific animal. The ruling then states that only bees and silkworms among insects can be sold; other vermin like snakes, scorpions, etc., are unanimously impermissible to sell. From the sea, only fish is allowed; other creatures like frogs, crabs, etc., are not. However, an exception is made for the leech (′alaq) due to human need, following a fatwa from al-Sadr al-Shahid. The passage then discusses partnerships in silk production: if one party provides worms and leaves and the other does the work, the resulting silk does not become joint property according to Imam Muhammad; the product belongs to the provider of the worms, and the worker gets compensation. This is analogous to giving a cow for feeding or chickens for eggs in a half-share arrangement—the original owner retains ownership of the offspring, and the other party is compensated for expenses and labor. These are detailed in the Book of Ijarah.

Key Terms
القز (al-qazz): Raw silk; the material produced from silkworms.
الإبريسم (al-ibrism): Silk thread; the processed form of qazz.
العلق (al-ʿalaq): A leech; a blood-sucking worm used in medicine, permissible to sell due to need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on selling a crab from the sea?
A) Permissible because it is from the sea.
B) Not permissible because only fish can be sold from the sea.
C) Permissible if it is alive.
D) Permissible only if needed for medicine.

Key Takeaway
Only bees, silkworms, and fish are generally sellable; other insects and sea creatures are haram unless there is a proven need like the leech.

Partnership in Silk Production: Ownership of the Product
Expands on the invalidity of partnerships in silk production and analogous cases, ruling that the product belongs to the original owner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a partnership to share silk produce is invalid and apply the analogy to cows and chickens.

Core Idea
When one party provides seeds/worms and the other provides labor, the product belongs to the seed owner, not jointly.

The passage continues with an important rule about partnerships in silk production. If one person provides the silkworm seeds and mulberry leaves, and another does the work, the resulting raw silk does not become joint property—even if they agreed to share. Imam Muhammad rules that the product belongs to the owner of the seeds, and the worker is entitled only to compensation for expenses and labor. This principle extends analogically to cows and chickens, ensuring clarity in agricultural and livestock partnerships.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
One party provides silkworm seeds and mulberry leaves; the other party provides labor on condition that the resulting silk be shared equally (or in any proportion).
2
Identify Imam Muhammad's ruling
According to Muhammad, this arrangement is not permissible (non-joint ownership). The silk belongs entirely to the provider of the seeds.
The text says 'لا يجوز عند محمد'.
3
Understand the legal basis
The product comes from the seeds owned by one party; the other's labor does not create ownership but a right to compensation.
This is a key principle: the increase follows the original property.
4
Apply the analogy
The same ruling applies to analogous cases: a cow given for feeding, or chickens given for eggs—the produce belongs to the owner of the animal, and the caretaker gets wages.
These are cited as examples in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Takeaway
In any contract where one provides the asset and another provides labor, the produce belongs to the asset owner, and the worker gets only wages—this is ijarah, not shirkah.

Sale of Pigeons and Birds: The Condition of Deliverability
States rulings for selling pigeons with conditions, and analogizes to birds in the air.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling pigeons and birds, and apply the condition of deliverability (tamkin al-taslim) to determine the validity of a sale.

Core Idea
A sale is valid only if the seller can deliver the item without trickery; pigeons at night are deliverable, by day they are not.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the sale of pigeons, a case not in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The key principle is that the seller must be able to deliver the goods. Pigeons in a dovecote are deliverable at night when they are inside, but not during the day when they fly out. This distinction also applies to birds in the air and even to valuable insects like the qirmiz, highlighting the centrality of tamkin al-taslim in Hanafi sales law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling pigeons (or birds). The general ruling is that it is permissible if conditions are met.
The ruling is from al-Hidayah: pigeons are wealth that can be delivered, so sale is permissible.
2
Identify the condition — tamkin al-taslim
The sale is valid only if the seller can deliver the goods without excessive effort (ihtiyal). At night, pigeons are inside and easily caught; during the day, they scatter.
The passage defines ihtiyal as trickery required to catch the pigeons during the day.
3
Understand the night/day distinction
Selling at night is valid because pigeons are all inside the dovecote and can be taken without trickery. Day sale is invalid because some are outside, requiring trickery to catch, so delivery is not possible.
This distinction is deduced from practice and reported in al-Dhakhira.
4
Extend the principle to other cases
The same reasoning applies to birds in the air (if return is known and delivery possible at night) and to the qirmiz insect, which is valuable and needed, making its sale permissible despite being a living creature.
The extension shows that the condition of deliverability is universal in sales.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المؤلف بيع الحمام وذكره في الهداية فقال : والحمام إذا علم عددها وأمكن تسليمها جاز بيعها لأنه مال مقدور التسليم. وفي الذخيرة إذا باع برج حمام مع الحمام فإن باع ليلاً جاز لأن في الليل يكون الحمام بجملته داخل البرج ويمكن أخذه منه من غير الاحتيال فيكون بائعاً ما يقدر على تسليمه، وفي النهار يكون بعضه خارج البيت فلا يمكن أخذه إلا بالاحتيال فلا يجوز اهـ. عنده دليل اختيار قولهما في النحل، وقول محمد في دود القز وبيضه ويفرق بينهما بفارق يلوح من قول بعضهم يجوز بيعه ليلاً ولا يجوز بيعه نهاراً لأنه يكون مجتمعاً حالة الليل متفرقاً حالة النهار في المراعي. قوله : (ولكن في الذخيرة إذا اشترى العلف الخ) انظر هل يقال مثله في بيع الدودة وهي القرمز التي يصبغ بها بناء على ما اشتهر من أن أصلها دود له روح يخنق بالكلس وبالخل، ومقتضى التعليل الجواز فإنها كثيرة الاحتياج بين الناس ولها مداخل كثيرة عند أرباب الصنائع وهي من أنفس الأموال عندهم وقد أجاز وأبيع السرقين تأمل. قوله : (فإن باع ليلاً جاز الخ) ألغز فيه الشيخ رمضان العطيفي فقال على هامش نسخته المكتوبة بخطه : يا إماماً في فقه نعمان أضحى حائز السبق مفرداً لا يجاري أي بيت يجوز بيعك إيا اهـ. قال الرملي : وتقدم في شرح قوله والطير في الهواء أنه إذا علم عوده وأمكن تسليمه بليل ولا يجوز نهاراً
The author did not mention the sale of pigeons, but it is mentioned in al-Hidayah, and he said: 'Pigeons, if their number is known and delivery is possible, their sale is permissible because they are wealth that can be delivered.' In al-Dhakhira: if one sells a dovecote with the pigeons, then if he sells at night, it is permissible because at night the pigeons are all inside the dovecote and can be taken without trickery, so he sells what he can deliver. During the day, some are outside, so they cannot be taken except by trickery, so it is not permissible. With him is evidence for preferring the opinion of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding bees, and Muhammad's opinion on silkworms and their eggs, and he distinguishes between them with a difference that appears from some who say: it is permissible to sell it at night and not during the day because it is gathered at night and scattered during the day in the pastures. His statement: (But in al-Dhakhira: if one buys the leech...) See if the same can be said about selling the worm that is the qirmiz used for dye, based on the well-known fact that its origin is a worm with spirit that is killed with lime and vinegar; the implication of the reasoning is permissibility, since it is much needed among people and has many uses among craftsmen, and it is among the most valuable of their properties, and they have permitted it, and sell it for manure. Consider. His statement: (For if he sells at night, it is permissible....) Shaykh Ramadan al-Utayfi made a riddle about it on the margin of his copy: 'O Imam in the fiqh of Nu'man, you have become the possessor of the lead, unique, unequaled. Which house do you sell by night and not by day?' Al-Ramli said: 'It has been presented in the commentary on his statement: 'And the bird in the air' that if its return is known and delivery is possible, it is permissible by night and not by day.'
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from the commentary, addressing the sale of pigeons and birds. The commentator notes that the author of Kanz did not mention pigeons, but al-Hidayah does. The rule: pigeons, like any property, can be sold if their number is known and delivery is possible. However, delivery is only possible at night when the pigeons are in the dovecote; during the day they fly out, making delivery uncertain without trickery, so the sale is invalid. This reasoning is analogous to bees and silkworms: some scholars distinguish between night and day based on the animals' behavior. The commentator then reflects on whether the same night/day distinction applies to the sale of the qirmiz insect (used for red dye), noting that despite being a living worm, its sale is permissible due to people's need, and it is killed with lime/vinegar. Finally, a poetic riddle from Shaykh Ramadan al-Utayfi highlights the ruling: selling a house (dovecote) at night is permissible but not during the day. Al-Ramli connects this to the earlier discussion on birds in the air: if the bird's return is known and delivery possible, selling it at night is allowed but not during the day.

Key Terms
الحمام (al-hamam): Pigeons; domesticated birds kept in dovecotes.
الاحتيال (al-ihtiyal): Trickery or effort to catch; used to describe difficulty in delivery during the day.
القرمز (al-qirmiz): A red dye from an insect; the insect itself is considered valuable property and its sale is permitted due to need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling a dovecote with pigeons permissible at night but not during the day?
A) Because pigeons are more valuable at night.
B) Because at night the pigeons are all inside and easily caught, fulfilling the condition of delivery.
C) Because daytime sales are prohibited by hadith.
D) Because the number of pigeons is not known during the day.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a sale in Hanafi fiqh hinges on the seller's ability to deliver the item without excessive effort; this condition leads to practical distinctions like night vs day for selling pigeons.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
The General Ruling on Selling a Runaway Slave: Zahir al-Riwayah vs. Abu Hanifa
Intra-Hanafi dispute on void sale vs. later validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the zahir al-riwayah ruling on selling a runaway slave and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding its validity after the slave returns.

Core Idea
A sale of a runaway slave is void; it does not become valid even if the slave returns, per zahir al-riwayah.

This lesson examines the basic ruling on selling a runaway slave ('abaq). The Hanafi school's established position (zahir al-riwayah) declares the sale void ab initio due to inability to deliver. However, a narration from Abu Hanifa suggests the contract becomes valid if the slave returns before rescission. We will analyze the evidence and the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling and its basis
Extract the ruling on selling a runaway slave from the text: it is not permissible (la yajuz) due to the Prophet's prohibition. Note that the immediate cause is inability to deliver at the time of contract.
The analogy is to selling a bird in the air – both lack deliverability.
2
Compare the two Hanafi positions
Zahir al-riwayah: sale is void, does not become valid later. Abu Hanifa (narration): sale becomes valid if slave returns before rescission, as impediment is removed. Note that Muhammad also has a similar narration.
The zahir al-riwayah is from the six core books; the other is a weaker transmission.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection from Abu Hanifa: the contract was valid at inception (property existed) and the impediment (inability to deliver) was later removed. The response: the contract was void from the start because the subject matter was not deliverable; the later return does not revive it. The narration is interpreted as a new contract by mutual handling (ta'ati) after return.
This interpretation reconciles the narration with the established position.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and tarjih
The zahir al-riwayah is the relied-upon fatwa, as affirmed by Abu Abdullah al-Balkhi and the author of al-Dhakhira. Al-Marghinani and the commentator of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq prefer this view.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah (highest).
5
Apply the principle to analogous cases
The same logic applies to bees (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's view) and silkworms (Muhammad's view). The key is ability to deliver at contract time.
These examples illustrate the operation of the illa.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الذخيرة، وأولوا تلك الرواية بأن المراد بها انعقاد البيع بالتعاطي الآن. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا باعه لابنه الصغير فإنه لا يجوز، وكذا اليتيم في حجره بخلاف ما إذا وهبه له فإنه يجوز والفرق أن شرط البيع القدرة ويصير قابضاً لابنه بنفس الهبة؛ ذكر هذه المسألة في الجامع، وفي المنتقى عن أبي يوسف : لو تصدق بعبد أبق له على ابنه الصغير لا يجوز، وروى المعلى عنه أنه يجوز فحصل عن أبي يوسف في المسألة روايتان .اهـ. وشمل كلامه أيضاً ما إذا باعه بعد ما أبق من يد الغاصب منه لما في الذخيرة وإذا أبق العبد المغصوب من يد الغاصب ثم إن المالك باع العبد من الغاصب وهو آبق بعد فالبيع جائز، والأصل أن الإباق إنما يمنع جواز البيع إذا كان التسليم محتاجاً إليه بأن أبق من يد المالك ثم باعه المالك، فأما إذا لم يكن التسليم محتاجاً إليه كما في مسألتنا يجوز البيع .اهـ. وقيد بالآبق لأن العبد المرسل في حاجة المولى يجوز بيعه، ولو باعه وليس بابق ثم أبق قبل القبض فإن المشتري بالخيار في فسخ ذلك العقد ولا يكون للبائع أن يطالب المشتري بالثمن ما لم يحضر العبد .اهـ. وجعل الراد على البائع كما في القنية وخرج المغصوب فقد ذكر محمد في الأصل أنه موقوف إن أقر به الغاصب تم البيع ولزم، بيع أيضاً . الأب أنه جائز يجوز ولم يفرق بينهما إذا كان بالنهار فراجعه قوله : (وأولوا تلك الرواية الخ) هذا أيضاً ينافي ما قدمه أول كتاب البيوع من التعاطي لا His statement: (and the runaway slave) means that the sale of a runaway slave is not permissible due to the Prophet's prohibition (peace be upon him). With him is evidence for preferring the opinion of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding bees, and Muhammad's opinion on silkworms and their eggs... (continues with same text as earlier, then:)... then he said: Because he is not able to deliver, and if he sells it and then the running away ends, the contract does not become valid because it was void due to lack of subject matter, like selling a bird in the air. According to Abu Hanifa, the contract is completed if not rescinded, because the contract was concluded with property existing, and the impediment (inability to deliver) has been removed, as if the slave ran away after the sale. This is also related from Muhammad. So in al-Hidayah, the first is the apparent transmission, and Abu Abdullah al-Balkhi used to give fatwa accordingly, as in al-Dhakhira. They interpreted that narration to mean that the sale is concluded by mutual handling (al-ta'ati) now. He made it absolute, so it includes when he sells it to his minor son: it is not permissible. Likewise, the orphan in his guardianship, unlike if he gives it as a gift to him, then it is permissible. The difference is that the condition of sale is ability to deliver immediately after the contract, which is absent, and the remaining hand (control) he has is sufficient for accepting a gift but not for accepting a sale, because sale is a receipt in exchange for money given from the son's property, while this is a receipt not in exchange for money leaving the child's property. So that hand suffices for the sake of the minor, because if it returns, it returns to the minor's ownership. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and al-Tabyin. In Fatawa Qadikhan, from the chapter of gift, there is a contrary: he said: 'If one gives his runaway slave as a gift to his minor son, it is not permissible, but if he sells it to him, it is permissible.' So he reversed the ruling, and I have not seen any of the commentators point this out. The truth is what the judge mentioned according to al-Mi'raj: 'If one sells the runaway slave to his minor son, it is not permissible, but if he gives it as a gift to him or to an orphan in his guardianship, it is permissible, because the remaining hand he has over the runaway suffices for accepting a gift but not for a sale.' As for the author of al-Dhakhira, he mentioned in the chapter of sales that if a father sells a slave sent on an errand to his minor son, it is permissible, but he did not mention it in the case of a runaway. And in the book of gifts: 'If one gives a slave that is a runaway as a gift to his minor son, as long as he is still within the land of Islam, the gift is valid and he becomes a receiver for his son by the gift itself.' This issue is mentioned in al-Jami'. In al-Muntqa from Abu Yusuf: 'If one gives in charity a runaway slave to his minor son, it is not permissible.' And al-Mu'alla related from him that it is permissible, so there are two narrations from Abu Yusuf on this issue. His statement also includes when he sells it after it ran away from the hand of the usurper, because in al-Dhakhira: 'If the usurped slave runs away from the hand of the usurper, then the owner sells the slave to the usurper while he is still a runaway, the sale is permissible. The principle is that running away only prevents the validity of sale when delivery is needed, i.e., when it ran away from the owner's hand and then the owner sells it. But if delivery is not needed, as in our case, the sale is permissible.' And it is restricted to the runaway because a slave sent on the master's errand can be sold. If he sells it while it is not a runaway and then it runs away before delivery, the buyer has the option to rescind the contract, and the seller cannot claim the price until the slave is produced. And the return is upon the seller, as in al-Qunyah. And the usurped slave is excluded, for Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl that it is suspended: if the usurper acknowledges it, the sale is completed and binding. Also, the father's sale [to his son] is permissible, and he did not distinguish between them if it is during the day, so check it. His statement: (And they interpreted that narration...) This also contradicts what was presented at the beginning of the Book of Sales that mutual handling does not conclude a contract after a void or invalid sale unless the first contract is rescinded. His statement: (And in Fatawa Qadikhan from the gift section, there is a contrary...) He said in al-Nahr: and it occurs in al-Khaniyya in some copies...
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This is an expansion probe from the commentary, focusing on the sale of a runaway slave ('abaq). The passage begins by stating the general ruling: sale of a runaway slave is not permissible due to the Prophet's prohibition. The commentary then dives into intra-Hanafi disputes. According to the apparent (zahir al-riwayah) position, the sale is void ab initio because the subject matter is not deliverable, analogous to a bird in the air. However, a narration from Abu Hanifa holds that the contract becomes valid if the slave returns before rescission, because the impediment was removed; this is similar to a slave running away after sale. This narration is interpreted to refer to a sale by mutual handling (ta'ati) at the time of return. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is the first one (void sale), as followed by Abu Abdullah al-Balkhi. The discussion extends to selling a runaway to one's own minor son: the sale is invalid because the father cannot deliver immediately, but a gift to the minor son is valid because the father's constructive possession suffices for gift. There is a contrary view in Fatawa Qadikhan reversing this. The truth is that the sale is invalid but the gift is valid. The passage also covers the case of a runaway slave from a usurper (ghasib): if the usurper acknowledges the running, the sale is valid because delivery is not needed from the owner. The general principle is that running away only prevents sale when delivery is required from the seller. Finally, the commentator addresses the objection that mutual handling after a void sale does not create a new contract without rescission, which seems contradictory to the interpretation of Abu Hanifa's view. The passage ends abruptly with a note on the Khaniyya.

Key Terms
الآبق (al-abaaq): A runaway slave; one who has fled from his master.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent/established transmission, i.e., the ruling from the six core books of Imam Muhammad (Zahir al-Riwayah).
التعاطي (al-ta'ati): Mutual handling; a sale concluded by offer and acceptance through action without verbal formula.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the zahir al-riwayah, what is the ruling on selling a runaway slave?
A) The sale is valid if the slave returns within three days.
B) The sale is void because the seller cannot deliver at the time of contract.
C) The sale is valid if the buyer knows the slave's location.
D) The sale is suspended until the slave is caught.

Key Takeaway
The sale of a runaway slave is void ab initio due to lack of immediate delivery; it does not become valid later, and the contrary narration is interpreted as a new sale by mutual handling.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Selling or Gifting a Runaway Slave to One's Minor Son
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale/gift to minor son

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings on selling vs. gifting a runaway slave to one's minor son, and evaluate the contrary view in Fatawa Qadikhan.

Core Idea
A father cannot sell a runaway slave to his minor son, but he can gift it; the contrary view reverses this.

This lesson explores a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: the validity of transferring a runaway slave to the father's minor son. The majority view (based on al-Mi'raj) holds that a sale is invalid but a gift is valid, because the father's remaining hand (yad) suffices for gift but not for sale. However, Fatawa Qadikhan reverses this. We will analyze the reasoning and the preferred position.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position on sale and gift to a minor son
From the text: selling a runaway slave to one's minor son is not permissible (la yajuz), but giving it as a gift (hiba) is permissible (yajuz). The sale is invalid because the father cannot deliver immediately; the gift is valid because the father's constructive possession suffices for the minor.
The condition for sale is immediate delivery; for gift, the father's hand is considered the minor's hand.
2
Identify the contrary view from Fatawa Qadikhan
Fatawa Qadikhan states the opposite: selling the runaway slave to the minor son is valid, while gifting it is invalid. This effectively reverses the ruling.
The commentator notes that none of the earlier commentators addressed this reversal.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind each view
Majority: sale requires delivery in exchange for money from the minor's property; the father's hand is insufficient. Gift involves no exchange, so the father's hand suffices. Qadikhan: perhaps views the father's hand as sufficient for sale because the minor's property benefits, but not for gift because the minor does not gain anything (?) – the text does not provide explicit reasoning.
The majority view is considered the truth (al-haqq) by the commentator, citing al-Mi'raj.
4
Determine the tarjih (preferred view)
The commentator prefers the majority view (sale invalid, gift valid). This is also the position of al-Mi'raj, Fath al-Qadir, and al-Tabyin. The contrary view in Qadikhan is noted but not followed.
The same principle applies to an orphan in the father's guardianship.
5
Consider the practical application
If a father wishes to transfer a runaway slave to his minor son, he should use a gift (hiba) to ensure validity. Using a sale would be void under the majority view.
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: the method of transfer determines validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والأبق) أي لم يجز بيع الآبق لنهي النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عنه عنده دليل اختيار قولهما في النحل، وقول محمد في دود القز وبيضه ويفرق بينهما بفارق يلوح من قول بعضهم يجوز بيعه ليلاً ولا يجوز بيعه نهاراً لأنه يكون مجتمعاً حالة الليل متفرقاً حالة النهار في المراعي قوله : (ولكن في الذخيرة إذا اشترى العلف الخ) انظر هل يقال مثله في بيع الدودة وهي القرمز التي يصبغ بها بناء على ما اشتهر من أن أصلها دود له روح يخنق بالكلس وبالخل، ومقتضى التعليل الجواز فإنها كثيرة الاحتياج بين الناس ولها مداخل كثيرة عند أرباب الصنائع وهي من أنفس الأموال عندهم وقد أجاز وأبيع السرقين تأمل. قوله : (فإن باع ليلاً جاز الخ) ألغز فيه الشيخ رمضان العطيفي فقال على هامش نسخته المكتوبة بخطه : يا إماماً في فقه نعمان أضحى حائز السبق مفرداً لا يجاري أي بيت يجوز بيعك إيا اهـ. قال الرملي : وتقدم في شرح قوله والطير في الهواء أنه إذا علم عوده وأمكن تسليمه بليل ولا يجوز نهاراً من يتم ولأنه لا يقدر على تسليمه ولو باعه ثم عاد الإباق لا ذلك العقد لأنه وقع باطلاً لانعدام المحلية كبيع الطير في الهواء وعن أبي حنيفة أنه يتم العقد إذا لم يفسخ لأن العقد انعقد لقيام المالية والمانع قد ارتفع وهو العجز عن التسليم كما إذا أبق بعد البيع، وهكذا يروى عن محمد؛ كذا في الهداية والأول ظاهر الرواية وبه كان يفتي أبو عبد الله البلخي كما في الذخيرة، وأولوا تلك الرواية بأن المراد بها انعقاد البيع بالتعاطي الآن. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا باعه لابنه الصغير فإنه لا يجوز، وكذا اليتيم في حجره بخلاف ما إذا وهبه له فإنه يجوز والفرق أن شرط البيع القدرة على التسليم عقب البيع وهو منتف وما بقي له من اليد يصلح لقبض الهبة لا لقبض البيع لأنه قبض بإزاء مال مقبوض من مال الابن وهذا قبض ليس بإزائه مال يخرج من مال الولد فكفت تلك اليد له نظراً للصغير لأنه لو عاد عاد إلى ملك الصغير؛ هكذا في فتح القدير والتبيين. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان من الهبة خلافه قال: ولو وهب عبده الآبق لولده الصغير لا يجوز وإن باعه جاز .اهـ. فقد عكس الحكم على ما نقله الشارحون ولم أر أحداً منهم نبه على هذا، والحق ما ذكره القاضي لما في المعراج : ولو باع الآبق من ابنه الصغير لا يجوز ولو وهبه له أو ليتيم في حجره يجوز لأن ما بقي له من اليد في الآبق يصلح لقبض الهبة دون البيع .اهـ. وأما صاحب الذخيرة فذكر في البيوع أن الأب لو باع العبد المرسل في حاجته لابنه الصغير جاز ولم يذكره في الآبق . وذكر في كتاب الهبة: لو وهب عبدا فله آبقاً من ابنه الصغير فما دام متردداً في دار الإسلام تجوز الهبة ويصير قابضاً لابنه بنفس الهبة؛ ذكر هذه المسألة في الجامع، وفي المنتقى عن أبي يوسف : لو تصدق بعبد أبق له على ابنه الصغير لا يجوز، وروى المعلى عنه أنه يجوز فحصل عن أبي يوسف في المسألة روايتان .اهـ. وشمل كلامه أيضاً ما إذا باعه بعد ما أبق من يد الغاصب منه لما في الذخيرة وإذا أبق العبد المغصوب من يد الغاصب ثم إن المالك باع العبد من الغاصب وهو آبق بعد فالبيع جائز، والأصل أن الإباق إنما يمنع جواز البيع إذا كان التسليم محتاجاً إليه بأن أبق من يد المالك ثم باعه المالك، فأما إذا لم يكن التسليم محتاجاً إليه كما في مسألتنا يجوز البيع .اهـ. وقيد بالآبق لأن العبد المرسل في حاجة المولى يجوز بيعه، ولو باعه وليس بابق ثم أبق قبل القبض فإن المشتري بالخيار في فسخ ذلك العقد ولا يكون للبائع أن يطالب المشتري بالثمن ما لم يحضر العبد .اهـ. وجعل الراد على البائع كما في القنية وخرج المغصوب فقد ذكر محمد في الأصل أنه موقوف إن أقر به الغاصب تم البيع ولزم، بيع أيضاً . الأب أنه جائز يجوز ولم يفرق بينهما إذا كان بالنهار فراجعه قوله : (وأولوا تلك الرواية الخ) هذا أيضاً ينافي ما قدمه أول كتاب البيوع من التعاطي لا ينعقد بعد بيع باطل أو فاسد ما لم يفسخ العقد الأول. قوله: (وفي فتاوى قاضيخان من الهبة خلافه قال في النهر ووقع في الخانية في بعض النسخ
His statement: (and the runaway slave) means that the sale of a runaway slave is not permissible due to the Prophet's prohibition (peace be upon him). With him is evidence for preferring the opinion of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding bees, and Muhammad's opinion on silkworms and their eggs... (continues with same text as earlier, then:)... then he said: Because he is not able to deliver, and if he sells it and then the running away ends, the contract does not become valid because it was void due to lack of subject matter, like selling a bird in the air. According to Abu Hanifa, the contract is completed if not rescinded, because the contract was concluded with property existing, and the impediment (inability to deliver) has been removed, as if the slave ran away after the sale. This is also related from Muhammad. So in al-Hidayah, the first is the apparent transmission, and Abu Abdullah al-Balkhi used to give fatwa accordingly, as in al-Dhakhira. They interpreted that narration to mean that the sale is concluded by mutual handling (al-ta'ati) now. He made it absolute, so it includes when he sells it to his minor son: it is not permissible. Likewise, the orphan in his guardianship, unlike if he gives it as a gift to him, then it is permissible. The difference is that the condition of sale is ability to deliver immediately after the contract, which is absent, and the remaining hand (control) he has is sufficient for accepting a gift but not for accepting a sale, because sale is a receipt in exchange for money given from the son's property, while this is a receipt not in exchange for money leaving the child's property. So that hand suffices for the sake of the minor, because if it returns, it returns to the minor's ownership. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and al-Tabyin. In Fatawa Qadikhan, from the chapter of gift, there is a contrary: he said: 'If one gives his runaway slave as a gift to his minor son, it is not permissible, but if he sells it to him, it is permissible.' So he reversed the ruling, and I have not seen any of the commentators point this out. The truth is what the judge mentioned according to al-Mi'raj: 'If one sells the runaway slave to his minor son, it is not permissible, but if he gives it as a gift to him or to an orphan in his guardianship, it is permissible, because the remaining hand he has over the runaway suffices for accepting a gift but not for a sale.' As for the author of al-Dhakhira, he mentioned in the chapter of sales that if a father sells a slave sent on an errand to his minor son, it is permissible, but he did not mention it in the case of a runaway. And in the book of gifts: 'If one gives a slave that is a runaway as a gift to his minor son, as long as he is still within the land of Islam, the gift is valid and he becomes a receiver for his son by the gift itself.' This issue is mentioned in al-Jami'. In al-Muntqa from Abu Yusuf: 'If one gives in charity a runaway slave to his minor son, it is not permissible.' And al-Mu'alla related from him that it is permissible, so there are two narrations from Abu Yusuf on this issue. His statement also includes when he sells it after it ran away from the hand of the usurper, because in al-Dhakhira: 'If the usurped slave runs away from the hand of the usurper, then the owner sells the slave to the usurper while he is still a runaway, the sale is permissible. The principle is that running away only prevents the validity of sale when delivery is needed, i.e., when it ran away from the owner's hand and then the owner sells it. But if delivery is not needed, as in our case, the sale is permissible.' And it is restricted to the runaway because a slave sent on the master's errand can be sold. If he sells it while it is not a runaway and then it runs away before delivery, the buyer has the option to rescind the contract, and the seller cannot claim the price until the slave is produced. And the return is upon the seller, as in al-Qunyah. And the usurped slave is excluded, for Muhammad mentioned in al-Asl that it is suspended: if the usurper acknowledges it, the sale is completed and binding. Also, the father's sale [to his son] is permissible, and he did not distinguish between them if it is during the day, so check it. His statement: (And they interpreted that narration...) This also contradicts what was presented at the beginning of the Book of Sales that mutual handling does not conclude a contract after a void or invalid sale unless the first contract is rescinded. His statement: (And in Fatawa Qadikhan from the gift section, there is a contrary...) He said in al-Nahr: and it occurs in al-Khaniyya in some copies...
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This is an expansion probe from the commentary, focusing on the sale of a runaway slave ('abaq). The passage begins by stating the general ruling: sale of a runaway slave is not permissible due to the Prophet's prohibition. The commentary then dives into intra-Hanafi disputes. According to the apparent (zahir al-riwayah) position, the sale is void ab initio because the subject matter is not deliverable, analogous to a bird in the air. However, a narration from Abu Hanifa holds that the contract becomes valid if the slave returns before rescission, because the impediment was removed; this is similar to a slave running away after sale. This narration is interpreted to refer to a sale by mutual handling (ta'ati) at the time of return. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is the first one (void sale), as followed by Abu Abdullah al-Balkhi. The discussion extends to selling a runaway to one's own minor son: the sale is invalid because the father cannot deliver immediately, but a gift to the minor son is valid because the father's constructive possession suffices for gift. There is a contrary view in Fatawa Qadikhan reversing this. The truth is that the sale is invalid but the gift is valid. The passage also covers the case of a runaway slave from a usurper (ghasib): if the usurper acknowledges the running, the sale is valid because delivery is not needed from the owner. The general principle is that running away only prevents sale when delivery is required from the seller. Finally, the commentator addresses the objection that mutual handling after a void sale does not create a new contract without rescission, which seems contradictory to the interpretation of Abu Hanifa's view. The passage ends abruptly with a note on the Khaniyya.

Key Terms
الآبق (al-abaaq): A runaway slave; one who has fled from his master.
الهبة (al-hiba): Gift; a transfer of ownership without exchange of compensation.
اليد (al-yad): Hand/control; legal possession or authority over an object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position, which of the following is valid regarding a father's transfer of a runaway slave to his minor son?
A) Both sale and gift are valid.
B) Sale is valid; gift is invalid.
C) Sale is invalid; gift is valid.
D) Both sale and gift are invalid.

Key Takeaway
For transferring a runaway slave to a minor son, a gift is valid under the majority Hanafi view, while a sale is void; the contrary view in Fatawa Qadikhan is not the relied-upon position.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
7 min
The Sale of a Runaway Slave: Default Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains the default ruling on selling a runaway slave and the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding subsequent return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi ruling on selling a runaway slave, explain the qiyas with a bird in the air, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Zahir al-Riwayah.

Core Idea
A runaway slave cannot be sold because delivery is impossible, but Abu Hanifa validates it if the slave returns before rescission.

This lesson examines the default ruling on selling a runaway slave (ābiq). The Hanafi school generally holds that such a sale is void (bāṭil) due to the seller’s inability to deliver the subject matter at the time of contract. However, there is a known intra-madhhab dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa held that the contract becomes valid if the slave returns before the buyer rescinds, while the relied-upon position (Zāhir al-Riwāyah) maintains that the sale remains void. We will explore the reasoning behind each view and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is selling a runaway slave. The default ruling is that the sale is bāṭil (void) because the seller cannot deliver the slave at the time of contract.
2
Understand the qiyas
The ruling is analogized to selling a bird in the air, which is also bāṭil due to inability to deliver. Both lack qudra ʿalā al-taslīm.
The illa is inability to deliver.
3
Note Abu Hanifa’s exception
Abu Hanifa holds that if the slave returns to the seller before the buyer rescinds, the contract becomes valid because the obstacle is removed. He compares it to a slave that runs away after the sale (which is valid).
The contract was validly concluded, and the subsequent return removes the defect.
4
Contrast with Zahir al-Riwayah
The relied-upon position is that the contract was bāṭil from the start (void ab initio), so later return does not validate it.
This is the position of Abu Abdullah al-Balkhi and the majority of later Hanafis.
5
Determine the muftā bihi
The muftā bihi is the Zahir al-Riwayah position: the sale remains void. This is the basis for fatwa.

المعراج، وأما إعتاقه فجائز لكن إن أعتقه عن كفارة عليه فإنه لا يجوز حتى تعلم حياته كما في المعراج، ويصح جعله بدل خلع كما قدمناه في بابه عند قوله ولو اختلعت على عبد أبق لها على أنها بريئة من ضمانه لم التسليم وهو المانع. ولم يذكر المصنف أنه يكتفي بقبضه عن قبض المبيع للتفصيل قالوا : إن كان أشهد وقت أخذه أنه أخذه ليرده على مالكه كان أمانه في يده فلا ينوب عن قبض البيع، فلو هلك قبل أن يصل إلى سيده لم
